PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 1 Getting Started with PowerShell Introduction This chapter introduces readers to PowerShell, a broad scripting language designed for automating administrative tasks and managing IT environments. The chapter focuses on the purpose, features, and benefits of PowerShell compared to traditional command line tools. Setting up an efficient environment is key to effective scripting, so this chapter also guides readers through installing and configuring PowerShell on different platforms, setting up Visual Studio Code ( VS Code ) for scripting, and integrating GitHub for seamless collaboration. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Introduction to PowerShell and its purpose Installation on Windows, Linux, and macOS Setup VS Code for PowerShell scripting Essential PowerShell commands PowerShell ISE for scripting GitHub setup and for script management Best practices for organizing scripts VS Code GitHub and version control extensions Objectives By the end of the chapter, the readers will have a foundation of what PowerShell is, along with how and why its usage is so common and attractive for many developers and administrators. They will also learn how to create a perfect environment for their PowerShell scripting experience, where they will use VS Code for scripting and GitHub for code management. Introduction to PowerShell and its purpose PowerShell is a cross-platform, object-oriented scripting language for automating administrative tasks, helping IT professionals do their work faster and in a cleaner manner. Unlike other command line interfaces, PowerShell is built on top of the .NET runtime, which allows it to work with the rich .NET objects pool. The object-oriented design enables more robust scripting and deep automation capabilities, especially for system administration and integration tasks. Starting from PowerShell 6, it is built on top of .NET Core, which enables cross-platform support for Linux, macOS, and Windows, unlike the original version 5.1 and below, which was Windows only. This makes PowerShell very powerful in modern IT environments, where hybrid infrastructures and automations are common and no longer imaginable without it. PowerShell is made of cmdlets (spelled command-lets ). These commands are created for specific administrative tasks, such as managing services, files, or cloud resources like Entra ID. The following figure shows what a PowerShell command looks like: Figure 1.1 : PowerShell cmdlet In the preceding figure, you can observe the already executed Get-Service command. After executing the command on a Windows 11 machine, the services located in the terminal appear on the machine. Like the preceding command, there are many others, and each command is designed with a naming convention (for example, Get-Process , Start-Service , Remove-Item ) for consistency and easier discovery of the commands. PowerShell was first launched on November 14, 2006. 19 years after its launch, PowerShell has evolved increasingly and is no longer limited to Windows. In 2018, after the launch of PowerShell 6.0, it was converted to an open-source, cross-platform tool, opening the doors for adoption in cloud automation and infrastructure management. Today, PowerShell provides an automation framework that allows users to interact with services like Azure, Microsoft 365, Microsoft Graph API, and on-premises Microsoft services such as Active Directory, as well as various APIs and numerous enterprise tools. PowerShell is a future-driven automation framework with capabilities that are expanding with each passing day. This makes it essential to understand what PowerShell is and its potential in IT environments. Installation on Windows, Linux, and macOS This section will explore how to setup PowerShell for Windows, Linux, and macOS step-by-step. You will see how easy it is to start using PowerShell to begin your scripting journey. Table 1.1 shows which PowerShell versions are built-in and in which versions you need to take care of the installation. Note: Be aware that Microsoft does not support versions lower than 5.1. We will work with Windows 11 and the newest PowerShell Version 7.5. The following table displays legacy PowerShell versions that are compatible with Windows only: PowerShell version Built-in Windows version PowerShell 1.0 Windows XP SP2, Windows Server 2003 SP1 (optional install) PowerShell 2.0 Windows 7, Windows Server 2008 R2 PowerShell 3.0 Windows 8, Windows Server 2012 PowerShell 4.0 Windows 8.1, Windows Server 2012 R2 PowerShell 5.0 Windows 10 (original release) PowerShell 5.1 Windows 10 (Anniversary Update and later), Windows 11, Windows Server 2016, Windows Server 2019, Windows Server 2022, Windows Server 2025 PowerShell 7. * Not built-in (manual installation required) for all Windows versions Table 1.1 : Windows PowerShell legacy versions The following table displays PowerShell versions that are cross-platform compatible for use on Windows, Linux, and macOS: PowerShell version Built-in Windows version PowerShell 6.0 Not built-in (requires manual installation) PowerShell 7.0 (LTS) Not built-in (requires manual installation) PowerShell 7.1 Not built-in (requires manual installation) PowerShell 7.2 (LTS) Available for Windows, Linux, macOS (manual installation) PowerShell 7.3 Available for Windows, Linux, macOS (manual installation) PowerShell 7.4 (LTS) Available for Windows, Linux, macOS (manual installation) PowerShell 7.5 Available for Windows, Linux, macOS (manual installation) Table 1.2 : PowerShell (Core) cross-platform versions PowerShell on Windows Using PowerShell on Windows is straightforward because it is built-in. We do not need to take care of installation until version 5.1 in modern systems. You can search for it in Windows Search. To find the PowerShell app, type Windows PowerShell in Windows Search, shown as follows: Figure 1.2 : Using Windows Search to find PowerShell PowerShell 5.1 differs from PowerShell 7.5 not only in platform support, but also in how certain logical operations behave, including differences in pipeline handling, error behavior, and script parallelization. Additionally, some cmdlets and features are exclusive to either version, making them functionally distinct in real-world scripting and automation tasks. All versions higher than PowerShell 5.1 are cross-platform versions and not built-in; hence, these versions require an installation. PowerShell 7.5 can be installed in different ways. In this chapter, we will look at graphical user interface ( GUI ) installation and command line installation, which are the most common ways to install PowerShell. First, we will install PowerShell on the GUI using Windows 11. Install PowerShell on Windows Execute the following steps to install PowerShell on Windows through the GUI: Download the latest Microsoft Software Installer ( MSI ) Package from GitHub. Browse the PowerShell GitHub page through the following link: https://github.com/powershell/powershell/releases Under the v7.5.0 Release of PowerShell , scroll down and open Assets , shown as follows: Figure 1.3 : PowerShell 7.5 Assets Click on PowerShell-7.5.0-win-x64.msi . The download will start automatically, as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.4 : PowerShell 7.5 download Open the location where the file is located and run the installer. The Setup Wizard for PowerShell 7 will appear, shown as follows: Figure 1.5 : PowerShell 7.5 installer Continue to click Next until you get to the last window, where Install is displayed. Click on Install . Now, PowerShell 7.5 will be installed on your computer. After successful installation, click Finish , as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.6 : Finishing PowerShell 7 installation Open the Windows Search and type PowerShell 7 . Click on the application PowerShell 7 , as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.7 : PowerShell 7.5 app A black terminal will open. Type in $PSVersiontable to verify the version of PowerShell, as shown in Figure 1.8 : Figure 1.8 : PowerShell 7.5 PSVersiontable You can now use PowerShell 7 on your PC. Install PowerShell on Windows with command line To install PowerShell on Windows through WinGet, complete the following steps: Open the Command Prompt as an administrator, as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.9 : PowerShell 7.5 CMD The Command Prompt will appear. Type winget search Microsoft.PowerShell , as shown in Figure 1.10 , and press Enter : Figure 1.10 : PowerShell 7.5 winget Press Y to agree to all the source agreements when you see the following on your screen: Figure 1.11 : PowerShell 7.5 winget and press Y Type winget install --id Microsoft.PowerShell --source winget to install PowerShell 7 version 7.5. PowerShell will download and install, as seen in the following figure: Figure 1.12 : PowerShell 7.5 winget installed Open PowerShell from the Windows Search as in Figure 1.6 . Using the command line to install PowerShell is significantly faster and more efficient. While GUI remains a valid option with identical results, the command line becomes more practical for larger deployments. For example, when installing PowerShell on multiple clients, such as 20 or more clients, the command line allows for time saving. Later in this book, methods for unattended software installations using PowerShell will be explored, enabling installations on numerous clients with a single command. If you need more information about installing PowerShell, you can refer to the Microsoft documentation on the following link: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/scripting/install/installing-powershell-on-windows?view=powershell-7.5 Install PowerShell on Ubuntu via package repository The following steps will allow you to install PowerShell on Linux (Ubuntu): 1. Open Terminal by clicking the icon shown in the following figure: Figure 1.13 : PowerShell 7.5 Ubuntu 2. Copy the following script for installation on your client: 1. ################################### 2. # Prerequisites 3. 4. # Update the list of packages 5. sudo apt-get update 6. 7. # Install pre-requisite packages. 8. sudo apt-get install -y wget apt-transport-https software-properties-common 9. 10. # Get the version of Ubuntu 11. source / etc/os-release 12. 13. # Download the Microsoft repository keys 14. wget -q https://packages.microsoft.com/config/ubuntu/ $VERSION_ID /packages-microsoft-prod.deb 15. 16. # Register the Microsoft repository keys 17. sudo dpkg - i packages- microsoft-prod.deb 18. 19. # Delete the Microsoft repository keys file 20. rm packages- microsoft-prod.deb 21. 22. # Update the list of packages after we added packages.microsoft.com 23. sudo apt-get update 24. 25. ################################### 26. # Install PowerShell 27. sudo apt-get install -y powershell 28. 29. # Start PowerShell 30. pwsh 3. Follow the script steps and make sure to execute all the prerequisites in the Terminal . All codes starting with a hashtag ( # ) are comments and do not need to be executed. 4. Line 27 is installing PowerShell. 5. After installation, type pswh to start PowerShell in the Terminal . 6. Type Get-Command in the Terminal to explore the commands you can use. You can see a few commands in the following figure: Figure 1.14 : PowerShell 7.5 Ubuntu Get-Command Note: It is easy to setup PowerShell on Ubuntu, and you are not limited to Windows. So, if you have requirements to automate tasks on Ubuntu, you are now able to do that with PowerShell! For detailed information, you can check the following link to the Microsoft documentation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/scripting/install/install-ubuntu?view=powershell-7.5 Install PowerShell on macOS To install PowerShell on macOS, complete the following steps: Open the following website on your browser: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/scripting/install/installing-powershell-on-macos?view=powershell-7.5 Scroll down until you reach the section Installation via Direct Download . Under PowerShell 7.5 , click powershell-7.5.0-osx-x64.pkg , as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.15 : PowerShell 7.5 Installation via Direct Download After that, select Open on the upper-right side. The installer will appear as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.16 : PowerShell 7.5 macOS installation Click Continue , then click Install . Now, put in your password for the installation and click Install Software , shown as follows: Figure 1.17 : Type password to install PowerShell 7.5 on macOS After the installation is successful, click Close . Open the launchpad on your Mac and search for PowerShell . You will see the following icon: Figure 1.18 : PowerShell 7.5 icon The PowerShell terminal will open. Type Get-Command to list all the commands you can use. The command will return a list of commands, as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.19 : Get-Command output If you need additional information for installation or version compatibility, refer to the Microsoft documentation on the following link: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/scripting/install/installing-powershell-on-macos?view=powershell-7.5 Setup VS Code for PowerShell scripting Normally, you do not need anything to write PowerShell scripts. You can open your editor to write a script, save the file as myscript.ps1 , and execute it. However, this method makes it hard to write and troubleshoot scripts. There are many tools that are free to use, which can help you to write scripts efficiently. One of these tools is VS Code, which offers several advantages for PowerShell scripting. It provides syntax highlighting for improved code readability, Git integration for version control, built-in debugging capabilities, and a dedicated PowerShell extension that enhances the scripting experience. For the following section , we will use Windows 11 to setup VS Code, but you can also do it on a macOS or Linux machine. Installing Visual Studio Code To install VS Code, refer to the following steps: Download VS Code from the link https://go.microsoft.com/fwlink/?LinkID=534107 This link will directly start the download for the user setup. If you need another version, refer to the following website: https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/setup/windows#_install-vs-code-on-windows After the download completes, execute the VS Code setup file. Click through the installer and install it. Now open VS Code using the Search function and type in VS Code . Alternatively, you can launch VS Code directly from the installer. The Get Started with VS Code window will appear, as seen in the following screen: Figure 1.20 : VS Code Close the walkthrough VS Code window by clicking x on the upper left side. VS Code has been successfully installed. Now, we will start to configure it using PowerShell. Visual Studio Code configuration for PowerShell After VS Code is installed, some configuration is needed to start scripting with PowerShell. For more information about VS Code, refer to the following website: https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/getstarted/getting-started The following figure displays the VS Code environment with the names of its key interface elements, which will be used throughout the book for easier navigation within VS Code: Figure 1.21 : VS Code Complete the following steps to get started: VS Code is already open. Click on the left sidebar on the explorer icon and then on open folder, as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.22 : File Explorer icon in VS Code Create a folder named PowerShell Automation in your preferred location. Select the newly created folder and then select Yes, I trust the authors . This adds a working location. In this folder, all the files and scripts will be located. Now, the PowerShell extension must be installed. To do so, follow the numbers from 1 to 3, as seen in the following figure: Figure 1.23 : Installing PowerShell extension After installation, the Terminal will appear automatically. If it does not open, go to the menu bar at the top, click Terminal , and then New Terminal . The Terminal starts automatically with PowerShell Version 7.5. Close the extension window after installation. Create a file named HelloWord.ps1 . The following figure shows the File Explorer section in VS Code. Click on the rectangle to reveal the Add File button, which is also marked in red: Figure 1.24 : VS Code After the creation of the file, it will be opened automatically in the editor. You are now ready to start scripting in VS Code. Essential PowerShell commands PowerShell provides helpful commands to find and understand other commands. This section covers three important ones, which include Get-Command to list available commands, Get-Help to learn how commands work, and Get-Alias to find the full names of shortcut commands. These tools make working with PowerShell easier and faster. Also, PowerShell uses a simple and consistent naming system called the Verb-Noun design. In this system, the verb describes the action to take, and the noun describes the object to which the action is applied. For example, Get-Item is used to read or fetch something, New-Item is used to create something, and Remove-Item is used to delete something. This clear structure makes it easy to understand what a command will do just by looking at its name. Get-Command To get a list of all available commands in the terminal, type Get-Command in the script editor and press F8 or execute selection on the top right corner. Type Get-Command -TotalCount 10 to get a list of only the first 10 commands in the terminal. You will get a PowerShell object with information about the 10 commands available on the system, as shown in Figure 1.25 : Figure 1.25 : Get-Command output Get-Help Type Get-Help -Name Copy-Item and execute the following line shown in the figure: Figure 1.26 : VS Code With this command, a list of all the needed information about a command is shown on the screen, such as the syntax, all usable parameters, and aliases. To get help with a command, it is not necessary to exit PowerShell. Instead, a user can get out-of-the-box help by using the Get-Help command. Get-Alias In PowerShell, sometimes a user comes across a command they do not understand. The Get-Alias command is used to understand the command that is hidden after an alias. For example, the alias of the Copy-Item command is cp . The output of Get-Alias cp is shown in the following figure: Figure 1.27 : Get-Alias output The result is the information for the command that is hidden under the alias cp . The Get-Alias command is quite helpful, especially when analyzing a script written by someone else. PowerShell ISE for scripting In addition to the VS Code for scripting, there is another scripting toolkit that comes with Microsoft Windows, which is the Windows PowerShell ISE . This script editor is not as powerful, but it does a great job. To open it, search for PowerShell ISE in Windows Search and click on it. Once the ISE opens, you can see the following big blue terminal: Figure 1.28 : PowerShell ISE Click on File and then on Open . Next, select the file created in VS Code. Once it opens, you can edit or execute it. You can also try to execute the three commands discussed in the preceding section. Note: PowerShell ISE is not supported in PowerShell 6 and beyond; it can only run PowerShell 5.1. GitHub setup for script management Never underestimate the importance of a script. Even small scripts require proper change tracking and version control to ensure they are saved correctly and managed efficiently. Every project should start with a structured approach, and a code repository is the foundation of that structure. It does not matter if you use GitHub, Azure DevOps, or another version control system; the key is to maintain organization and control over your code. Using a code repository offers several benefits. It is cost-free and allows team members to collaborate on the same script seamlessly. The repository serves as a backup, protecting code from accidental deletion. Additionally, it provides a clear history of who made changes and what modifications were made at specific times, ensuring better code tracking and accountability. The following can happen without a code repository: Codes can be overwritten by other persons in an organization. If something is changed in a script, it is not easy to go back to the previous version. Working with multiple people in the same script becomes very difficult. There are a lot of local backup scripts in the folder, which may lead to confusion. If the preceding points seem familiar, the next section will help you use a code repository for your scripts. It will improve collaboration, protect your code, and make version tracking much easier. Create a GitHub account Execute the following steps to create a GitHub account; you can skip these steps if you already have one: Open the browser and go to the following site: https://github.com / Click on Sign up on the top right. Enter your email, password, and username, then click Continue . Verify your account. Confirm your email address by entering the code. Log in to your newly created GitHub account. You are now ready to create your first code repository. Creating a repository A repository is used to store code and other project files. Everything in the repository is tracked with version control, which means that a user can see changes, go back to previous versions, and collaborate with others without losing any work. Complete the following steps to create a repository: On the GitHub page, click on Create repository , as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.29 : Create a repository on GitHub Fill in the repository name. For example, you can name the repository PowerShell-Automation . Give it a description. Choose if the repository should be accessible publicly or privately: Private : The owner of the repository can choose who can interact with this repository. Public : Anyone on the internet can access this repository. Select Private . Click on Create repository . Best practices for organizing scripts After a repository has been created, you can use it for various purposes. While creating a repository, keep the following pointers in mind: Use clear names that best describe the script. Use lowercase names with underscores or hyphens for better readability. Keep a consistent naming convention throughout the whole project. Use folders for grouping scripts that have a similar logic. An example of grouping scripts in folders can be seen in Figure 1.30 : Figure 1.30 : Grouping scripts in GitHub Create a README.md which contains the following information: What the script does. How to run the script. The dependencies of the script. Example usage of the script. When committing changes, write clear messages. Do not use the updated script as the name. Instead, use Added Variable $count at line 34 for better readability. Never put secrets or passwords in the script. Always keep the repository clean and archive old, unused scripts. After learning the essentials for repositories, one can connect GitHub to VS Code. Note: A user can also write scripts and upload them manually to GitHub and modify them there, but this is not very user-friendly and not recommended. With VS Code, it is easier to manage a repository. VS Code GitHub and version control extensions Complete the following steps to configure VS Code for GitHub: Install Git for source control : Open VS Code. Navigate to the Terminal . Use a PowerShell terminal (check out the terminal profile). Copy the following code into the Terminal : winget install --id Git.Git -e --source winget Execute the preceding line of code. This will install Git on the computer. After a successful installation process, the following output will appear in the Terminal : Figure 1.31 : Git The installation is as follows: Restart the VS Code application. Click on the SOURCE CONTROL icon. Click on Initialize Repository . Prepare the following command with the data: Git remote add origin https://github.com/your-githubusername/your-githubrepository-name.git git config --global user.email you@example.com git config --global user.name "Your Name" Execute the preceding three lines of code in the Terminal . Execute git remote -v in the Terminal . The following two lines of output will appear: origin https://github.com/uzejnovic-ahmed/PowerShell-Automation.git (fetch) origin https://github.com/uzejnovic-ahmed/PowerShell-Automation.git (push) Type git add in the Terminal to stage all the changes. After modifying the code, select the scripts to commit the changes. If only specific scripts are to be committed, git add update.ps1 can be used, and only then will the script be ready for the commit. GUI can also be used by clicking the + icon highlighted in Figure 1.32 : Figure 1.32 : Stage changes Type git branch -M main in the Terminal , which renames the current branch to main branch. Type and execute git push -u origin main in the Terminal . A GitHub Authorization window pops up. Click Sign in with your browser. If you are already logged in to Authorize Git Credential Manager , click on the green button Authorize git-ecosystem , as in the following figure: Figure 1.33 : Authorize Git Credential Manager This will successfully connect the source control (Git) with the GitHub repository, as shown in the following figure: Figure 1.34 : Connected Git Check the repository, the HelloWord.ps1 file should be there. It will look like the following figure: Figure 1.35 : HelloWord.ps1 file You can check how change tracking works by completing the following steps: Open VS Code and open the HelloWord.ps1 script. Add a new line and type a PowerShell command. Save the changes. Click on the SOURCE CONTROL icon. The script is now listed under Changes . Click on it. The old script will be on the left side, and the changes made will be on the right side. Now, you can control the changes, and if it looks okay, stage and commit the changes. Click on Sync . Conclusion In this chapter, you explored the fundamentals of PowerShell and learned how to setup an effective scripting environment. You discovered PowerShell’s purpose, key features, and how to execute basic commands to get started. Additionally, you configured VS Code and GitHub for script management, version control, and seamless collaboration. This foundation provides you with the knowledge to start with advanced scripting. The next chapter will focus on improving scripting by customizing VS Code. You will learn how to use tools like source control, SQL databases, and AI helpers like GitHub Copilot to work more efficiently. By the end of the next chapter, your VS Code setup will be ready to handle all your scripting tasks.

PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 2 Enhancing Scripting with Visual Studio Code Introduction This chapter explores how to integrate SQL databases and artificial intelligence ( AI ) tools like GitHub Copilot into your workflow. You will learn how to customize the Visual Studio Code ( VS Code ) environment with extensions that enhance productivity, such as PowerShell support, GitHub integration, and database management. At the end of this chapter, you will have a personalized setup for your scripting needs. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Managing workspaces in VS Code Integrate SQL database in VS Code Integrate Copilot into VS Code Customizing the environment for productivity Objectives By the end of this chapter, the readers will have the skills to customize the VS Code environment with essential extensions, including those for Todo, Copilot, and database management. This approach will help the readers to work faster and solve scripting challenges more easily, ensuring that your development process runs smoothly and fits exactly into their workflow. Managing workspaces in VS Code When working with PowerShell, it is important to keep files organized in a clear and logical folder structure. A well-structured repository makes it easier to find scripts, follow consistent practices, and align with industry standards. This habit is valuable not only in PowerShell projects but also in many other areas of software development. Once a good structure is in place, VS Code workspaces can be used as an additional layer to bring everything together in one view. A workspace does not replace proper organization, but it can simplify daily work by giving quick access to all related folders and files within a single environment, especially when managing larger projects or collaborating with a team. Folder structure best practices A good folder structure is crucial in projects because it keeps everything organized, predictable, and easy to maintain. It ensures that scripts, tests, and documentation are always stored in the right place, which reduces confusion and saves time when working alone or as part of a team. Clear organization also supports collaboration, helps new contributors quickly understand the project, and follows common industry standards, which is especially important for long-term projects. Here is an example of folder structures in a PowerShell project: MyPowerShellProject/ │ ├── src/ # Core scripts and modules │ ├── Public/ # Exported functions │ └── Private/ # Internal helper functions │ ├── tests/ # Pester tests │ ├── Unit/ # Unit tests │ └── Integration/ # Integration tests │ ├── docs/ # Documentation and guides │ ├── examples/ # Usage examples │ ├── logs/ # Log files │ ├── data/ # Static data files (e.g., JSON, CSV) │ ├── ModuleName.psd1 # Module manifest ├── ModuleName.psm1 # Module implementation ├── README.md # Project overview └── LICENSE # Licensing information The sample folder structure is designed to keep every part of a PowerShell project organized and easy to maintain. The src folder contains the core code of the project. Inside it, the Public folder holds functions that are shared and available to the user, while the Private folder contains helper functions used internally. The tests folder is where all Pester tests are stored. Unit tests go into the Unit subfolder, and integration tests go into the Integration subfolder. The docs folder is for documentation and guides, while the examples folder provides usage examples that show how to use the module in practice. The logs folder is used to store log files, and the data folder keeps static files such as JSON or CSV that are required by the scripts. Outside the folders are the main module files, which include the ModuleName.psd1 file, which is the module manifest, and defines important information about the module. The ModuleName.psm1 file is the main implementation of the module. Finally, the README.md file gives an overview of the project, and the LICENSE file defines the legal usage rights. The next approach organizes automations by platform, then by service, and finally by each specific script. Every automation folder contains the script in verb-noun.ps1 format, a logs folder for runtime logs, a configs folder for settings like JSON files, and a README.md for documentation. At the root, a .gitignore ensures logs and other unnecessary files are not uploaded. Refer to the following example: Platform ├── Service │ └── AutomationName │ ├── verb-automation.ps1 # │ ├── logs # Log files generated by the script │ ├── configs # Config files (e.g., JSON, XML) │ ├── README.md # Documentation and usage guides └── .gitignore # Ensure Git does not upload log files etc. For example, under the Google platform, the Calendar service has the CollectUserCalendarEvents automation with its script, configs, logs, and guide. The same structure is used for CleanEmptyMeetingRooms and for Gmail tasks like ReportJiraMailboxUsage , as shown: Google ├── Calendar │ └── CollectUserCalendarEvents │ ├── Collect-UserCalendarEvents.ps1 │ ├── logs │ ├── configs │ │ └── UserCalendarEvents.JSON │ └── README.md ├── CleanEmptyMeetingRooms │ ├── Clean-EmptyMeetingRooms.ps1 │ ├── logs │ ├── configs │ │ └── EmptyMeetingRooms.JSON │ └── README.md ├── Gmail │ └── ReportJiraMailboxUsage │ ├── Report-JiraMailboxUsage.ps1 │ ├── logs │ ├── configs │ │ ├── JiraMailboxUsage-mboxList.JSON │ │ └── JiraMailboxUsage-guardRail.JSON │ └── README.md └── .gitignore # Ensure Git does not upload log files etc. This layout keeps each automation self-contained and easy to maintain. Creating a workspace Creating a workspace is very simple. First, you need to open a new instance of VS Code. Then, add the folders to the workspace and save the workspace file to a preferred location. Start by opening a new instance of VS Code as follows: Open VS Code. Click on File on the upper-left side and select New Window . This will open an empty VS Code window. Please close all other open windows and then complete the following steps: Click on File and Select Save Workspace As . The Windows Explorer will appear. Navigate to C:\PowerShell Automation , the Folder created in Chapter 1 , Getting Started with PowerShell . Save the workspace with the name PowerShell Automation workspace . Click Save . This will create an empty workspace. The workspace is nothing other than a JSON file for configuration that contains all the folders and settings added to the workspace. You can open the workspace file in Notepad to see what it contains, as shown in the following figure: Figure 2.1 : VS Code workspace The following steps will allow you to add folders to the workspace: Go back to the VS Code window and select File . Click on Add Folder to Workspace . Browse to C:\PowerShell Automation and click Add . In the explorer view of VS Code, the Hello World Script and the workspace file have appeared. Now, we will create a second folder in a different location with two files using PowerShell as follows: Create a new file in the workspace named create-demo-files.ps1 . Copy the following code in the file, and save it. You can also find this script in the repository: # Create the directory New -Item -Path "C:\PowerShell Workspace Demo" -ItemType Directory # Create the first file with text content New -Item -Path "C:\PowerShell Workspace Demo\File1.txt" -ItemType File - Value «This is the content of File1.» # Create the second file with text content New -Item -Path "C:\PowerShell Workspace Demo\File2.txt" -ItemType File - Value "This is the content of File2." Click on execute script on the top right of the VS Code window. It is highlighted under Number 1 in Figure 2.2 : Figure 2.2 : Script execution on VS Code window The new folder and two new files with text content are created. Now, these files can be added to the workspace. Click on File and then on Add Folder to Workspace . Select the newly created folder C:\PowerShell Workspace Demo . Click Add . Explore the VS Code explorer view. The new folder with the two files will have appeared. Note: Be aware that only the files in your PowerShell Automation folder are tracked by source control. You can now search in the workspace by clicking on the search icon in VS Code on the left side under File Explorer . In the Search field, type in a keyword you want to search for. This will show all the files and folders containing this word on the screen. If you click on one file, it will be opened in the script editor, as shown in Figure 2.3 : Figure 2.3 : VS Code workspace search function This section focused on the benefits of using VS Code workspaces. Workspaces allow folders and files from different locations to be combined into one unified view, simplifying navigation and file management. This setup not only keeps projects organized but also ensures that everyone on a team works with the same folder structure, which cuts down on setup time and errors. In practice, workspaces streamline workflows by making it faster to search through code and maintain an efficient development environment. Integrate SQL database in VS Code This section will focus on how to integrate SQL databases into VS Code, making it easier to manage the data that needs to be stored. Often, automation processes require databases to save data and retrieve it based on specific dates or events. The most efficient approach is to centralize the database connections, coding environment, Git integrations, and scripts in one place. This unified setup eliminates the need to switch between different programs to check table entries or work on your code, resulting in a smoother and more productive workflow. The first step is to install SQL Server. If you already have an SQL Server, then you can use it. If not, refer to the following steps outlined to install Microsoft SQL Server 2022 Express: Open the following link: https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/download/details.aspx?id=104781 Download the SQL Server 2022 Express as shown in the following figure: Figure 2.4 : Downloading Microsoft SQL Server 2022 Express Run the downloaded installer. After installation completes, save the connection string and the instance name. The connection string will be needed later to connect to the database. It looks like the following: Server=localhost\SQLEXPRESS;Database=master;Trusted_Connection=True; The instance name is SQLEXPRESS . After installing the SQL Express Server, you can now integrate it into VS Code by installing the SQL Server extension by completing the following steps: Open VS Code and follow the numbered steps in the following figure: Figure 2.6 : VS Code SQL extension After the installation, two new icons will appear on the left menu bar. Database Projects and SQL Server. PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 3 Understanding Cmdlets and Modules Introduction Cmdlets and modules are at the heart of PowerShell’s functionality. This chapter introduces the syntax, usage, and parameters of cmdlets and demonstrates how to install, import, and manage modules from the PowerShell Gallery. The chapter also explores some of the most used modules and understand how they extend PowerShell’s capabilities. This chapter ensures you can leverage cmdlets and modules effectively in future scripts. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: • Introduction to cmdlets • PowerShell modules • PowerShell Gallery • Commonly used PowerShell modules Objectives By the end of this chapter, the readers will understand how to use PowerShell cmdlets and modules. They will learn what cmdlets are, how to use them, and how to find the right cmdlets for their tasks. This chapter will give them insights into the working of modules, including how to install and manage them, and how to use the PowerShell Gallery to find new modules. This will help them to expand PowerShell’s abilities and make their scripts more powerful. Introduction to cmdlets Cmdlets (pronounced command-lets) are the heart of PowerShell. They are like mini tools built-into the system, designed to help you get things done quickly, whether checking running processes, managing files, or configuring settings. Unlike other command line commands, cmdlets have a consistent, easy to understand format, making them simpler to use. Cmdlets are the basic commands in PowerShell. They are small, purpose-built functions designed to handle specific tasks, like fetching system information, managing files, or configuring a client. Cmdlets can be grouped into PowerShell modules, which can be loaded into a session when needed. Unlike traditional executables, cmdlets run within the PowerShell environment. They can be written in any .NET language or directly in PowerShell’s scripting language. Cmdlets can be executed on their own or combined with other cmdlets, making them incredibly powerful. Users can automate complex tasks efficiently, saving time and effort, by chaining cmdlets together. Basic syntax The PowerShell syntax is easy to read and very predictable. This is because cmdlets are based on a Verb-Noun structure. The Verb defines the action the cmdlet will perform. For example, Get , Set , New , and Remove . The Noun determines the object the cmdlet is working on. For example, Process , Service , and Item . The following table gives some examples of the Verb-Noun structure of cmdlets: Verb Noun Example cmdlet Get Process Get-Process New Item New-Item Start Job Start-Job Test Path Test-Path Table 3.1 : Verb-Noun cmdlet examples PowerShell parameters A cmdlet can be executed alone, and it will work. However, it is also possible to run a cmdlet with parameters to modify the behavior of cmdlets. Parameters are added after the cmdlet. The following table shows a simple example: Cmdlet Result Get-Process Lists all running processes. Get-Process -name notepad Lists only the process named notepad. Table 3.2 : Parameter cmdlet examples The name parameters ask for the process name. With this parameter, the cmdlet can filter the processes down to only output the notepad process. There is also another type of parameter, called switch parameters . This parameter changes the behavior of the cmdlet with the parameter itself, and no other value is required. The difference can be seen clearly in Figure 3.1 . The first command lists only the process information. The second command lists the file version information because the switch FileVersionInfo has been used: Figure 3.1 : PowerShell parameters PowerShell pipeline As mentioned in the introduction, it is possible to chain cmdlets. The pipeline ( | ) allows users to connect two cmdlets, passing the output of the first cmdlet directly into the second. This allows users to write scripts more efficiently and makes it easier to read. There is no need to store the results of a cmdlet in a variable or write extra logic, as the pipeline handles it automatically. As shown in Figure 3.2 , the pipeline is used to pass the output of the Get-Process cmdlet to Out-GridView . The Out-GridView cmdlet opens a new window displaying the output from Get-Process in an interactive, sortable table: Figure 3.2 : PowerShell pipeline to pass the output There are no limitations to using the pipeline in a single command, allowing for creative and powerful data processing. The following figure shows an example of a longer pipeline command that retrieves running processes, filters them based on CPU usage, sorts them, and then gives the output in the terminal: Figure 3.3 : PowerShell pipeline Now that you have a good idea of how PowerShell cmdlets work, let us see how to find and use them. PowerShell has built-in ways to discover available cmdlets, check what they do, and explore their parameters, so you do not have to memorize everything. Discovering and using cmdlets When using PowerShell, users have access to many cmdlets right out-of-the-box. Additionally, PowerShell’s capabilities can be extended by adding more cmdlets through modules. Finding the right cmdlet is essential when trying to solve a problem. Thus, PowerShell comes with built-in tools to help you discover and use cmdlets efficiently. Whether searching for a command, checking its parameters, or looking for examples, PowerShell makes it easy to find exactly what you need. Available cmdlets In every PowerShell session, the command Get-Command can be used to list all the available commands. This is a good approach to list all commands, but it is not easy to find something because it returns a list of many commands. Filter statements can narrow down the results and make searches more precise. Instead of listing all available cmdlets, filters can be used to find the exact command needed. This helps reduce clutter and makes it easier to locate the right command. In Figure 3.4 , the -Name parameter is used with the value *Process* , where * acts as a wildcard character. The wildcard character * is used to match any sequence of characters. This allows users to search for cmdlets dynamically without needing to type the full name: Figure 3.4 : PowerShell commands In the preceding figure, a list of all commands that contain the string Process is shown. Cmdlets can be filtered more effectively by filtering the search based on -Verb or -Noun parameters. Use the -Verb parameter to see all commands that start with a specific verb: Get-Command -Verb Get The preceding command lists all cmdlets that begin with Get , such as Get-Process , Get- Service , and Get-Item . Similarly, users can search for cmdlets related to a specific object by using the -Noun parameter, using the following command: Get-Command -Noun Service The preceding command will return all cmdlets where the noun is Service , such as Get-Service , Start-Service , and Stop-Service . In a situation where the user knows the module name but not the cmdlet name, they can use the -Module parameter. The following command returns all cmdlets from the mentioned module: Get-Command -Module Microsoft.PowerShell.Management The results can be narrowed down using the preceding techniques, as shown in the following figure: Figure 3.5 : PowerShell commands and results Once the command is found, the next step is to find the command parameters. To do this, the -Syntax parameter can be used, as shown in the following figure: Figure 3.6 : Syntax parameter Another approach is to use the Get-Help command, mentioned in Chapter 1 , Getting Started with PowerShell . This command is designed to output all the needed information and includes the syntax of the command, shown in the preceding figure. Cmdlets are the main commands in PowerShell that are built to handle specific tasks easily. They follow a Verb-Noun structure, making it simple to understand what they do. Use Get- Command and Get-Help or filter by verb and noun to find the right cmdlet. Wildcards make searching more flexible, and the pipeline ( | ) lets users chain cmdlets together for even more power. PowerShell modules Now that you know how to use cmdlets, let us take it a step further and discover modules. Modules are collections of cmdlets, functions, and scripts that extend PowerShell’s capabilities. Instead of working with individual cmdlets, modules group related commands together, making them easier to manage. Relationship between modules and cmdlets Imagine you have a toolbox filled with different tools for various tasks. A PowerShell module works the same way. It contains multiple cmdlets that help to perform specific tasks. Once a module is installed, it gives access to all the cmdlets inside it, just like adding new tools to the toolbox. For example, PowerShell does not have built-in cmdlets for Azure management. However, by installing the Az module, you can gain access to Azure related commands, allowing you to manage tasks in Azure directly from PowerShell. The following list outlines the three types of modules in PowerShell: Built-in modules : Built-in modules come with PowerShell by default and provide essential cmdlets for managing files, services, processes, and other system tasks. For example, the module Microsoft.PowerShell.Management includes commands for handling the file system, registry, and services. Since these modules are pre-installed, they are always available without needing installation. Community modules : A community or custom module is a PowerShell module created and shared by developers, IT professionals, or organizations within the PowerShell community. These modules are typically published on the PowerShell Gallery or other repositories, allowing others to download and use them. Unlike Microsoft-provided modules (such as Az or ExchangeOnlineManagement ), community modules are not officially maintained by Microsoft. Instead, they are developed and updated by individuals or groups to solve specific problems, automate tasks, or extend PowerShell’s functionality. Custom modules : Custom modules are created by users themselves to solve specific tasks. They can include custom cmdlets, functions, and scripts tailored to a specific environment. For example, a company might create a Custom-ADManagement module for managing Active Directory users and groups. Installing and managing PowerShell modules PowerShell modules add new cmdlets to the environment. Managing these modules properly helps keep everything running smoothly and efficiently. Knowing how to install, update, and remove modules is important for keeping your PowerShell setup organized and working well. Keeping modules up to date ensures you have the latest features, security fixes, and improvements, while removing old or unused ones helps keep things clean and fast. Find and install modules from PowerShell Gallery Complete the following steps to install PowerShell modules using the PowerShell Gallery: Open the following link to browse the PowerShell Gallery: https://www.powershellgallery.com/ In the Gallery, you can find all available modules. Here, we will install the ExchangeOnlineManagement module. Search for the ExchangeOnlineManagement module and open it. You can see the module in the following figure: Figure 3.7 : Overview of the ExchangeOnlineManagement module in the PowerShell Gallery Copy the installation command shown in the preceding figure. Paste it in the terminal in VS Code and execute the command. The screen will give a prompt that a NuGet provider is required to continue, as illustrated in Figure 3.8 : Figure 3.8 : Prompt requesting installation of the NuGet provider during module setup Click [ Y ] to install the NuGet provider. Click [ Y ] again to trust the repository. You can set the installation policy to trusted for the PSGallery by executing the following command: Set-PsRepository -name PSGallery -InstallationPolicy Trusted In the following figure, you can see that after execution of the preceding command, the repository is trusted: Figure 3.9 : PowerShell Gallery repository marked as trusted after updating the installation policy Now execute the following command again: Install-Module -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement -Scope CurrentUser The module is installed under the current directory of the user: C:\Users\testuser\Documents\WindowsPowerShell\Modules To install it for the whole system, you need to change the scope to AllUsers , as shown in the following snippet: Install-Module -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement -Scope AllUsers The Module is installed under the following Directory : C:\Program Files\WindowsPowerShell\Modules\ExchangeOnlineManagement Note: If you install it without administrator permission, you will get an error that administrator rights are required! Before using any module that has been installed, it needs to be imported. To import the module, execute the following command: Import-Module -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement Now, the module is imported, and you can check if the cmdlets are available. Execute Get-Command -module ExchangeOnlineManagement to get the available commands. In the following figure, it can be observed that some commands listed from the ExchangeOnlineManagemant module: Figure 3.10 : Available cmdlets after importing the ExchangeOnlineManagement module Find modules with PowerShell Searching for modules is also possible with a PowerShell command. Using the Find-Module cmdlet offers users the opportunity to find modules in the PowerShell Gallery. In the following figure, you can see the use of Find-Module exch* to find all modules in the gallery starting with exch : Figure 3.11 : Result of searching the PowerShell Gallery using the Find-Module cmdlet Updating modules in PowerShell IT systems evolve fast, and so do PowerShell modules. They get updates with new features, bug fixes, and security improvements. That is why it is important to keep PowerShell modules updated. However, module updates can break scripts that rely on the current version. Thus, users must always test the new version first before rolling it out to production. Once you are sure everything works as expected, you can safely update it in your environment. The following list has some scenarios for updating, finding, and installing modules. You can run these cmdlets in your test environment to get some experience in working with modules: 1. Update the ExchangeOnlineManagement module to the latest version: Update - Module -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement 2. Update all installed modules from the PowerShell Gallery: Update - Module 3. Check the currently installed version of ExchangeOnlineManagement : Get - Module -ListAvailable -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement 4. Check the latest available version in the PowerShell Gallery: Find- Module -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement 5. Update the module for all users (requires running PowerShell as Administrator): Update - Module -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement - Scope AllUsers 6. Install a specific version (if a script requires a specific version and updating is not an option): Install- Module -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement -RequiredVersion 3.2.0 -Force 7. Remove ExchangeOnlineManagement from the imported modules before uninstalling it: Remove- Module -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement 8. Uninstall the module: Uninstall- Module -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement - AllVersions Install- Module -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement 9. Verify the update after installation: Get - Module -ListAvailable -Name ExchangeOnlineManagement Tips for the management of modules The following are some useful tips for module management: Always choose to Run as Administrator when installing or updating modules for all users. Use - Scope CurrentUser to install modules without admin rights. Check for updates regularly using Find-Module and Update-Module . Test new module versions in a separate environment before updating in production. Use -RequiredVersion to install specific versions. Remove old versions to avoid conflicts using Uninstall-Module -AllVersions . The following command displays all PowerShell module locations: $env:PSModulePath -split ";" Figure 3.12 shows all PowerShell module directories: Figure 3.12 : Default PowerShell module directories defined in the PSModulePath The preceding command can be helpful when troubleshooting issues where PowerShell fails to load a module. You can verify whether the module is installed in the correct location by checking these paths. PowerShell Gallery PowerShell Gallery is Microsoft’s official online repository for PowerShell modules and scripts. It provides a way to find, install, update, and share modules easily without needing to download and configure them manually. The Gallery includes both official Microsoft modules and community-created modules, offering a wide selection of tools. Many of these modules are signed and verified, ensuring they come from trusted sources. PowerShell can also update installed modules automatically, keeping them up to date with new features and security fixes. With a few simple commands, users can search for modules, install them, and manage their PowerShell environment more efficiently. Understanding the NuGet provider NuGet provider is an important part of PowerShell that lets users download, install, and update modules from PowerShell Gallery. It works like a package manager, making sure that PowerShell can get the modules and any extra parts needed from an online source. Without it, PowerShell would not be able to install or update modules from the Gallery. One of the main reasons the NuGet provider is so important is that it helps manage all the dependencies a module might need to work properly. It also ensures that downloads come from trusted sources, so that something unsafe is not accidentally installed on the system. Since the PowerShell Gallery depends on NuGet, PowerShell needs it to install new modules. Before installing a module, it is always a good idea to check if the NuGet provider is already installed. If it is missing, it needs to be installed so that PowerShell can connect to the PowerShell Gallery. When first attempting to install a module, users are prompted to install the NuGet provider before proceeding. After installing it, users can install modules without being asked again. Keeping the NuGet provider up to date is important to ensure everything works smoothly when installing or updating PowerShell modules. Commonly used PowerShell modules Table 3.3 lists some commonly used PowerShell modules and their usage. When working with PowerShell, you will likely need to use some of these modules. Thus, it is helpful to know what they are and what they do. A basic understanding of these modules can make your work easier and help you find the right commands for different tasks. Module name Purpose Out-of-the-box Microsoft.PowerShell.Management Manages files, processes, services, and system settings Yes Microsoft.PowerShell.Utility Provides utility cmdlets for formatting, debugging, and remote execution Yes ActiveDirectory Manages Active Directory users, groups, and computers No (requires RSAT: Active Directory) PSWindowsUpdate Automates Windows Updates on local and remote machines No (available from PowerShell Gallery) ImportExcel Works with Excel files without needing Excel installed No (available from PowerShell Gallery) SqlServer Manage SQL Server instances, databases, and queries No (available from PowerShell Gallery) Pester Runs tests and validations for PowerShell scripts No (available from PowerShell Gallery) ExchangeOnlineManagement Manages Microsoft Exchange Online mailboxes and settings No (available from PowerShell Gallery) Az Manages Azure resources and services No (available from PowerShell Gallery) Microsoft.Graph Administers Microsoft cloud services. No (available from PowerShell Gallery) Table 3.3 : Windows PowerShell modules You can also explore out-of-the-box PowerShell commands through the following link: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/module/microsoft.powershell.diagnostics/?view=powershell-7.5 These modules are widely used for system administration, cloud management, automation, and testing. Understanding which module to use for a specific task makes working with PowerShell much easier and more efficient. However, these are just a few of the thousands of modules available. No matter what you are trying to accomplish, there is a good chance that a module already exists to help you solve the problem more quickly and effectively. Conclusion Cmdlets and modules are the building blocks of PowerShell, allowing users to perform tasks efficiently and extend PowerShell’s capabilities. In this chapter, you learned how to use cmdlets, their syntax, and parameters. You also learned to manage modules to enhance functionality. This chapter also explored PowerShell Gallery as a source for discovering new modules.
