PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 4 Variables, Operators, and Loops Introduction Understanding variables, operators, and loops is key when writing PowerShell scripts. They help make your code more flexible, reusable, and ready for real automation tasks. Variables are like storage boxes; they hold values such as numbers, text, or objects. You can use them to store results from a command, keep track of counters in a loop, or pass data from one part of the script to another. PowerShell makes working with variables easy, and you do not even need to declare a type as PowerShell figures it out for you. Operators are the tools that let you work with those variables. You can compare values, perform calculations, or check conditions. Whether you are adding numbers, checking if a user exists, or replacing text in a string, operators help make decisions and shape the flow of your script. Loops take it a step further. Instead of writing the same command over and over, loops help you repeat actions like processing a list of files, users, or data rows. Using for, foreach, while, and do-while loops, you can handle almost any kind of repetition. Together, variables, operators, and loops form the core of most scripts. Once you understand how to use them, you will be able to build scripts that can react to different inputs, repeat actions automatically, and handle logic that adjusts to each situation. This is where automation begins. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: • Variables in PowerShell • Operators in PowerShell • Loops in PowerShell • Conditional statements Objectives By the end of this chapter, the readers will be able to define and use different types of variables in PowerShell, apply logical, comparison, and arithmetic operators effectively, and write loops such as for, foreach, while, and do-while to automate repetitive tasks. They will learn to control script execution flow using if, else, and switch statements, allowing them to build efficient, dynamic, and reusable PowerShell scripts for automation. Variables in PowerShell Think about a storage room filled with different storage boxes. Each box is designed to hold specific items. For example, if you want to store water, you will use a box that is designed to hold liquids. If you need to store apples, you will choose a box that keeps them fresh. Similarly, in PowerShell, variables act like storage boxes, holding different types of data. Just like you would not put water in a box meant for apples, you need to use the right type of variable for the data you are storing. Some variables store text, some store numbers, and others can hold multiple values, like a list of names. Knowing the correct variable type helps understand how to handle the data properly. By using variables, you can store, organize, and reuse information in your scripts, making them more dynamic and efficient, just like a well-organized storage room helps you to find what you need quickly. Variable syntax The syntax for variables in PowerShell is simple and flexible. Every variable name must begin with the ( $ ) symbol, followed by a descriptive name that reflects its purpose. This makes it easier to understand what the variable stores just by looking at its name. As a best practice, variable names should only contain letters, numbers, and underscores (_). Avoid using spaces or special characters, as they can make variables harder to use and lead to errors in scripts. Working with variables To assign a variable to a value, we use the assignment operator ( = ). It is not necessary to declare the variable before using it. The default value of each variable is $null . $Null is the equivalent of empty. So, each variable assigned by $null will also be cleared. Figure 4.1 shows some examples of how we can assign values to variables. It is also possible to assign command output directly to a variable. This is shown in the following figure in line 74 : Figure 4.1 : Assigning command output to PowerShell variables If needed, you can also clear the content of the variable or remove this variable completely from the script. Using Clear-Variable Services will clear the variable services. We do not need to use $Services because we are passing it as a parameter to the Clear-Variable command. Another method to clear a variable is to assign $null to the variable. The result using both methods will be the same. To remove a variable, you can use the following commands: Remove-Variable variablename Remove-Item -Path Variable:\variablename If you need to find out what variables are actually assigned, you will use the command as follows: Get-Variable The Get-Variable command outputs all variable names and their assigned values. In the output, there are also many prefilled variables from PowerShell available. You can differentiate between variables by user-created variables, automatic variables, and preference variables. The details are as follows: User-created variables : These are the kind of variables you can define yourself in a script or command. They store values like text, numbers, arrays, or objects and can be used throughout your script. Automatic variables : These are built-in variables created and maintained by PowerShell. They hold system-generated values, such as the current script's path, error details, and pipeline objects. You do not need to define them as they are always available. Examples of automatic variables are as follows: o $PSVersionTable : Displays the PowerShell version details. o $Error : Stores all error messages from previous commands. o $PWD : Shows the current working directory. • Preference variables : These variables control PowerShell behavior when the script is executed, including error handling, output formatting, and debugging. Examples of preference variables are as follows: o $ErrorActionPreference : Determines how errors are handled ( Stop , Continue , SilentlyContinue , Ignore ). o $VerbosePreference : Controls if verbose messages are shown or not. o $ConfirmPreference : Defines when confirmation prompts appear. Variable types In PowerShell, variables can hold different types of data, and using the right data type helps make scripts work better. The types are automatically assigned by PowerShell depending on the value you are using. Some variables store text, some hold numbers, and others keep lists or organized data. Knowing how these types work in the background will help you save, manage, and use information properly in your scripts. The following code sample shows how values can be assigned to variables. The hashtag on the right side of each line is only a comment with the datatype: #-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-# #Variable #DataType # #-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-#-# $Name = "Michael" # String $Number = 42 # Integer $Price = 19.99 # Double $IsAdmin = $true # Boolean $Fruits = @( "Apple" , "Banana" , "Orange" ) # Array After assigning a value to a variable, you can check the variable type using the command GetType() . In the following figure, the data types are listed after executing the GetType() command for each variable. The output is shown on the right side of the figure after executing the variables on the left side: Figure 4.2 : Executing PowerShell variables Using the GetType() command is very helpful for debugging scripts and identifying errors. Some cmdlets require specific data types, so it is best practice to know what data types are expected by the commands you use. Checking if your variables have the correct data type ensures that your script runs smoothly and avoids unexpected issues. It is not forbidden to assign different data types to a variable. We can assign an integer, a string, or a date object to the same variable. In the following figure, three different object types are assigned to one variable: Figure 4.3 : Assigning object types to PowerShell variables Using the GetType() command is not enough to find out what data types are stored in the array. Right now, it is only an array of objects. Now we need to determine the object types. In order to do this, we will use the Get-Member command and select the TypeName . In the following code sample, you can see the command used to determine the object types that are in the $arrayofobjects variable : $arrayofobjects | get - member | Select -Object TypeName - Unique &lt;# Output : TypeName -------- System.Int32 System.String System.DateTime #&gt; Assigning different data types to an object array is not considered best practice and should be avoided. The recommended approach is to explicitly cast variables to ensure they only hold values with the intended data types or values that can be automatically converted. This improves script readability, reduces errors, and makes the code easier to manage. Cast variables Casting can be used to define the data type of a variable, making sure that it only accepts values of that type or those that can be automatically converted. To cast a variable, users must use brackets before the variable. The following example shows the syntax of a cast variable: [datatype]$variable The following snippet shows the casting of the variable $number to an integer datatype. Then we will check what happens if we assign a string with numbers and a string with letters: [ int ] $number = 12 $number = "123456789" # The assigned string is converted to an integer. $number = "This is a String" # This will throw an Error because $number is casted as an integer After casting the $number variable as an integer as given in the preceding snippet, we restricted it to only hold integer values or values that can be converted into an integer. Then, we assigned a numeric string to the variable. Since the string contained only numbers, PowerShell automatically converted it to an integer without any issues. However, we then attempted to assign a string containing alphabetical characters. This value could not be converted to an integer, so PowerShell returned an error that the input string was not in a valid format. Using variables in commands After assigning values to variables, we can use these values in each part of our script. To showcase this, we will use the Write-Output command in the following code section: Write-Output "The Name of the User is $Name with the Number $Number `n" The following figure shows the expected output of the Write-Output command: Figure 4.4 : Write-Output command These two variables have been added to dynamically insert the name and number into the string. Additionally, variables can be used as parameter values in commands, making scripts more flexible and reusable for different environments. Scopes in PowerShell In PowerShell, variable scope defines where a variable can be accessed and modified. There are three main scopes, which include local, script, and global. Understanding these scopes helps prevent conflicts and ensures that variables behave as expected in different parts of a script or session. Local scope The local scope applies to variables inside a function or script block. A variable declared within this scope is only accessible within the same function or block where it was created. Once the function or block finishes executing, the variable is removed. Local scope is the default in PowerShell, meaning that variables exist only within their current execution context. The following code sample shows an example of the local scope: function Test - LocalScope { $LocalVar = "I exist only in this function" Write-Output $LocalVar } Test-LocalScope Write-Output $LocalVar # This will return nothing because $LocalVar is not accessible outside the function. Script scope The script scope applies to variables that exist throughout a single script execution. A variable declared in script scope can be accessed anywhere within that script, but cannot be accessed from outside the script. This is useful for keeping variables contained within a script without affecting the global PowerShell session. An example of script scope is shown in the following code sample: $Script :MyScriptVar = "Only available in this script" function Show - ScriptVar { Write-Output $Script :MyScriptVar } Show-ScriptVar Write-Output "This Variables is $MyScriptVar " # Will output the $myScriptVar variable Global scope The global scope applies to the entire PowerShell session. A variable declared in this scope can be accessed from anywhere, including scripts, functions, and the command line. Global variables should be used carefully, as they persist across different scripts and functions, potentially causing conflicts. The following is an example of the global scope: $Global :MyGlobalVar = "Accessible everywhere" function Show - GlobalVar { Write-Output $Global :MyGlobalVar } Show-GlobalVar # Accessible everywhere Write-Output "This Variables is $MyGlobalVar " # Will output the $myGlobalVar variable Operators in PowerShell When developing with PowerShell, you need a way to perform actions like comparing values, doing math, handling text, or managing data. This is where operators come in. Think of operators as special symbols or words that tell PowerShell what kind of operation to perform. Just like in basic math, where + means addition and - means subtraction, PowerShell has its own set of operators to help you work with numbers, text, conditions, and even objects. Types of operators PowerShell provides several types of operators to perform different kinds of tasks. This section gives an overview of each type, including an explanation of where they are used and a list of available operators. Arithmetic operators These operators help perform basic mathematical operations, such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division. They are commonly used in scripts that deal with numbers, such as calculating storage sizes. Note: Used for mathematical calculations. The operators are listed in the following table: Operator Description Example + Addition 10+2  12 - Subtraction 8 – 4  4 * Multiplication 2 * 6  12 / Division 10 / 2  5 % Modulus 10 % 3  1 Table 4.1 : PowerShell arithmetic operators In the following code sample, you can see an example of arithmetic operators and their results in PowerShell: 10 + 2 #result 12 8 - 4 #result 4 2 * 6 #result 12 10 / 2 #result 5 10 % 3 #result 1 Assignment operators Assignment operators in PowerShell are commonly used to set, update, or modify the value of a variable during script execution. Note: Used to assign values to variables. The operators in the following table are used to store or update values in variables directly: Operator Description Example = Assigning a value $x = 5 += Add and assign $x += 3 -= Subtract and assign $x -= 2 *= Multiply and assign $x *= 4 /= Divide and assign $x /= 2 Table 4.2 : PowerShell assignment operators The following code shows an example of assignment operators and their results in PowerShell: 1. $x = 5 #result 5 2. $x += 3 #result 8 3. $x -= 2 #result 6 4. $x *= 4 #result 24 5. $x /= 2 #result 12 Comparison operators Comparison operators in PowerShell are commonly used to compare values and determine if they are equal, not equal, greater than, or less than, often within conditions like if statements. Note: Used to compare values and return true or false. The comparison operators in Table 4.2 are useful when making decisions in scripts: Operator Description Example -eq equal 10 -eq 10: True -ne Not equal 5 -ne 6: True -gt greater than 10 -gt 5: True -lt Lesser than 3 -lt 8: True -ge Greater than or equal 6 -ge 6: True -le Lesser than or equal 4 -le 5: True Table 4.3 : PowerShell comparison operators In the following code, you can see an example of comparison operators and their results in PowerShell: 10 -eq 10 # result True 5 -ne 6 # result True 10 -gt 5 # result True 3 -lt 8 # result True 6 -ge 6 # result True 4 -le 5 # result True Logical operators Logical operators are used in scripts when you need to evaluate multiple conditions at once, such as checking if a user has access and is active. Note: Used to combine multiple conditions. In the following table, you can see some examples: Operator Description Example -and Returns true if both conditions are met $true -and $false: False -or Returns true if at least one condition is met $true -or $false: True -not Negates a condition -not $true: False ! Alias for -not !$false: True Table 4.4 : PowerShell logical operators Refer to the following code sample for an example of logical operators and their results in PowerShell: ($ true - and $ false ) # result False ($ true - or $ false ) # result True (- not $ true ) # result False (!$ false ) # result True String operators String operators help find patterns in text, modify strings, and check if a value matches a certain format. Note: Used to search, compare, and manipulate text. The following table has some examples of string operators: Operator Description Example -like Check if a string matches a pattern. "Hello" -like "H*": True -notlike Check if a string does not match a pattern. "PowerShell" -notlike "P*": False -match Check if a string matches a pattern or a regex pattern. "abc123" -match "\d+": True -match Check if a string matches a pattern or a regex pattern. abc123" -match "abc": True -notmatch Check if a string does not match a regex pattern. "abc" -notmatch "\d+": True -replace Replace a part of a string. “Hello” –replace “l”, “x”: “Hexxo” Table 4.5 : PowerShell string operators The following code shows an example of an output using string operators: ("Hello" - like «H* ") # result True ("PowerShell" -notlike "P*") # result False ("abc123" - match «\d+ ") # result True ("abc123" - match «abc ") # result True ("abc" -notmatch "\d+") # result True (“Hello” –replace “l”, “x”) # result Hexxo PowerShell operators help you work faster and easier. They let you do math, compare values, work with text, check conditions, and move data between commands. You can write better scripts with less effort by learning how to use these operators. Whether you need to add numbers, check if something is true or false, or modify text, operators simplify the process. Note: The more you use operators, the more you will realize how powerful they are. Try them out, experiment a little, and see what is possible! Loops in PowerShell When writing scripts, you often need to repeat actions multiple times. Instead of typing the same code again and again, PowerShell provides loops that automatically run a block of code as many times as needed. Loops are helpful when working with multiple files, going through lists of data, or running commands until a certain condition is met. They make automation easier and save time. In this section, we will look at the four main types of loops in PowerShell and how to use them. For loop Loops in PowerShell are used to repeat actions multiple times, making it easy to automate tasks like processing lists or running commands until a condition is met. Note: Best for counting and iteration. In a for loop, we use variable assignments, comparison operators, and increment or decrement operations to control how many times the loop runs. The loop follows a specific structure, shown as follows: An initialization : o This is where a starting value for the loop variable is setup. o Exampl e: $i = 1 (We start counting from 1 ). A condition : o This checks if the loop should keep running. o The loop runs as long as this condition is true. o Example : $i -le 5 (Run while $i is less than or equal to 5 ). An increment or decrement (updates the counter) : o This updates the loop variable at the end of each cycle. o Example : $i++ (Increases $i by 1 each time). In the following sample, you can see how a for loop is structured: for (&lt; initialization &gt;; &lt; condition &gt;; &lt;increment/decrement&gt;) { &lt; code to execute &gt; } The following code is an example of a for loop. This loop runs as long as $i is less than or equal to 5 , based on the condition we set. Each time the loop executes, $i increases by 1 until the condition is no longer met. for ( $i = 1 ; $i -le 5 ; $i ++) { Write-Output "Iteration $i " } #Output : Iteration 1 Iteration 2 Iteration 3 Iteration 4 Iteration 5 When using the for loop in PowerShell, you need to manage the loop variable carefully to avoid infinite loops. If you forget to update the counter ( $i++ or $i-- ), the loop will run forever, causing your script to hang. Also, be mindful of errors when setting the loop condition, as using -lt instead of -le or vice versa can lead to missing or extra iterations. The main advantage of the for loop is its control and flexibility. Since it explicitly defines initialization, condition, and increment or decrement, it allows precise iteration through a set number of cycles. This makes it ideal for tasks where you need a fixed number of repetitions, such as iterating through numeric ranges, processing indexes in arrays, or performing calculations that require step-based iteration. Foreach loop The foreach loop is designed to iterate through a collection of items, such as arrays, lists, or objects. Instead of using a counter, it automatically picks each item from the collection and processes it one-by-one. Note: Best for lists and arrays. The loop follows a simple structure, shown as follows: • A collection to loop through : o The foreach loop requires a collection of items (like an array or a list). • A loop variable : o This holds the current item from the collection in each iteration. o Example : $name in $names (Each time the loop runs, $name holds the next item). • A block of code to execute : o The code inside the loop runs once for each item in the collection. In the following code, you can see an example of a foreach loop. This loop runs once for each item in the collection, assigning the current item to a variable and executing the code block until all items have been processed: $names = @( "Ahmed" , "Michael" , "Mateo" , "Katharina" , "Peter S" , "Peter M" ) foreach ( $name in $names ) { Write-output "Hello $name !" } Output : Hello Ahmed! Hello Michael! Hello Mateo! Hello Katharina! Hello Peter S! Hello Peter M! The biggest advantage of the foreach loop is its simplicity when working with collections. It automatically picks each item from an array or object list and assigns it to a variable, making the code cleaner and easier to read. This makes foreach ideal for processing lists of users, files, or other structured data without the need for manual indexing. While loop The while loop in PowerShell keeps running a block of code as long as a specified condition is true, making it useful when the number of repetitions is not known in advance. Note: Best for unknown repetitions. Unlike the for loop, it does not have an automatic counter. Instead, the condition is checked before each iteration, and the loop continues until the condition is no longer met. This is useful when you do not know in advance how many times the loop should run. It has the following structure: • A condition to check : o The loop keeps running while the condition is true. o Example : $i -gt 0 (runs while $i is greater than 0 ). • A block of code to execute : o This code runs repeatedly as long as the condition remains true. • An update inside the loop : o Since while does not have a built-in counter, you must manually update any variables inside the loop to prevent infinite looping. o Example : $i-- (decreases $i in each loop). The following code sample shows an example of a while loop. This loop runs as long as the condition is true, executing the code inside and updating the variable each time. Once the condition is false, the loop stops. $i = 5 while ( $i - gt 0 ) { Write-Output "Countdown: $i " $i -- # Decrease $i each time } When using a while loop in PowerShell, make sure the condition eventually becomes false. If it does not, the loop will run forever, and your script could get stuck. Unlike the for loop, the while loop does not update variables automatically, so you have to change them inside the loop to make sure it stops at the right time. The best thing about the while loop is that it keeps running until a condition is met, even if you do not know in advance how many times it needs to run. This makes it great for waiting for something to finish, checking for user input, or running commands until a certain result is reached. It gives you flexibility when working with unpredictable situations. Do-while loop The do-while loop is similar to the while loop, but with one key difference, which is that it always runs at least once, even if the condition is false from the start. This is because the condition is checked after the loop executes, not before. Note: Best for running at least. The do-while loop is structured as follows: • A block of code to execute first : o The code inside the loop runs at least once before checking the condition. • A condition to check after each execution : o The loop continues running while the condition is true. o Example: $i -le 10 (Runs as long as $i is 10 or less). In the following figure, you can see an example of a do-while loop, which runs at least once and continues executing as long as the condition is true: do { $userinput = Read-Host "Enter a number greater than 10" } while ( $userinput -le 10) Write-Host "You entered $userinput , which is valid!" &lt;# Output : Enter a number greater than 10: 5 Enter a number greater than 10: 10 Enter a number greater than 10: 15 You entered 15, which is valid! #&gt; The do-while loop always runs at least once, no matter what. This means even if the condition is false from the start, the loop will still run one time. Be careful when using it for things like user input or system checks because the first run happens before PowerShell checks if the loop should continue. Also, like the while loop, make sure the condition eventually becomes false, or the loop might run forever. The best thing about the do-while loop is that it guarantees at least one execution before checking the condition. This makes it useful when you need to do something at least once, like asking a user for input, retrying a command, or running a process before deciding to repeat it. Conditional statements In PowerShell, conditional statements let your script make decisions. Instead of always running the same commands, your script can check conditions and choose what to do based on the results. This makes your code more dynamic and flexible. The following list outlines the two most common ways to handle conditions in PowerShell: If-else statement : For checking a few specific conditions. Switch statement : For handling multiple possible values efficiently. In this section, we will explore both if-else and switch statements, how they work, and when to use them. If-else statement The if-else statement runs different blocks of code based on whether a condition is true or false. Note: Best for simple condition checks. It follows the structure in the following code: if (&lt;condition &gt;) { &lt;code to run if condition is true &gt; } elseif (&lt;another condition&gt;) { &lt;code to run if this condition is true &gt; } else { &lt;code to run if no conditions are true &gt; } With the if-else statement, you have full control over what happens inside the condition. You can run any PowerShell command, call functions, or even add additional conditions inside. This makes it possible to handle complex decisions in your scripts. The following code is an example with a for loop inside an if statement: $number = 3 if ( $number - gt 0 ) { Write-Output "Counting from 1 to $number :» for ( $i = 1 ; $i -le $number ; $i ++) { Write-Output $i } } else { Write-Output "Number is not greater than 0." } &lt;# result : Counting from 1 to 3 : 1 2 3 #&gt; The if-else statement helps your script make decisions based on conditions. It is easy to use and flexible, allowing you to add extra checks, loops, or functions inside. This makes it great for handling different situations in your script. It also keeps your code clear and works with all types of data, like numbers, text, or user input. Additionally, it ensures that only the correct commands run when needed, making your script more efficient and smarter. However, good formatting is important. Writing clean and well-structured code helps avoid confusion, especially when dealing with multiple conditions in longer scripts. Switch statement The switch statement is useful when you need to check multiple possible values and decide what to do based on them. Instead of writing many if-elseif conditions, the switch organizes everything in a cleaner manner, which makes it easier to read. Note: Best for multiple cases. It goes through each possible value and runs the matching code, making scripts shorter and more efficient. This is especially helpful when working with user input, menu selections, or different system states. The structure of the switch statement is as follows: switch (&lt;value&gt;) { &lt;case1&gt; { &lt; code to run if value matches case1&gt; } &lt;case2&gt; { &lt; code to run if value matches case2&gt; } default { &lt; code to run if no cases match&gt; } } The switch statement checks the value and runs the matching block of code. Instead of using multiple if-elseif conditions, the switch statement makes the script cleaner and easier to read. The switch statement in PowerShell follows a clear structure. Each part has a specific role in deciding what code should run: switch (&lt;value&gt;) : This is the value being checked. PowerShell will compare it against different cases. &lt;case1&gt; and &lt;case2&gt; : These are possible values that PowerShell checks. If the value matches one of these, the corresponding code runs. default : This runs only if none of the cases match. It works like an else in an if-else statement. Consider an example for better understanding. The following script checks the value of $role and runs the matching case. If $role is "Admin" , it prints "You have full access." If it is "Editor" , it prints "You can edit content." If it is "Viewer" , it prints "You can only view content." If $role does not match any of these values, it prints "Unknown role." This last case is called the default case, which runs when no other cases match. $role = "Editor" switch ( $role ) { "Admin" { Write-Host "You have full access." } "Editor" { Write-Host "You can edit content." } "Viewer" { Write-Host "You can only view content." } default { Write-Host "Unknown role." } } #Output : You can edit content. The switch statement is a powerful way to handle multiple conditions in PowerShell. It provides a cleaner and more efficient alternative to long if-elseif chains, making scripts easier to read and manage. It works with different data types, such as numbers and text, ensuring that only the correct commands run based on the given value. The switch statement is especially useful for handling, for example, user roles, menu selections, and system settings, making decisions, and making scripts more structured and efficient. Conclusion In this chapter, we looked at some of the most important parts of PowerShell variables, operators, loops, and conditions. We saw how variables are used to store information like text, numbers, or lists. Then, we learned how operators help us do things like math or compare values. Loops came in handy when something needed to run more than once, and conditional statements helped the script make decisions. All of these pieces work together to make scripts smarter and more useful. They help avoid repeating the same code and make it easier to change things when needed. Scripts become more flexible and can do more based on the situation. Understanding these basics makes it much easier to build scripts that save time and reduce manual work. With just a few lines of code, it is possible to handle tasks that would take much longer to do by hand. In the next chapter, the focus will be on working with objects and the pipeline. This will show how PowerShell handles data in a more structured way, how information moves between commands, and how to get the exact results needed, clean, organized, and ready to use. PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 5 Working with PowerShell Objects and Functions Introduction In this chapter, we will explore the reason why PowerShell is called an object-oriented scripting language. PowerShell is not just about running commands and getting outputs. It is about working with real objects that carry detailed information and can be manipulated easily. This is one of the main reasons PowerShell stands out from other scripting or command line tools. When executing a command in PowerShell, the result is not plain text. It is a structured object that you can filter, sort, and format without requiring additional effort. This makes it efficient when handling data from systems, such as Active Directory, file systems, or even application programming interfaces ( APIs ). You do not need to parse the output line-by-line. Instead, you can access properties directly, like $object.Name or $object.Status . Alongside objects, functions play a significant role in writing clean and reusable scripts. Think of a function as a block of code that does a specific job. You can call it whenever you need it, and it can even return results just like a PowerShell command would. Functions help you keep your scripts organized and avoid repeating the same code again and again. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: PowerShell functions Function parameters and return values Understanding the PowerShell pipeline PowerShell objects PowerShell custom objects Working with object properties Advanced features of function Handling errors with try, catch, and finally Organizing functions into modules Best practices using functions Objectives This chapter starts by introducing how objects work in PowerShell and why they are important for working with data. The readers will learn how to access object properties, filter results, and use the pipeline to pass data from one command to another in a simple and efficient way. Next, the focus will be on creating custom objects. These are useful when one wants to structure their own data, when building reports, or collecting information in a clean and readable format. The second part of the chapter will explain how to create and use functions. Functions help to group parts of the script into reusable blocks. This avoids repeating code and makes the scripts easier to update and understand. The readers will also learn how to use parameters and return values inside functions. Later, more advanced function features will be covered, such as setting default values, validating input, and combining outputs. After that, the chapter will guide you on how to organize their functions into full scripts and reusable modules. This becomes especially useful in larger projects where keeping things organized matters. They will also learn how to use try, catch, and finally blocks to handle errors properly and make sure their scripts keep running even when something goes wrong. By the end of this chapter, they will be able to work with objects and functions in PowerShell. Their scripts will be cleaner and easier to maintain. PowerShell functions A function in PowerShell is a named block of code that does one specific job. It is written once and then used when needed by just calling its name. This saves users from writing the same code repeatedly. Functions help keep the script neat and clean. Instead of putting everything into one big file, the work can be broken down into small, easy-to-read pieces. For example, you can have one function to create users, another to remove users, and a third to check their status. Functions also make scripts easier to update and reuse. If something changes, you only need to change it in one place. Functions can even be copied into other scripts or shared with other users. Create and call a function Creating a function is simple. Use the function keyword, give it a name, and write the code inside curly brackets. To create a simple PowerShell function, execute the following code: function Hello-PowerShell { Write-Output "Hello from PowerShell!" } Hello-PowerShell The function is defined once and can be called multiple times. The name of the function can be used like a regular command. Reusing logic instead of repeating code Let us say you are writing a backup script and need to show the same status message several times. Instead of copying and pasting the same line in the code, you can put it in a function. That way, if you want to change the message, you only need to change it in one place. This saves time and reduces mistakes. The following example shows the Print-Message function: function Print-Message { Write-Output "Everything is running smoothly." } # Call the function Print-Message Function parameters and return values Functions can take input values, which are called parameters . Parameters make functions more useful and flexible as they allow users to change a function’s behavior depending on the input. Parameters can be defined using the param() block at the top of the function. One or more parameters can be added, and default values can also be set. When a user calls the function, the values for these parameters are passed using their names. This way, the same function logic can be reused to get different results based on the input. Parameters are like doors you open to pass information into your function. In the following example, the function can take input, which makes the function flexible and reusable. The param() block is used to define parameters, shown as follows: function Greet-User { param ( [ string ]$Name ) Write-Output "Hello, $Name!" } Greet-User -Name "Ahmed" There is an alternative method to define parameters using parentheses directly after the function name, shown as follows: function Test-Function ($param1, $param2) { Write-Output "Param1: $param1" Write-Output "Param2: $param2" } Test-Function "One" "Two" The basic parameter method is a shorter way to write a function with parameters, but it is less flexible than using the param() block. For simple functions with few parameters, the parentheses method works well. However, for anything more advanced (like validation, default values, or parameter sets), use the param() block. Setting default values Sometimes, you want your function to use a value even when the caller has not passed anything. This is where default values come in. A default value can be assigned to a parameter, and PowerShell will use it automatically if the caller leaves the value out. This helps in making functions easier to use because users do not always need to specify every detail. It is also useful when users want a function to always have a safe starting point. In the following code block, we assign default values to parameters, and if no value is passed, the default is used: function Greet-User { param ( [ string ]$Name = "Guest" ) Write-Output "Hello, $Name!" } Returning data from a function Functions can also send information back to the part of the script that called them. This is called returning data . The data that comes back can be simple, like a string or a number, or more complex, like a custom object. Returning data is useful when a function calculates something or gets information that is to be used later. The returned value can be stored in a variable and used in other parts of the script. For example, you can write a function that checks user details and returns them as an object with the return keyword. The following code block is an example of what returning data looks like in a function: function Get-UserInfo { return [PSCustomObject]@{ UserName = "ahmed" Role = "Admin" } } Let us look at an example of a logging function that builds and returns a custom object. Instead of just printing a message, the function creates a structured log entry that includes the current time, the type of message, and the message content. This makes the log easier to work with later. The log can be exported to a CSV, filtered, or displayed in a table. Refer to the following code for the logging function: function Write-Log { param ( [ string ]$Message, [ string ]$Type = "Info" ) return [PSCustomObject]@{ TimeStamp = Get-Date Type = $Type Message = $Message } } $logEntry = Write-Log -Message "Script started" $logEntry Understanding the PowerShell pipeline One of the most powerful features in PowerShell is the pipeline. It is what allows users to take the output of one command and pass it directly into the next. This is done by using the pipe symbol ( | ), which acts like a connector between commands. Refer to the following example: Get-EventLog -LogName System | Select-Object -First 5 Figure 5.1 shows the output of the preceding command: Figure 5.1 : Output obtained by using a PowerShell pipeline In the preceding example, Get-EventLog pulls all the entries from the system event log. Instead of getting all the entries, we pass that output directly through the pipeline into Select-Object , which outputs the first five entries, shown as follows: Get-LocalUser | Where-Object { $_.Enabled -eq $ true } | Format-Table Name, LastLogon The pipeline in PowerShell is not limited. You can use it as many times as needed in a single line. In the previous example, the pipe is used twice. First, we get all local users, then we filter only the ones that are enabled. Finally, the output is formatted to show just their names and last logon times in a table. This clearly shows how each step builds on the previous one, creating a smooth flow. Another very useful example is piping the output into a file. The following command checks for processes using more than 100 MB of memory and saves the results into a CSV file: Get-Process | Where-Object { $_.WorkingSet -gt 100 MB } | Export-Csv -Path "high-memory-processes.csv" -NoTypeInformation Instead of storing intermediate results in variables or writing loops, the pipeline does all the work in one smooth line. Everything that moves through the pipeline in PowerShell is a full object. This means that the data keeps all its details, such as .Name , .Status , .WorkingSet , and more. You can keep working with these properties at every step without losing any information. When using the pipeline, the result always depends on the last cmdlet. For example, Get-Service -Name spooler gives you the full-service object with many properties. However, if you run Get-Service -Name spooler | Select-Object -Property Name , the output is no longer the full service; it is just an object with the Name property. If you save that result in a variable, only the reduced object is stored. Be careful when working with the pipeline, as it can sometimes reduce readability and make a script harder to follow. Use it thoughtfully, deciding where it truly adds value in automation scripts, and where using clear single line commands is the better choice. PowerShell objects In PowerShell, everything is treated as an object. An object is like a small container that holds all the information about something. For example, when executing a command like Get-Process , each process that shows up in the result is actually an object. That object contains details like the process name, ID, CPU usage, memory, and more. You do not just get raw text back. You get a fully structured object that PowerShell understands. That makes working with data much easier and more flexible. A PowerShell object detailed An object is a data structure that encapsulates data (properties/fields) and behavior (methods/functions) in a single unit. It is an instance of a class or type. In PowerShell specifically: An object is an instance of a .NET class or custom type. It can have: o Properties : named values (e.g., Name , Length ) o Methods : actions the object can perform (e.g., ToString() , Clone() ) Example in PowerShell: $file = Get-Item "C:\example.txt" $file .Name # Property $file .FullName # Property $file .Delete() # Method This $file is an object of type System.IO.FileInfo. Note: PowerShell is object-oriented. Every output is an object, not just text. Object properties and methods To understand a PowerShell object, we can use the command Get-Member . This command may not look very attractive to use in a script, but it is one of the best tools for discovering what is hidden inside an object. Try the following command, and you will see a list of all the properties you can access: $file = Get -Item "C:\PowerShell Workspace Demo\File1.txt" Get - Member -InputObject $file # using a pipeline $file | Get - Member Accessing object values using dot notation To access the properties of an object, we use dot notation. This means you type a dot ( . ) followed by the name of the property you want to access. The following example shows how this works: $user = Get-LocalUser -Name "Administrator" $user.Name $user.Enabled When you check the output, it will show the username Administrator and either true or false, depending on whether the Administrator account is enabled or not. The $user is an object that holds information about the local user. With dot notation, you can get exactly the piece of data you need, without any complicated logic. Example usage of PowerShell objects The following code shows some quick and useful examples that show how object-based output helps in real scripts: # Get all running processes with CPU usage over 100 Get-Process | Where-Object { $_ .CPU - gt 100 } # List services that are currently stopped Get-Service | Where-Object { $_ .Status -eq 'Stopped' } # Show just the names and statuses of the top 5 services Get-Service | Select-Object -First 5 -Property Name, Status # Check for the existence of a specific file in a directory $fileList = Get-ChildItem -Path "C:\PowerShell Workspace Demo" foreach ( $item in $fileList ) { if ( $item .Name -eq "File1.txt" ) { Write-Host "Found File1.txt" } } In all of these examples, you are working with structured data that PowerShell provides as objects. Each object contains specific properties that can easily be accessed and utilized in scripts. This makes it simple to filter, sort, or display exactly the information you need without extra steps. PowerShell custom objects PowerShell’s ability to create custom objects gives complete control over how users store and structure data. Instead of just working with the default output from commands, you can build your own objects with exactly the information you need. This is very helpful when you want your script to return clean and well-organized data. Custom objects are especially useful when complex data needs to be represented in a way that makes sense for the scripts. For example, if you are collecting information about users, services, or files, you can choose which properties to include and how they are displayed. You can also rename the properties to make them more user-friendly. By creating custom objects, you can group different types of information into one single result. This makes it easier to export the data, log it, or send it to another system. You can also sort, filter, or export custom objects to CSV, JSON, or HTML reports. In this section, we will explore how to create, manipulate, and manage custom objects in PowerShell. Creating custom objects in PowerShell The simplest way to create a custom object in PowerShell is by using the [PSCustomObject] type. This is done by using the @{} syntax, where you define key-value pairs inside a hash table. The following code is an example of how a PSCustomObject is created: $userObject = [PSCustomObject]@{ UserName = "ahmed" LoginTime = (Get-Date) Status = "Active" } # Accessing the properties $userObject.UserName # ahmed $userObject.LoginTime # current date and time $userObject.Status # Active In this example, an object has been created with the following three properties, which are UserName , LoginTime , and Status . These properties can be accessed by using the dot notation. The biggest benefit of this method is readability. One way to create a custom object in PowerShell is by using New-Object together with Add-Member . This method allows you to build the object step-by-step by adding each property manually. It gives you full control over how the object is built and what it contains. In the following example, you can see how a custom object is created: $person = New-Object -TypeName PSObject $person | Add-Member -MemberType NoteProperty -Name "FirstName" -Value "Ahmed" $person | Add-Member -MemberType NoteProperty -Name "LastName" -Value "Uzejnovic" $person | Add-Member -MemberType NoteProperty -Name "Age" -Value 30 Another simple way to create a custom object is by using Select-Object . This method is useful when you already have data from a command and want to select only specific properties or rename them to fit your needs. It is quick and often used for building cleaner output or reports. Refer to the following code: $ object = "" | Select-Object FirstName, LastName, Age $ object .FirstName = "Ahmed" $ object .LastName = "Uzejnovic" $ object .Age = 30 If you want to keep the order of properties in your object (like for reports), use the [ordered] type, as shown: $data = [ordered]@{ Name = "Service1" Status = "Running" Time = Get-Date } $ object = [PSCustomObject]$data Adding properties to custom objects One powerful feature of objects and custom objects is the ability to add new properties after the object has been created. When you create an object using [PSCustomObject] , you cannot add new properties to it later using dot notation. However, if you still want to add more properties after it is created, you can do so using Add-Member . You can add a property dynamically to a custom object using the following code: $userObject | Add-Member -MemberType NoteProperty -Name "Email" -Value "ahmed@example.com" # Now the object has a new Email property $userObject.Email # ahmed@example.com The Add-Member cmdlet allows you to add new properties (for instance, NoteProperty , as shown in the preceding example) to an existing object. You can also add methods or other types of members (like script properties) using the Add-Member approach. Modifying properties of custom objects The value of an existing property can be modified directly using dot notation. It is especially useful when information in an object needs to be updated based on new input, calculations, or logic in the script. In the following example, you start with an object where a user is marked as Active , but later in the script, you check their status and want to update it to Inactive . Instead of creating a new object, the value of that property can be changed as follows: $userObject.Status = "Inactive" # Now the Status property is updated $userObject.Status # Inactive There is also a second way to modify properties by creating a more dynamic script. Consider the example of a larger script where you do not know which property needs to be updated ahead of time. Maybe you are looping through multiple keys, or the property name comes from a user input. In the following example, we are updating the object $task : # Create a custom object representing a task $task = [PSCustomObject]@{ Name = "BackupJob" Status = "Running" Message = "Backup started." } # Now we want to update the property based on some input $propertyName = "Status" $newValue = "Completed" # Update the property dynamically $task.PSObject.Properties[$propertyName].Value = $newValue # Output the result $task Removing properties from custom objects Sometimes, you want to remove a property from a custom object. This can happen if the property is no longer needed or should not be included in the final output. While PowerShell does not let you remove properties from all object types, you can remove properties that were added dynamically. If the object was created using New-Object and properties were added afterward, those properties can be removed using the .PSObject.Properties.Remove() method: # Remove the "Email" property $userObject.PSObject.Properties.Remove( "Email" ) # Check the object again $userObject This will completely remove the Email property from $userObject . If you try to access $userObject.Email after it is removed, it will no longer exist and will not return any value. Creating custom objects in loops A common use case for custom objects is creating multiple objects in a loop. An example use case would be when you want to create a collection of user objects from a list of data. The following code shows how to build a custom object for each user in a loop: $users = @() #sample data $usersData = @( @{UserName = "user1" ; Status = "Active" }, @{UserName = "user2" ; Status = "Inactive" }, @{UserName = "user3" ; Status = "Active" } ) foreach ($user in $usersData) { $userObject = [PSCustomObject]@{ UserName = $user.UserName Status = $user.Status LoginTime = (Get-Date) } $users += $userObject } $users | Format-List In the preceding example, a custom object is created for each user, and the objects are collected in an array ( $users ). The result is a list of user objects, each with its own UserName , Status , and LoginTime . This output can then be formatted into a table or list for easy reading. Working with object properties Once we have learned that PowerShell objects hold all the data needed, we can start exploring how to work with the object properties. Every object has a set of properties and methods. These are the details or data points and functions/methods stored inside the object. Working with object properties lets you take full control of the data. You can sort, filter, or group objects based on the values inside them. For example, you might want to find all services that are currently stopped, or list all users that belong to a certain department. Once you understand how to read and use these properties, your scripts will become more powerful and much more flexible. This is one of the most important skills when working with PowerShell. Let us say you only want to see the names of all services. You do not need to write a complex script. PowerShell lets you extract just the property you want by using Select-Object . An example is shown as follows: Get-Service | Select-Object -Property Name This command will output a list showing only the service names. If you want multiple properties, you can just add them with the following code: Get-Service | Select-Object -Property Name, Status This will give you a clean table showing the name and the status of each service. The following figure displays the output of the previous command: Figure 5.2 : Output showing the status of each service You can also filter the results based on a specific property. This is done with the Where-Object command. The following is an example to list only services that are currently running: $serviceList = Get-Service foreach ( $service in $serviceList ) { if ( $service .Status -eq "Running" ) { $service } } The $_ symbol represents each object coming through the pipeline. In this case, we are checking if the Status property is equal to Running . Use Sort-Object if it is necessary to sort the data. For example, to sort services by their name, run the following code: Get-Service | Sort-Object Name To format the output, PowerShell offers various formatting commands, such as Format-Table and Format-List . These are useful when you want to display the data in a specific way in the console. An example is shown in the code as follows: Get-Service | Format-List Name, Status Get-Service | Format-Table Name, Status Run the preceding two lines of code and compare the output. In many cases, it is easier to read, especially when working with many properties. PowerShell gives you full control over working with object properties. Users can display exactly what is needed, filter by any value, sort the results, and format everything for a clean output. Advanced features of functions Using param() allows users to give structure to a function. Each parameter becomes clearly defined, and users will know exactly what kind of input the function needs. Data types like [string] , [int] , or [bool] can be used to ensure that the input is the correct type. This helps catch errors early and makes scripts more reliable. The param() block is especially useful in larger scripts or shared tools where clear, consistent input is important. Adding validation Sometimes, you want to control what values a user can pass to a function, which can be done using the [ValidateSet()] attribute. The attribute allows only specific, pre-approved values to be used as input. If someone tries to pass a value that is not in the list, PowerShell will show an error. This is useful for things like environments, statuses, or actions where only a few values make sense. For example, if you want the user to choose between Dev , Test , or Prod , you can use [ValidateSet()] attribute to enforce this. It is a great way to reduce user mistakes and make your functions safer to use. The following code used [ValidateSet()] attribute in the Set-Environment function: function Set-Environment { param ( [ ValidateSet( "Dev" , "Test" , "Prod" ) ] [ string ]$Environment ) Write-Output "You selected: $Environment" } #This will work Set-Environment -Environment "Dev" #This will throw an error Set-Environment -Environment "Custom" Figure 5.3 shows the error that appears when a value passed is not a part of the allowed list. This validation helps users to avoid unexpected results and ensures the function is used the way it was designed. Figure 5.3 : Output showing a validation error Validating input with regular expressions [ValidatePattern()] allows users to define a regular expression ( regex ) pattern that the input must match. If the value does not match the pattern, PowerShell will show an error before the function runs. In the following example, only usernames with 3 to 15 characters, consisting of letters, numbers, or underscores, are allowed, and any other characters will cause an error: function Set-Username { param ( [ ValidatePattern( "^[a-zA-Z0-9_]{3,15}$" ) ] [ string ]$UserName ) Write-Output "Username '$UserName' is accepted." } Set-Username -UserName "admin_user" # Valid Set-Username -UserName "user@!" # Invalid – will throw an error The following figure shows the error that appears when the validation pattern does not match: Figure 5.4 : Validation pattern error in PowerShell Validating input with validation script If you need even more control over the validation logic, PowerShell allows you to use [ValidateScript()] . This runs a custom script block to check the value. If the script block returns $true , the input is accepted. If it returns $false , PowerShell throws an error. This is helpful when your checks are too complex for [ValidateSet()] or [ValidatePattern()] , such as when checking if a file exists or if a number is in a certain range. The function in the following example only allows values between 18 and 99 , while other values outside this range will be rejected: function Set-Age { param ( [ ValidateScript({ $_ -ge 18 -and $_ -le 99 }) ] [ int ]$Age ) Write-Output "Age '$Age' is accepted." } Set-Age -Age 25 # Valid Set-Age -Age 12 # Invalid – will throw an error The following figure shows the error that appears when the validation script returns $false : Figure 5.5 : PowerShell function validation error Validating input with validate range [ValidateRange()] makes sure a number stays within a set range. If the value is too high or too low, PowerShell stops the script and shows an error. Refer to the following code for an example: function Set-VolumeLevel { param ( [ ValidateRange(0, 100) ] [ int ]$Level ) Write-Output "Volume set to $Level%" } Set-VolumeLevel -Level 80 # Valid Set-VolumeLevel -Level 120 # Invalid The error that appears when the validation range is not in range is shown as follows: Figure 5.6 : Validation error displayed when the parameter value is outside the allowed range Validating input with validation length Use [ValidateLength()] to limit the length of a string value. It is great for things like usernames, passwords, or descriptions. Refer to the following code which is an example of the [ValidateLength()] attribute: function Set-Username { param ( [ ValidateLength(3, 12) ] [ string ]$User ) Write-Output "Username '$User' accepted." } Set-Username -User "Admin" # Valid Set-Username -User "A" # Invalid The error that appears when the validation length is too short is shown as follows: Figure 5.7 : Validation error displayed when the parameter length does not meet the minimum requirement Validating input with validation count If the function takes a list of values, it is possible to limit how many items are allowed using [ValidateCount()] , shown as follows: function Set-TrustedDevices { param ( [ ValidateCount(1, 3) ] [ string[ ]]$Devices ) Write-Output "You set $($Devices.Count) trusted device(s)." } Set-TrustedDevices -Devices "Laptop" , "Phone" # Valid Set-TrustedDevices -Devices @() # Invalid Set-TrustedDevices -Devices "Laptop" , "Phone" , "Tablet" , "Desktop" # Invalid The following figure shows the error that appears when the validation count does not fit: Figure 5.8 : Validation error displayed when the parameter count exceeds the allowed limit Note: All these validations make functions safer and help other users to use them the right way. They act like guardrails, making sure only the correct and expected input is allowed. This reduces the chance of errors, protects against bad data, and helps guide users to use the function exactly as designed. By adding these checks, scripts can be made more stable, easier to troubleshoot, and much more user-friendly. Turn a function into an advanced function Adding [CmdletBinding()] above your param() block converts the normal PowerShell function into an advanced function. This gives the function extra features, such as built-in PowerShell cmdlets. You can use -Verbose to show detailed messages that explain what the function is doing internally. If you include lines like Write-Verbose "Doing something" , this message will only be shown if the function is run with the -Verbose switch. An example is shown as follows: Restart-MyService -ServiceName "Spooler" -Verbose The -Debug switch works similarly, allowing you to show special debug output with Write-Debug . This is useful for development and testing. The -ErrorAction switch lets users choose how they want to handle errors. For example, they can stop execution on error or silently continue, shown as follows: Restart-MyService -ServiceName "Spooler" -ErrorAction Stop You can also support -WhatIf and -Confirm if you add logic using SupportsShouldProcess . These are useful for critical actions, like deleting files or restarting services. -WhatIf simulates the action without making changes, and -Confirm asks before continuing. An example of -WhatIf is shown as follows: Restart-MyService -ServiceName "Spooler" -WhatIf The following code shows the function used for the preceding examples: function Restart-MyService { [ CmdletBinding(SupportsShouldProcess = $true,ConfirmImpact=’High’) ] param ( [ Parameter(Mandatory) ] [ string ]$ServiceName ) if ($PSCmdlet.ShouldProcess( "Service: $ServiceName" , "Restart" )) { Write-Verbose "Restarting $ServiceName..." Restart-Service -Name $ServiceName -Force } } The preceding function includes support for -WhatIf and -Confirm through SupportsShouldProcess , making it fully capable of handling all advanced parameters like -Verbose , -ErrorAction , -Debug , -WhatIf , and -Confirm . Use of parameter sets Parameter sets allow users to define different combinations of input for one function. This is useful when a function can be used in different ways depending on the scenario. Each parameter set defines a group of parameters that can be used together. When a user calls the function, PowerShell automatically figures out which set is being used based on the parameters provided. This is especially helpful when you want to let users provide input in more than one format, such as by name or by ID, but not both at the same time. It keeps the function flexible while still making sure it behaves in a predictable manner. An example is illustrated in the following code: function Get-UserData { [ CmdletBinding(DefaultParameterSetName = "ByName" ) ] param ( [ Parameter(ParameterSetName = "ByName" , Mandatory) ] [ string ]$UserName, [ Parameter(ParameterSetName = "ById" , Mandatory) ] [ int ]$UserId ) if ($PSCmdlet.ParameterSetName -eq "ByName" ) { Write-Output "Getting user by name: $UserName" } elseif ($PSCmdlet.ParameterSetName -eq "ById" ) { Write-Output "Getting user by ID: $UserId" } } Get-UserData -UserName "ahmed" # Getting user by name: ahmed Get-UserData -UserId 101 # Getting user by ID: 101 Accepting pipeline input Functions can be designed to accept input directly from the pipeline. This is done by using [Parameter(ValueFromPipeline)] and a process {} block. This is a very useful feature because it allows your function to become part of a larger command chain. Instead of having to collect data first and then loop through it manually, PowerShell can pass each item through your function, shown as follows: function Get-UpperCase { param ( [ Parameter(ValueFromPipeline) ] [ string ]$Text ) process { $Text.ToUpper() } } "hello" , "world" | Get-UpperCase Pipeline input makes scripts shorter and, in some cases, more practical. Results can be passed from one command into a function just like with any built-in PowerShell cmdlet. This helps the function integrate naturally into PowerShell workflows, where many users expect to pipe data between commands. However, while scripts may become shorter, they are not always easier to read. In some situations, using a few additional lines can improve readability and provide more flexibility than relying heavily on pipelines. Handling multiple inputs You can handle arrays of values and loop through them inside your function. This is especially useful when a function needs to work on multiple items one-by-one, such as when restarting several services. When a parameter is declared as an array using [string[]] type, PowerShell lets users pass multiple values to it. Inside the function, a foreach loop can be used to go through each value and perform the desired action: 1. function Restart-Services { 2. 3. param ( 4. [ string[ ]]$ServiceNames 5. ) 6. foreach ($name in $ServiceNames) { 7. Restart-Service -Name $name -Force 8. 9. } 10. } 11. 12. Restart-Services -ServiceNames "Spooler" , "wuauserv" Handling errors with try, catch, and finally When running PowerShell scripts, things do not always go as planned. A file might be missing, a command could fail, or the system may return an unexpected result. To make your scripts more reliable and prevent them from stopping suddenly, PowerShell gives you a way to handle these situations using try , catch , and finally . The try block is where you place the code that might fail. If something goes wrong, PowerShell jumps to the catch block. This is where you can write an error message, log the issue, or take action to fix it. The finally block always runs, whether there was an error or not, and is often used to clean up, close files, or display a final message. Refer to the following code: try { Get-Content "C:\MissingFile.txt" } catch { Write-Error "An error occurred. File not found." } finally { Write-Host "Finished running the script." } Note: The try , catch block only works with terminating errors. These are errors that would stop the script from continuing. Most cmdlets throw non-terminating errors by default, which means the script keeps running, and the catch block will not be triggered. To turn a non-terminating error into a terminating one, you can use -ErrorAction Stop . In the following code example, we are using the -ErrorAction parameter, which means that the execution of the script will continue in the catch block: try { Get-Content "C:\MissingFile.txt" -ErrorAction Stop } catch { Write-Error "Now the catch block runs because we used -ErrorAction Stop." } Using try , catch helps make your scripts safer. Instead of letting errors break everything, you can respond to them in a controlled way. This is useful in automations where you want your script to handle problems and continue, rather than fail completely. You can also log the error message inside the catch block using $_.Exception.Message . If you want more detail, you can include the exact line number where the error happened using $_.InvocationInfo.ScriptLineNumber : try { Get-Content "C:\MissingFile.txt" -ErrorAction Stop } catch { Write-Error "Error on line $($_.InvocationInfo.ScriptLineNumber): $($_.Exception.Message)" } The output of the previous sample code is as follows: Figure 5.9 : PowerShell try, catch This makes it much easier to find out what went wrong and where, especially when troubleshooting longer scripts. Instead of guessing which part caused the error, you can see the exact line number and error message right away. This helps you fix issues faster and saves time during testing. In larger scripts or in automation that runs on a schedule, clear error logs are very helpful for tracking down problems without needing to run the script manually. It is a simple but powerful way to make your error handling more useful, professional, and easier to maintain. Organizing functions into modules When your PowerShell scripts get bigger, it becomes harder to manage everything in one file. A good way to keep your scripts clean and reusable is by moving your functions into separate script files or modules. This helps reduce duplicate code and makes things easier to update over time. A script is a .ps1 file that can contain commands or functions. A module is a .psm1 file that is meant to store one or more functions. Modules are helpful when you want to reuse the same functions in different scripts. Let us now create a sample module. Start by creating a new file named PowerShell_Tools.psm1 and write the following sample code into it. This file will act as our PowerShell module: function Get-DiskInfo { Get-PSDrive -PSProvider FileSystem | Select-Object Name, Free, Used, @{Name = "UsedPercent" ; Expression = { [math]::Round(($_.Used / ($_.Used + $_.Free)) * 100 , 2 ) } } } Export-ModuleMember -Function Get-DiskInfo Export-Module Member must reference the function name. This makes the function later in the script available. Now, create a file named MainScript.ps1 and copy the code from the following sample: Import-Module "C:\PowerShell Automation\Powershell Objects.ps1\PowerShell_Tools.psm1" Get-DiskInfo Now, you can see it behaves like a standard module. After importing it, we have access to the Get-DiskInfo command. If we had several functions in this module and exported them, they would also be available. Using modules is great for keeping your code clean and shared, but they also come with responsibility. If multiple scripts use the same module and you change something, like the function name, its output, or how parameters work, it could break the other scripts that depend on it. To avoid this, always take care of the following practices: Always use Export-ModuleMember to control what gets exposed. Version your module if you plan to make changes (e.g., PowerShell_Tools_v2.psm1 ). Test module changes with all affected scripts. Use clear names and comments inside your module. Keep backup copies before updating shared modules. Note: Modules are a deep subject and could fill an entire chapter of their own. In this section, only the basics are covered, focusing on Export-ModuleMember and a simple example. A full treatment of modules would also include file structure conventions, separate public and private functions, and creating a .psd1 manifest. Best practices for functions Following the best practices for functions will help you write cleaner and more maintainable scripts. When you write functions correctly, they become easier to understand, reuse, and share with others. These small changes can make a big difference in the long-term quality of your automation. The best practices are as follows: Keep function names and parameters clear : Use names that clearly describe what the function does. Some examples are as follows: Restart-StoppedServices is the correct way to name the function. The name Do-IT is not the correct name for a function, as it can cause confusion for other users. Clear names make scripts easier to read and help others understand what your function is supposed to do just by looking at its name. Always return objects when possible : Returning structured data makes it easier to work with a function’s output later (like exporting or filtering). Plain text is harder to reuse. Objects give more control and flexibility in what we can do next. Avoid global variables inside functions : Try to keep your function independent by using parameters to pass values and return results. Global variables can lead to hard-to-find bugs and make your script less predictable. Add comments or help to explain functions : Use comment-based help so you and others understand what the function does. This is helpful when you return later to update the script or share it with someone else. You can add help directly inside the function using a special comment block, as shown in the following example: function Test-ServiceStatus { &lt; # .SYNOPSIS This function checks if a Windows service is currently running. .DESCRIPTION It accepts the name of a service and checks if the service is in a 'Running' state. Useful for monitoring scripts or status checks. .PARAMETER Name The name of the Windows service you want to check. .EXAMPLE Test-ServiceStatus -Name "Spooler" This will check if the 'Spooler' service is running. .OUTPUTS Boolean (True or False) .NOTES Author: Ahmed Uzejnovic Created: 2025-03-30 #&gt; param ( [ string ]$Name ) $service = Get-Service -Name $Name -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue if ($service - and $service.Status -eq 'Running' ) { return $ true } return $ false } Get-Help Test-ServiceStatus The cmdlet Get-Help shows the help text written inside the function, making it easier for others (or yourself) to understand how to use it correctly. Validate the function input : Always try to validate function input to reduce errors, improve security, and guide users. Input validation ensures that only expected and correct values are passed into a function. This helps avoid mistakes, protects the logic from bad or harmful input, and makes a function more user-friendly. Using [ValidateSet()] , [ValidatePattern()] , or [ValidateScript()] makes a function safer and easier to use, even by others who did not write the original code. Accept pipeline input : Supporting pipeline input allows a function to work like native PowerShell commands. It helps to process data one item at a time without needing extra loops or logic. This makes functions easier to use in one-liners and automation scripts. It is a must-have when writing clean, flexible, and PowerShell-friendly code. Use [CmdletBinding()] in functions : Adding [ CmdletBinding()] turns a normal function into an advanced function. This enables built-in features, such as -Verbose , -Debug , -ErrorAction , -WhatIf , and -Confirm . These options make a function behave more like native PowerShell cmdlets. It also helps in writing cleaner scripts by allowing better error handling, detailed output for troubleshooting, and safer execution when running important changes. Use Errorhandling : Always include error handling to catch possible issues like missing files, access denied, or failed commands. It helps your script run more reliably and gives you clear messages when something goes wrong. Even a simple try, catch can save time and make troubleshooting much easier. One function for each task : A function should focus on doing one thing well. Adding multiple responsibilities increases the risk of hidden bugs, makes the function harder to test and reuse, and often leads to brittle scripts. Keep each function centered around one clear purpose. Conclusion In this chapter, working with PowerShell objects and functions was covered in detail. It showed how to work with structured data using objects and how to build functions to organize and reuse logic. Features like parameter validation, pipeline input, and advanced function options were also explained to help create scripts that are easier to manage, maintain, and scale. The next chapter moves into practical automation tasks. It starts with managing files and directories, covering how to create, copy, move, and delete files and folders. It also introduces working with attributes, permissions, and advanced file handling using .NET libraries. Real-world examples will help apply the skills from this chapter in everyday IT scenarios.
