PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 6 Managing Files and Directories Introduction Managing files and directories is a fundamental part of any IT environment. Whether working with local systems, network drives, or automation scripts, handling files and folders is something that cannot be avoided. It is one of the core tasks that appear in nearly every project, from setting up user profiles to backing up logs or organizing reports. Therefore, having a consistent way to manage these tasks is critical. In PowerShell, file and directory operations become much easier and faster. Instead of manually navigating through folders, dragging files, or creating structures by hand, PowerShell allows you to automate everything, from simple actions like creating folders and moving files to more complex tasks like managing file permissions and setting specific attributes. This saves time but also reduces errors and ensures you can easily reuse your code as a snippet in future scripts. This chapter focuses on the essential areas you need to know when working with files and directories in PowerShell. First, we will explore creating, copying, moving, and deleting files and folders. Next, the management of file attributes, such as hidden or read-only states, which are often used in automation, will be explored. We will also work with folders and file permissions, something that becomes important in shared or restricted environments. At the end of this chapter, we will look at the use of .NET libraries in PowerShell to perform advanced file operations. This chapter is designed to give readers a foundation for working with file systems in an automated and practical way to apply these skills across daily tasks and projects. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: • Creating files and folders • Writing to files • Copying and moving items • Renaming and deleting files and folders • Managing file attributes • Working with file and folder permissions • Advanced file operations with .NET • Best practices Objectives By the end of the chapter, the readers would be able to manage files and folders in PowerShell. The chapter begins with the basics, such as how to create files and folders in specific locations. They will learn to build folder structures and organize their file system through automation. The chapter will then cover writing content into files and updating or replacing text inside existing files, which is helpful when working with templates or configuration files. Readers will also learn to copy and move items from one place to another, including handling folders, using filters like wildcards, and dealing with naming conflicts or file overwrites. The chapter explores renaming files and folders and deleting them safely. The readers will also explore how to check if a file is hidden, read-only, or marked with other attributes, and change those properties using PowerShell. They will learn how to view and manage file and folder access. Finally, the chapter will introduce advanced file handling using .NET, and they will learn to work with file streams, collect detailed file information, and understand when .NET methods provide more flexibility than the PowerShell commands. By the end of this chapter, they will have a complete toolkit to manage files and directories efficiently and to automate these tasks as part of their daily workflows. Creating files and folders Creating files and folders is one of the first tasks most people do when learning PowerShell. Whether you are setting up a folder structure for a project or creating a log file using a script, PowerShell makes this easy with the New-Item cmdlet. Using New-Item to create files and directories The New-Item cmdlet is used to create files and folders. It takes a path, an ItemType (file or directory), and optionally, some content. To create a new file, use the following code: New-Item -Path "C:\PowerShell Automation\log.txt" -ItemType File If successfully executed, the file is created, and the details are shown in the output. You can see the output in the following figure: Figure 6.1 : Creating a new file using PowerShell Use the following command to create a new folder: New-Item -Path "C:\PowerShell Automation\Logs" -ItemType Directory Refer to the following figure, which also depicts the way to create a new folder: Figure 6.2 : Creating a new folder using PowerShell Choosing file types PowerShell lets you choose the file type simply by changing the file extension in the path. You can use the following extensions for choosing the file types: .txt for text files. .log for logs or output files. .ps1 for PowerShell scripts. The following code illustrates an example: New-Item -Path "C:\PowerShell Automation\Logs\script.ps1" -ItemType File A blank PowerShell file has been created and is ready to use. So, you can create all file extensions you can imagine using PowerShell. Creating folders in specific paths If you have permissions, you can create as many files and folders as you want. There is no limit to how many files and folders you can create; however, take care of the default character path on Windows systems, which is 260 characters. It is important to check if the folder exists before creating it. Note: Always check before creating a folder if the folder exists or not. If the folder already exists, the following error message will be shown on the screen: Figure 6.3 : Error message showing that a folder already exists The following code is an example that shows how to check if a specific folder already exists; in case the folder does not exist, the New-Item cmdlet will create the folder: if (- not (Test-Path -Path "C:\PowerShell Automation\Logs" )) { New-Item -Path "C:\PowerShell Automation\Logs" -ItemType Directory } Using the -Force parameter The New-Item cmdlet is smart enough to prevent accidental overwriting. If you try to create a file or folder that already exists, PowerShell will return an error, as shown in Figure 6.3 . This behavior is helpful because it protects existing items from being overwritten by mistake. However, in many situations, you may want to overwrite an existing file or folder on purpose. For example, when re-running a script that needs to recreate the same structure or log file. In such cases, you can use the -Force parameter. On adding the -Force parameter, PowerShell suppresses the error message and creates the file or folder even if it already exists. No warning is shown, and no confirmation is needed. The item is simply created or replaced silently, which makes it perfect for automation scenarios where the script should continue without interruptions. Refer to the following command for an example: New-Item -Path "C:\PowerShell Automation\log.txt" -ItemType File -Force Best practices for organizing folder layouts Keeping the folder structure clean and consistent is important, especially when working with automation. The following best practices will help you stay organized and avoid confusion as your project grows: Use clear and consistent folder names (e.g., Logs , Scripts , Backups ). Include timestamps or dates in folder names if used for logs or reports. Avoid spaces in folder names to reduce path errors in scripts. Group folders by purpose ( C:\Automation\Inputs , C:\Automation\Outputs ). Use variables to store common paths in your scripts for easier reuse. The following example shows how to use a variable to check if a folder already exists. The same variable is then reused to create the folder if it does not exist. This makes the script easier to read and manage. $logFolder = "C:\PowerShell Automation\Logs" if (- not (Test-Path $ logFolder)) { New-Item -Path $ logFolder -ItemType Directory } Writing to files PowerShell not only helps you manage files and folders but also lets you easily write and update the content inside those files. Whether you are logging output, building reports, or updating configuration files, PowerShell provides simple and flexible ways to work with file content. Writing content with Set-Content The Set-Content cmdlet, as shown in the following code, is used when you want to overwrite the content of a file completely, so if the file already has content, it will be replaced without warning: $logFolder = "C:\PowerShell Automation\Logs" Set-Content -Path "$logFolder\DailyReport.txt" -Value "Task completed successfully." If the file does not exist, it will be created automatically. Appending text using Add-Content If you want to add text to an existing file without removing the old content, use Add-Content . This is great for logs or when you want to build a file line-by-line. The following is an example of the same: Add-Content -Path "$logFolder\DailyReport.txt" -Value "Finished step 2 at $(Get-Date)" Check the created file. You should see two entries, shown as follows: Figure 6.4 : Output of Add-Content Saving command output to files You can also write the output of commands directly into files. This is useful for saving results, logs, or even exporting data from a script. The following command will help you to save the output: Get-Process | Out-File -FilePath "$logFolder\RunningProcesses.txt" The following figure illustrates the contents of the RunningProcesses file after saving the output of the Get-Process command into it: Figure 6.5 : Contents of the RunningProcesses file Replacing text in files Sometimes, users need to change part of the content inside a file, such as updating a username, a path, or a configuration value. This can be done by reading the content, modifying it, and saving it back. For the next example, we will create an INI file and read the file with Get-Content . Next, we need to use -replace to modify the text. Lastly, we will use Set-Content to write back the modified text, as shown in the following code: @" [General] AppName=PowerScript Version=1.0 EnableLogging=true [User] username=olduser Language=en-US Theme=Dark "@ | Set-Content -Path "$logFolder\Settings.ini" -force $content = Get-Content -Path "$logFolder\Settings.ini" $content = $content -replace "username=olduser" , "username=newuser" Set-Content -Path "$logFolder\Settings.ini" -Value $content Writing to files is very common in daily tasks. Some examples are as follows: Creating log files during script execution. Storing user or system data in a structured format. Updating configuration templates before deployment. Saving results from scheduled tasks or jobs. Combining Set-Content , Add-Content , and Get-Content allows users to handle almost any file-writing task using just a few lines of PowerShell. Copying and moving items Copying and moving files or folders are common tasks. PowerShell makes this simple with two main cmdlets: Copy-Item and Move-Item . These commands help in organizing files, backing up data, or shifting things from one location to another without opening File Explorer . Copy-Item vs. Move-Item Copy-Item creates a copy of the file or folder in the new location, while the original stays where it is. On the other hand, Move-Item moves the item to a new location and removes it from the original place. Consider the following examples: Copy-Item -Path "$logFolder\DailyReport.txt" -Destination "$logFolder\Destination" Move-Item -Path "$logFolder\RunningProcesses.txt" -Destination "$logFolder\Destination" Use Copy-Item when you need to make backups or duplicate files. Use Move-Item when you want to clean up or relocate files as part of a process. Copying files and folders with all contents To copy an entire folder and everything inside it, the -Recurse parameter should be added, shown as follows: Copy-Item -Path "C:\PowerShell Automation" -Destination "C:\PowerShell Automation Backup" -Recurse This copies all subfolders and files from C:\PowerShell Automation to C:\PowerShell Automation Backup . Note: Without -Recurse , only the top-level folder is copied without its content. The following list shows the use cases of the -Recurse parameter: Backups : It ensures the full folder structure is copied. Deployments : The parameter moves all required files and subdirectories between environments. Log archiving : It allows users to capture all log files, even in nested folders. Shared folders : This prevents data from going missing when copying team or user folders. Using wildcards to filter files PowerShell supports wildcards to filter files. This is useful when moving or copying only specific file types. The following code allows you to copy all files with the extension .txt : Copy-Item -Path "$logFolder\*.txt" -Destination "$logFolder\Destination" To move files where the name begins with an error, execute the following code: Move-Item -Path "$logfolder\error*.log" -Destination "$logfolder\Destination" Moving items across drives or networks Files can not only be moved between folders on the same drive but also moved or copied across different drives and network locations. This is especially useful when working in larger environments where data needs to be transferred between systems, servers, or shared locations. You can use existing file shares or create a folder and share it for testing using the following code: Move-Item -Path "$logFolder\source\DailyReport.txt" -Destination "\\WINDEV2407EVAL\Fileshare" Moving and copying files is a key part of many PowerShell scripts. It helps automate cleanup processes, archive logs, transfer reports, or manage user data. With just a few lines of code, PowerShell gives full control over file operations across the entire network. Renaming and deleting files and folders Managing file and folder names or cleaning up unnecessary data is part of almost every script. PowerShell gives you full control with simple commands to rename or delete items safely and efficiently. Renaming files and folders Any file or folder can be renamed using Rename-Item by providing the current path of the file or folder, along with the new name. Refer to the following code for an example: Rename-Item -Path "$logFolder\oldname.txt" -NewName "newname.txt" The aforementioned command keeps the file in the same folder but changes its name. Folders can also be renamed the same way, using the following code: Rename-Item -Path "$logFolder\Destination" -NewName "Renamed_Destination" This is very useful when archiving, cleaning up, or reorganizing structures. Deleting items with Remove-Item To delete files or folders, use Remove-Item . For example, to delete a single file or folder, use the following code: Remove-Item -Path "$logFolder\Renamed_Destination\Summary.txt" Remove-Item -Path "$logFolder\Renamed_Destination" If the folder that is to be removed contains files, -Recurse must be added to the command. If -Recurse is not added, PowerShell will block the action and will ask if you want to delete all files and subfolders too. In the next code sample, we are using -Force to ensure the folder and its contents are deleted without any confirmation prompts: Remove-Item -Path "$logFolder\Renamed_Destination" -Force Best practices to avoid accidental deletion Deleting files and folders can be risky. To avoid accidental data loss, keep the following best practices in mind: Always check the file or folder paths before using Remove-Item . Use -WhatIf to test the delete actions safely. Avoid using Remove-Item with wildcards ( * ) unless absolutely sure. Consider moving files to a temporary recycle folder first, then deleting them later. Add logging to track what was deleted during automation. Note: Renaming and deleting files are simple tasks, but they can lead to problems if used carelessly. PowerShell gives you powerful tools, but using them carefully keeps your data safe. Managing file attributes Every file and folder on a system has attributes that define its behavior. Attributes like Hidden or ReadOnly are commonly used when organizing data or protecting files from accidental edits. PowerShell allows users to view and change these attributes easily using simple commands. Attributes are a flag set on a file or folder that affects the way it is handled by the system. The most common ones are as follows: ReadOnly : File cannot be modified. Hidden : File or folder is hidden from normal view. System : Marks it as a system file (use with care). To view all available attributes, use the following command: [ System.Io.FileAttributes ] | Get-Member -Static -MemberType property The output of the preceding command is shown in Figure 6.6 : Figure 6.6 : Attributes of all files The attributes can be viewed and changed using the .Attributes property in PowerShell. Viewing attributes using .Attributes Use the following command to check the attributes of a file or folder, use Get-Item and the property .Attributes : (Get-Item -Path "$logFolder\script.ps1" ).Attributes The command in the aforementioned example will show the value Archive . Changing and removing attributes To change or add attributes, assign them using the Set-ItemProperty or directly modify .Attributes . The following code changes the file attribute to ReadOnly : (Get-Item -Path "$logFolder\script.ps1" ).Attributes = "ReadOnly" Checking the attributes after executing the preceding command will show that .Attributes has changed to ReadOnly . Another way to remove or reset the attributes is shown as follows: (Get-Item -Path "$logFolder\script.ps1" -Force).Attributes = "" The preceding code will change the attribute to Normal . The following code will set the attribute to Hidden : (Get-Item -Path "$logFolder\script.ps1" ).Attributes += "Hidden" After the attribute is set to hidden, on using .Attributes , an error message will show up, as shown in the following figure: Figure 6.7 : Error message shown by PowerShell on using .Attribute To use Get-Item for hidden files, use the -Force parameter: (Get-Item -Path "$logFolder\script.ps1" -Force).Attributes The output of the preceding command should contain ReadOnly and Hidden . That means that the file is not marked as ReadOnly and is hidden. Note: If you want to read hidden files with PowerShell, always use -Force . Use cases in automation Setting and checking file attributes can be very helpful in scripts. The following list enumerates a few automation cases: Marking files as ReadOnly after generating reports. Filtering files based on attributes before processing them. Skipping hidden files during automation tasks. Working with file and folder permissions Managing file and folder permissions is important when working in shared environments or when your scripts create content that should only be accessed by certain users or services. PowerShell lets you view and modify these permissions using access control lists ( ACLs ). Basic file and folder permissions in PowerShell Permissions control who can read, write, or modify a file or folder. These are managed through access control entries ( ACEs ), which are stored in an ACL. Each entry defines what a user or group is allowed or not allowed to do. PowerShell can read and modify these permissions using Get-Acl and Set-Acl . Viewing existing permissions using Get-Acl We can check the current permissions on a file or folder using the following code: Get-Acl -Path "$logFolder" | select owner, AccessToString | fl The following figure shows the output of the aforementioned command: Figure 6.8 : Output of the Get-Acl command The preceding figure shows the owner of the item and also the access permissions for other users or groups. Modifying access with Set-Acl To change permissions on a file or folder using PowerShell, the first step is to retrieve the current ACL with the Get-Acl cmdlet. Next, a new permission rule is created using the [System.Security.AccessControl.FileSystemAccessRule] class, where the user, the type of access (such as Read or Modify ), and how it should be applied are defined. Once the rule is created, it can be added to the existing ACL object. Finally, the updated permissions can be applied to the file or folder using Set-Acl . Consider a scenario where you are running a script that creates daily logs. You want the log folder to exist and to give write access only to a specific user account (such as a service account or automation user). To do so, execute the following code: $path = "C:\PowerShell Automation\Logs" $user = "windev2407eval\user" # Create the folder if it does not exist if (- not (Test-Path $path )) { New-Item -Path $path -ItemType Directory } # Get current ACL $acl = Get- Acl $path # Create new access rule $accessRule = New-Object System.Security.AccessControl.FileSystemAccessRule( $user, "Modify" , "ContainerInherit,ObjectInherit" , "None" , "Allow" ) # Add the rule and apply it $acl.AddAccessRule($accessRule) Set-Acl -Path $path - AclObject $ acl The following figure shows the newly added access permissions: Figure 6.9 : Updated access permissions Managing and architecting permissions is not always straightforward, especially in Windows environments where several access layers and inheritance rules overlap. If the concept of permissions is new, it is recommended to review the fundamentals first, as a clear understanding is essential before automating or applying complex permission structures. Advanced file operations with .NET While PowerShell cmdlets like Get-Item , Copy-Item , and Remove-Item cover most file tasks, sometimes, users need more control. As PowerShell is built on .NET, .NET classes can be used to perform advanced file operations directly, giving more flexibility and access to features that standard cmdlets may not support. Using .NET in PowerShell is helpful in the following cases: Working with file properties, such as size or timestamps, in detail. Using file streams for reading or writing large files. For faster performance or more precision on complex tasks. When PowerShell cmdlets do not provide or support the operations users need to execute. Working with [System.IO] The [System.IO.File] and [System.IO.FileInfo] classes allow users to work with files just like in C#. The following code gives access to detailed information about the file, such as file size (in bytes), the date of creation, and the file type: $file = New-Object System.IO.FileInfo "$logFolder\script.ps1" $file.Name $file.Length $file.CreationTime $file.Extension Reading and writing files using file streams With .NET, users can open a file as a stream and read or write to it in a more controlled way. The following code allows users to read all text from a file: $filecontent = [System.IO.File]::ReadAllText( "C:\PowerShell Automation\Logs\Settings.ini" ) Next, to write this text to another file, use the following command. Here, the `n indicates only a new line, and line breaks are added to improve the readability of the file output: [ System.IO.File ]::WriteAllText( "C:\PowerShell Automation\Logs\PSFile.txt" , "This text was added by .NET-powered file writing `n $filecontent." ) The following .NET operations can also be used: ReadAllLines() gets the file as an array of lines. AppendAllText() adds content to an existing file. Copy() and Delete() allow users to manage files. PowerShell cmdlets vs .NET The following table shows a comparison of the PowerShell cmdlet and the .NET activity: Cmdlet .NET Get-Content [System.IO.File]::ReadAllText() Set-Content [System.IO.File]::WriteAllText() Add-Content [System.IO.File]::AppendAllText() Copy-Item [System.IO.File]::Copy() Remove-Item [System.IO.File]::Delete() Table 6.1 : Comparison of PowerShell cmdlets and .NET PowerShell cmdlets must always be used over .NET functionality when the former allows you to perform the required tasks. This is because PowerShell cmdlets are easier to read, quicker to write, and usually enough for most file tasks. Cmdlets like Get-Content , Copy-Item , and Remove-Item are designed to be simple to execute. However, if more control is required, such as when working with file size, reading specific lines, or handling very large files, then using .NET can be the better choice. .NET has more detailed options and can be faster for some tasks. It is also useful when PowerShell does not have a direct cmdlet for the required task. Note: So, cmdlets can be used for everyday file work. Use .NET only when you need more advanced features and better performance. Best practices When working with files and folders in PowerShell, a small mistake can lead to unexpected results or even data loss. To keep your scripts safe, efficient, and easier to manage, it is important to follow some best practices. The following tips will help you write cleaner code and avoid common pitfalls: Use full paths in scripts to avoid mistakes, especially when deleting or moving files. Always test actions like Remove-Item with -WhatIf first to prevent accidental data loss. Check if paths exist before working with them using Test-Path . Use variables for paths and filenames to make the code easier to update and reuse. Organize folders with a clear structure, especially for logs, reports, or backups. This makes automation easier to manage. Use PowerShell cmdlets first and switch to .NET when you need more flexibility or performance. Add comments to scripts, especially when working with permissions or complex file logic. This helps you and others understand the script later. Conclusion This chapter focused on the management of files and folders using PowerShell, from the basics to more advanced techniques. The tasks covered in the chapter are common tasks in almost every environment, and PowerShell makes them easy to automate. We started by creating files and folders using New-Item, including how to work with different file types and folder structures. Then we covered writing content to files using Set-Content, Add-Content, and how to replace text inside files, which is helpful when working with templates, logs, or reports. Next, the chapter covered the copying and moving of files with Copy-Item and Move-Item, including working with wildcards, folders, and even across network paths. After that, we looked at renaming and deleting items. Managing file attributes, such as checking if a file is hidden or read-only and changing settings when needed, was also covered. Readers explored setting and modifying file and folder permissions using Get-Acl and Set-Acl. Finally, we looked at using .NET for advanced file operations. This gives more control when PowerShell cmdlets are not enough, especially when working with large files or when better performance is needed. The next chapter will focus on managing Active Directory using PowerShell, including creating and managing users, resetting passwords, updating group memberships, and searching for objects like users or computers, all from the command line. The chapter will cover tools to automate daily admin tasks, reduce manual work, and bring more consistency to the environment. Whether working with a few users or thousands of users, this knowledge will help manage Active Directory efficiently. PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 7 Managing Active Directory with PowerShell Introduction Active Directory ( AD ) is one of the most important parts of modern IT environments. It controls user logins, group memberships, access rights, and many security settings. AD acts as a central place where all users, computers, groups, and other resources are managed. Without it, handling permissions or applying consistent rules across a company would be messy and hard to maintain. In most environments, AD is used daily. Creating users, resetting passwords, assigning group access, or disabling inactive accounts are tasks every admin knows. Doing these things manually through a graphical interface may work for small setups, but it becomes slow and inefficient in larger environments. That is where PowerShell becomes extremely helpful. PowerShell lets you automate AD tasks, saving a lot of time and avoiding manual mistakes. AD allows users to create hundreds of users in seconds, update attributes across the whole company, or find and clean up unused accounts with just a few lines of code. These tasks become repeatable, traceable, and easy to adjust later if something changes. On top of that, PowerShell gives you full control. Every change is done through scripts, which means you always know what is happening. You can run checks before making changes, log the results, and be sure that every step is done the same each time. Managing a small domain or a complex enterprise setup, using PowerShell with AD is one of the best ways to work faster, safer, and more consistently. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Setting up the Active Directory module Creating and managing user accounts Updating user attributes Managing group membership Searching for users and computers Working with organizational units Managing permissions in Active Directory Objectives This chapter will help the readers to manage AD using PowerShell in a fast, simple, and reliable way. It starts with setting up the AD module and checking that everything is working. From there, they will learn how to create users, update their properties, and reset passwords without having to click through the graphical interface. Readers will also learn to handle user group memberships. This includes checking which groups a user belongs to, listing all members of a group, and adding or removing users from groups using just a few lines of code. These are everyday tasks that become much easier and faster when automated. Another important part of this chapter is working with organizational units ( OUs ), which will help them keep their AD structure clean and organized. They will learn to move users between OUs and keep things structured in a smart way. Searching in AD is also covered in detail. Readers will learn how to filter results using PowerShell's built-in -Filter parameter. By the end of this chapter, they will learn to automate common AD tasks, avoid mistakes, and work more efficiently with the AD module and Lightweight Directory Access Protocol ( LDAP ) queries. Setting up the Active Directory module To manage AD with PowerShell, a few prerequisites must be in place. The system must be a domain controller or a domain-joined Windows machine within an environment where AD is already installed and configured. For PowerShell-based management, the Remote Server Administration Tools ( RSAT ) module is required. This section explains the process to install RSAT, import the AD module, verify that it is available, and confirm the connection to the domain. Once these steps are completed, the environment is ready for managing AD through PowerShell. Installing the RSAT tools On Windows 10 and Windows 11, the RSAT module is included as an optional feature and can be installed using PowerShell. Use the following sample command to install RSAT tools: Add -WindowsCapability -Online -Name "Rsat.ActiveDirectory.DS-LDS.Tools~~~~0.0.1.0" The following figure shows the result after successful installation: Figure 7.1 : Managing AD For Windows Server systems, RSAT can be installed using the following command: Install-WindowsFeature -Name "RSAT-AD-PowerShell" The following figure shows the result after installing the RSAT tools on Windows Server 2025: Figure 7.2 : Successful installation of RSAT tools on Windows Server After the successful installation of the RSAT tools, we can now check if our Module is ready to use with the Import-Module ActiveDirectory command. Importing the Active Directory module To import the AD module, run the following command: Import- Module ActiveDirectory After running the Import-Module ActiveDirectory command, run Get-Module -Name ActiveDirectory to check if the module is imported correctly: Get - Module -Name ActiveDirectory If the module appears in the output, as shown in the following figure, it has been successfully imported: Figure 7.3 : Successful import of AD module Most common Active Directory commands The AD module includes a large number of cmdlets. The following command lists all of them: Get -Command - Module ActiveDirectory The list is big, but only a handful of cmdlets are commonly used in day-to-day administration. The following table has a list of frequently used commands, which gives you an idea of the possibilities of using the AD PowerShell module: Cmdlet Description Get-ADUser Retrieves user account information New-ADUser Creates a new user account Set-ADUser Updates user properties Enable-ADAccount Enables a user account Disable-ADAccount Disables a user account Add-ADGroupMember Adds a user to a group Remove-ADGroupMember Removes a user from a group Get-ADGroupMember Lists all members of a group Get-ADComputer Retrieves computer account information Get-ADPrincipalGroupMembership User group membership Table 7.1 : PowerShell cmdlets to manage files and directories The following link allows you to access the Microsoft documentation about the AD module, get help with the syntax of AD cmdlets, and a well-explained list of all commands: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/module/activedirectory/?view=windowsserver2025-ps Creating and managing user accounts User accounts are one of the most essential objects in AD. In this section, you will learn to create new users and manage common account settings. These include enabling or disabling accounts, resetting passwords, and configuring login-related options. All examples use the AD module and are designed to be simple, clear, and practical for daily operations. Before starting the creation of users, make sure to use a domain user account that has the rights to create and modify user accounts. If you are not logged in with an account that has access to do so, you can also use the credential parameter. This parameter is used for all AD commands. The following code is an example of the usage of the credential parameter: $credentials = Get -Credential -UserName "user" -Message "Enter your password" Get -ADuser - Identity «ahmed» -Server «DC01» -Credential $credentials Creating a user with New-ADUser To create a new user, the New-ADUser cmdlet is used. At a minimum, it is recommended to set the properties Name , SamAccountName , UserPrincipalName , and Path , which define the target OU. In this example, the session is running under a user account with the necessary permissions to create users in AD. Therefore, there is no need to supply credentials. Before creating any users, it is important to review the user naming conventions used in the environment. Pay special attention to how SamAccountName and UserPrincipalName are structured, as these may follow specific patterns or include department codes, initials, or other standardized elements. To avoid issues, consider the character limitations for these properties. You can check these limits on the following link: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-server/identity/ad-ds/plan/active-directory-domain-services-maximum-limits Ensuring consistency in naming and staying within these limits helps prevent errors and supports a clean and maintainable AD structure. When writing functions or modules, use these limits for input validation. The following code creates a new user in a specified OU: $firstName = "automation" $lastName = "test" $samAccountName = "atest" $userPrincipalName = "$firstname$lastname @demo .local" $displayName = " $firstName $lastName " $ouPath = “OU=European,OU=TESTUSER,DC=demo,DC=local” $password = ConvertTo-SecureString “P @ssword123 ” -AsPlainText -Force $userParams = @{ Name = $displayName GivenName = $firstName Surname = $lastName SamAccountName = $samAccountName UserPrincipalName = $userPrincipalName DisplayName = $displayName Path = $ouPath AccountPassword = $password Enabled = $true } New-ADUser @userParams The method used here is splatting, which is a recommended way to pass multiple parameters to a cmdlet using a hash table. This makes the code more readable and easier to maintain, especially when dealing with many properties. Consider this as best practice. You can use splatting for every PowerShell command. After executing the preceding code sample, if no error or output is returned, the user has been created successfully. To receive output after creating a user and to store the created user object for further use in the script, the -PassThru parameter must be added. This returns the user object as output, which can then be saved into a variable. In the following code, the -PassThru parameter is added directly to the command to demonstrate that splatting and standard parameters can be combined in the same line. Alternatively, the PassThru key can also be added to the splatting hash table itself. Both methods are valid and can be used depending on preference or script structure. $ user = New -ADUser @userParams -PassThru After creating a user with the -PassThru parameter, the result in the $user variable is as follows: Figure 7.4 : Result in the $user variable on using -PassThru Bulk user creation from a CSV file To create multiple users, data can be prepared in a .csv file and imported using the Import-Csv command. Each row should include values like GivenName , Surname , SamAccountName , and the target OU. This method is often used because CSV files are easy to create, update, and work well with tools like Excel. Here, the CSV file is used as a simple example to explain the process. However, for real automated environments, it is better to use more reliable data sources, such as an SQL database, an internal human resource ( HR ) system, or a web service application programming interface ( API ). These sources allow better control, security, and integration with other systems. CSV files are static and can lead to errors if used as the main data source in long-term or automated setups. A CSV file demonstrates looping through data and creating users; the goal should be to connect the script to a proper system that always provides up to date and validated information. Note: This approach of reading external data and creating users from it works with many sources. However, the idea stays the same. Get the data, process it, and automate the creation. The following is an example of CSV: GivenName;Surname;SamAccountName;OU Michael;Seidl;mseidl;OU=Automation,OU=TESTUSER,DC=demo,DC= local ahmed;Uzejnovic;auzejnovic;OU=Automation,OU=TESTUSER,DC=demo,DC= local Next, create a .csv file and save it on your device. Then change the OU to fit your environment. If you want, you can also add more users. Now, we can use Import-Csv to import the .csv file to PowerShell: $ users = Import-Csv "C:\Automation\users.csv" -Delimiter ';' The imported data is automatically converted to a PowerShell object as illustrated in the following figure: Figure 7.5 : Managing AD and imported data Note: When working with CSV files in PowerShell, it is important to be aware that the default delimiter used by Import-Csv and Export-Csv is not globally fixed, but depends on the Windows region settings of the system. In many English-speaking regions, the comma ( , ) is used as the default delimiter, while in several European locales, such as Germany or Austria, the semicolon ( ; ) is applied instead. This behavior can lead to confusion when sharing CSV files across systems with different regional configurations. To ensure consistency, it is recommended to explicitly specify the delimiter using the -Delimiter parameter in your commands. Now, we can loop through each object in the $users collection. For every user, a display name and user principal name ( UPN ) are created by combining the given name and surname. A secure password is generated using ConvertTo-SecureString , and all required user properties are stored in a hashtable called $userParams . This includes fields like Name , GivenName , Surname , SamAccountName , UserPrincipalName , DisplayName , Path , and the account password. The user is also set to be enabled upon creation. Finally, the New-ADUser cmdlet is called with splatting ( @userParams ) to create the user account in AD. This method makes sure that each user is created in a clean, controlled, and consistent way. The code to do so is as follows: foreach ( $user in $users ) { $displayName = " $($user .GivenName) $($user .Surname)" $upn = " $($user .GivenName) $($user .Surname) @demo .local" $password = ConvertTo-SecureString "P @ssword123 " -AsPlainText -Force $userParams = @{ Name = $displayName GivenName = $user .GivenName Surname = $user .Surname DisplayName = $displayName SamAccountName = $user .SamAccountName UserPrincipalName = $upn Path = $user .OU AccountPassword = $password Enabled = $true } # Create user New-ADUser @userParams } After executing the preceding code, the users in the CSV file are created in AD, as illustrated in the following figure, which shows that two users have been created: Figure 7.6 : Two users created in AD Enabling and disabling accounts To enable or disable user accounts in AD, there are two common methods. The first method uses the Disable-ADAccount and Enable-ADAccount cmdlets directly by specifying the -Identity parameter: # Disable an account Disable-ADAccount -Identity samaccountname # Enable an account Enable-ADAccount -Identity samaccountname This is simple and effective for one or multiple users. An alternative method is to use Set-ADUser with the -Enabled property, illustrated as follows: # Disable an account Set -ADUser - Identity samaccountname -Enabled $ false # Enable an account Set -ADUser - Identity samaccountname -Enabled $ true This approach gives more flexibility, especially when working with custom logic or bulk operations. For example, Set-ADUser -Identity "testuser01" -Enabled $false disables the account, while setting it to $true enables it. Both methods achieve the same result and can be used depending on the context or preference. Resetting account password Resetting passwords is a common task in user account management during onboarding, password recovery, or security-related actions. PowerShell provides a simple way to reset a user's password using the Set-ADAccountPassword cmdlet, shown as follows: Set -ADAccountPassword ` - Identity auzejnovic ` -NewPassword (ConvertTo-SecureString «NewP@ssword 1995" -AsPlainText -Force) This command sets a new password for the specified user account. The ConvertTo-SecureString cmdlet is used to ensure that the password is passed in a secure format. After resetting a password, it is often necessary to force the user to change it at their next login. This ensures that the password remains private and known only to the user, especially if the new password was set by an administrator or helpdesk. This step adds a layer of security and helps maintain compliance with internal password policies. To force a password change at next logon, use the following command: Set -ADUser - Identity auzejnovic -ChangePasswordAtLogon $ true Account login settings In addition to enabling and disabling accounts, AD also supports other login-related settings. Two common examples are unlocking locked accounts and defining logon hours. To unlock an account that has been locked due to multiple failed login attempts, use the following command: Unlock-ADAccount - Identity auzejnovic This is very useful in cases where a user enters the wrong password too many times and gets locked out. Updating user attributes After user accounts have been created, it is often necessary to update their properties during onboarding, department changes, or administrative corrections. The ActiveDirectory PowerShell module provides a straightforward way to modify user attributes in AD using the Set-ADUser cmdlet. Updating a single property The simplest way to change a user property is by calling Set-ADUser with the -Identity parameter and the property to update. For example, to change a user's title, use the following command: Set -ADUser - Identity auzejnovic -Title «IT Support Specialist» The code in the following example sets the same attribute but outputs a result with the property included: Set -ADUser - Identity auzejnovic -Title «IT Support Specialist» -PassThru | Get -ADUser -Properties title The following example allows you to update a user property dynamically using variables: $user = "auzejnovic" $propertie = 'title' $value = 'IT Support Specialist 1' Set-ADUser -Identity $user -Replace @{ $propertie = $value } -PassThru | Get-AdUser -Properties $propertie In this case, the property name and its value are stored in variables. This allows for more flexible scripting where the same logic can be reused for different attributes without rewriting the command. The -Replace parameter accepts a hashtable , and by using the variable $propertie as a key, the script becomes fully dynamic. To take this even further, this pattern can be wrapped into a function or used in a loop to update multiple fields for multiple users. For example, if user update data is received from a form, API, or structured file, the property name and value can be processed dynamically at runtime. Updating multiple properties To update multiple properties at once, use the following code: Set -ADUser - Identity auzejnovic ` -Department «IT» ` -Office «HQ-Building1» ` -TelephoneNumber «+43 123 456 789» The same can be achieved by using -Replace and a hashtable, shown as follows: Set-ADUser -Identity $user -Replace @{ title = "IT Support Specialist" department = "IT" physicalDeliveryOfficeName = "HQ-2nd Floor" } -PassThru | Get-ADUser -Properties title, department, physicalDeliveryOfficeName Managing group membership Group membership is one crucial part of AD management. Groups are used to control access to file shares, applications, printers, email lists, and many other resources. Managing which users belong to which groups helps to simplify permission management and keep the environment organized. Get all groups for a user To list all the groups a user is a member of, use the Get-ADUser cmdlet with the -Properties parameter to retrieve the MemberOf attribute, shown as follows: Get -ADUser - Identity auzejnovic -Properties MemberOf | Select -Object -ExpandProperty MemberOf The following figure shows the output, which is a PowerShell object array: Figure 7.7 : PowerShell output showing group memberships returned as an object array To get readable names, each distinguished name ( DN ) can be passed through Get-ADGroup , as shown: (Get-ADUser -Identity auzejnovic -Properties MemberOf).MemberOf | ForEach-Object { Get-ADGroup $_ | Select-Object -ExpandProperty Name } The output is shown in the following figure: Figure 7.8 : Resolved group names after translating distinguished names using Get-ADGroup Get all members of a group To list all members of a specific group, use the Get-ADGroupMember command, as shown: Get -ADGroupMember - Identity «automation1 " The output will include the full user objects of the group members, as shown in the following figure: Figure 7.9 : Full user objects returned when retrieving all members of an AD group In practice, it is common to encounter scenarios where groups are members of other groups, known as nested group membership. To handle this and retrieve all users across all group levels, the -Recursive parameter can be used with Get-ADGroupMember . This ensures that indirect members, coming from nested groups, are also included in the results. The following figure illustrates an example of nested membership: Figure 7.10 : Nested membership The following command retrieves all members of a group, including the nested ones: Get -ADGroupMember - Identity «automation1» - Recursive | Select -Object -ExpandProperty name Figure 7.11 shows the output of the preceding code. Properties can be used based on your requirements. Here, the name has been used to get an easily readable list of users: Figure 7.11 : Output showing all members of the group Add a user to a group To add one or more users to a group, use the Add-ADGroupMember cmdlet. This is commonly done during user onboarding or when granting access to shared resources, such as file shares, applications, or email distribution lists, as illustrated in the following example: Add -ADGroupMember - Identity «automation2» -Members «mseidl» Multiple users can be added at once by separating them with commas: Add -ADGroupMember - Identity «automation2» -Members «mseidl», «auzejnovic» The group must already exist in AD, and the users must be valid account names. This method ensures that membership is updated instantly and consistently across the domain. For bulk assignments, this command is often used in loops or as part of onboarding scripts. Remove a user from a group To remove a user from a group, the Remove-ADGroupMember cmdlet is used: Remove-ADGroupMember - Identity «automation2» -Members «auzejnovic» -Confirm:$ false The -Confirm:$false flag disables the confirmation prompt, making the command more suitable for use in scripts or automated tasks. Removing users from groups is a common step during offboarding or when revoking access to specific systems. It is recommended to regularly review group memberships to ensure that users only have access to what is necessary for their role. Time-based group membership In some scenarios, users need temporary group membership, for example, to access a system for a limited-time during support, audits, or project work. Traditionally, this required storing dates externally and scheduling scripts to clean up expired access. Since Windows Server 2016, AD includes a built-in feature to solve this: privileged access management ( PAM ) with time-to-live ( TTL ) group memberships. This feature allows users to be added to a group with an expiration time. Once the TTL expires, the user is automatically removed from the group by AD, without the need for any custom cleanup script. Requirements AD forest functional level must be Windows Server 2016 or higher, which can be checked using the following command: (Get-ADForest) .ForestMode The PAM feature must be enabled. To check if it is already active, use the following command: Get -ADOptionalFeature - Filter «name -eq ‹Privileged Access Management Feature›» If the EnabledScopes property is empty, the feature is not enabled, as shown in the following figure: Figure 7.12 : Check if PAM is enabled To enable the feature, use the following code: Enable-ADOptionalFeature 'Privileged Access Management Feature' ` -Scope ForestOrConfigurationSet ` -Target demo.local Note: Be aware, once enabled, you cannot disable this feature anymore. After running the command, re-check with Get-ADOptionalFeature to confirm the feature is active. Adding a user to a group temporarily After the feature is enabled, users can be added to groups with a defined TTL using the -MemberTimeToLive parameter, as shown: $ User = «mseidl» $ Group = «TTLGroup» $TTL = New -TimeSpan -Minutes 5 Add -ADGroupMember - Identity $ Group -Members $ User -MemberTimeToLive $TTL View time-limited memberships To check the remaining time for temporary group members, use the following code: Get -ADGroup $ Group -Property member -ShowMemberTimeToLive The output will display the group members, and for each member with a TTL assigned, the remaining time will appear in seconds directly before the DN. This indicates how long the user will remain in the group before being automatically removed. An example of the output of the preceding code is illustrated in the following figure: Figure 7.13 : Viewing limited-time memberships Searching for users and computers Searching for users and computers in AD is something that comes up often in daily IT work. For example, it may be needed to find a specific user, see who works in a certain department, or get a list of all users in one OU. PowerShell makes this easy with commands like Get-ADUser and Get-ADComputer . Get a single user by samaccountname To retrieve a user by their login name, use the following command: Get -ADUser - Identity «auzejnovic» This command returns basic information, such as DN, security identifier ( SID ), and object class. You can also use the Object globally unique identifier ( GUID ), DN to retrieve the user. Get a user with additional properties By default, only a few properties are returned. To include more details, use the -Properties parameter, as shown: Get -ADUser - Identity auzejnovic -Properties DisplayName, Title, Department The properties mentioned in the command will be added to the output. To access these properties, you can use dot sourcing as shown in the following code: $ user = Get -ADUser - Identity auzejnovic -Properties Title $user.Title # Result -&gt; IT Support Specialist Search for users with a filter To list all users in the domain, the following command can be used: Get -ADUser - Filter * This returns every user account in AD. In large environments, this can be a long list, so it is often better to filter the results. Use the following command to search for users based on a specific attribute, such as Title : Get -ADUser - Filter «Title -eq ‹IT Support Specialist›» -Properties Title The preceding command returns a list of all users where the Title attribute is set to IT Support Specialist . String comparisons in filters are not case-sensitive, so IT , it , or It will all return the same results. The -Filter parameter supports other operators too, which are listed as follows: -eq : Equal to -ne : Not equal to -like : Allows wildcards like * -notlike : Excludes matching patterns Filters are very helpful for finding users based on job title, location, office, or any custom field. Multiple filters can also be combined using -and or -or to narrow the results further. Narrowing down the results not only makes the output easier to read, but it also improves performance in larger environments. When the query is more specific, PowerShell and AD can return the results faster because less data needs to be processed. Using wildcards * To find AD users or computers, we can use the filter with a wildcard ( * ): Get -ADUser - Filter «Name -like ‹ahm*›» Wildcards can be used in any string field when using the -like operator. It will return all users whose name starts with ahm . Searching in a specific OU Using the -SearchBase parameter enables us to search in a specific OU, shown as follows: Get -ADUser - Filter * -SearchBase «OU=European,OU=TESTUSER,DC=demo,DC=local» This also helps to narrow down large searches and improve performance in big environments. Get-Computer accounts Computer accounts are just as important as user accounts in AD. They represent devices that are joined to the domain, such as workstations, laptops, and servers. Managing computer objects helps keep the environment clean, secure, and organized when tracking inactive devices or applying group policies. To retrieve all computer objects in the domain, use the following command: Get -ADComputer - Filter * This command lists every computer account, which can be a long list in larger networks. To narrow down the search, use a filter as shown in the following example: Get -ADComputer - Filter «Name -like ‹ SE*'" The preceding command finds all computer accounts that start with SE . Using filters makes it easier to focus on specific naming patterns. Additional properties can be retrieved to get more useful information about each computer, such as the installed operating system or the last time the computer was active, shown as follows: Get -ADComputer - Filter * -Properties OperatingSystem, LastLogonDate This allows checking if machines are still being used or if they can be removed from AD. It is a good practice to regularly review and clean up old computer accounts that have not logged in for a long time, as this helps keep the directory accurate and reduces the risk of unused machines being overlooked. Using LDAP filters Instead of using the -Filter parameter, it is also possible to use -LDAPFilter to search for users in AD. LDAP filters follow a different syntax but offer more flexibility and support for advanced queries, as illustrated: Get-ADUser -LDAPFilter "(&amp;(objectClass=user)(title=IT*))" The aforementioned command returns all user accounts where the title starts with IT . The &amp; symbol means both conditions must be true. LDAP filters can also be used to check for multiple departments, missing values, or group membership. Working with organizational units OUs are used to organize objects in AD, such as users, computers, and groups. They help create a structured and manageable environment by grouping related objects, for example, by department, location, or function. OUs also play an important role in applying group policies and delegating administrative control. Create a new organizational unit To create a new OU in AD, use the command New-ADOrganizationalUnit , shown as follows: New -ADOrganizationalUnit -Name "IT-Automation-Users" -Path "DC=demo,DC=local" This creates an OU named IT-Automation-Users under the domain root. The -Path parameter defines where in the AD hierarchy the OU will be placed. OUs can also be created inside other OUs by adjusting the path. Move an object to an OU To move a user, computer, or group to a different OU, use the Move-ADObject cmdlet. You will need to provide the object’s DN or GUID as the identity, as shown in the following command: $ user = Get -ADUser - Identity «auzejnovic» Move-ADObject - Identity $user.DistinguishedName -TargetPath «OU=IT-Automation-Users,DC=demo,DC=local» This moves the user into the IT-Automation-Users OU. Note: When an object is moved to a different OU, its DN changes. If the script continues to use the old DN after the move, it may result in errors. It is recommended to re-query the object after moving it if further actions are needed. Rename an organizational unit To rename an OU, use the Rename-ADObject cmdlet. First, the OU’s DN must be retrieved. This can be done using Get-ADOrganizationalUnit , illustrated as follows: Get -ADOrganizationalUnit - Filter «Name -eq ‹IT-Automation-Users›» | Rename-ADObject -NewName «IT-Department» This command changes the name of the OU from IT-Automation-Users to IT-Department . Renaming an OU only changes the name; it does not affect the objects inside the OU. However, since the DN includes the OU name, this change will update the DN of all child objects as well. Note: It is a good practice to verify the new DN if other scripts rely on the previous path. Delete an organizational unit By default, when creating an OU using AD Users and Computers at the GUI, the option protect object from accidental deletion is often enabled. This adds a security permission that prevents the object from being deleted or moved unless the protection is removed. To check if the protection is enabled, use the following command: Get -ADOrganizationalUnit - Identity «OU=oldou,DC=demo,DC=local» -Properties ProtectedFromAccidentalDeletion The output is shown in the following figure, where the -Properties ProtectedFromAccidentalDeletion is set to true: Figure 7.14 : Output showing protection is enabled To remove the protection, use the following command: Set -ADOrganizationalUnit - Identity «OU=oldou, DC=demo, DC=local» -ProtectedFromAccidentalDeletion $ false After disabling protection, the OU can be moved or deleted as needed. Note: Enable this protection for all critical OUs to reduce the risk of accidental changes. It can be applied manually or automated as part of OU creation scripts. To delete an OU, use the Remove-ADOrganizationalUnit cmdlet as follows: Remove-ADOrganizationalUnit - Identity «OU=oldou, DC=demo, DC=local» -confirm:$ false By default, this will only work if the OU is empty. If the OU contains users, groups, computers, or sub-OUs, the -Recursive switch is required, shown as follows: Remove-ADOrganizationalUnit - Identity «OU=oldou, DC=demo, DC=local» - Recursive -Confirm:$ false Note: This action is permanent. All child objects inside the OU will be deleted as well. Always double-check the contents before removing an OU. For safety, it is recommended to first move or export the objects elsewhere if they are still needed. Managing permissions in Active Directory AD allows setting detailed permissions on objects such as users, groups, and OUs. These permissions control who can read, modify, move, or delete objects. Proper permission management is critical to ensuring secure and organized administration, especially in environments where tasks are delegated across different teams. View permissions on an AD object To see the current permissions on an AD object, use Get-Acl as follows: $OU = "OU=IT-Department,DC=demo,DC=local" $acl = Get-Acl "AD: $OU " $acl .Access This shows a list of access control entries ( ACEs ), including who has access, the rights they have, and the permissions applied to child objects. Set permissions on an AD object To apply custom permissions, the following code assigns ReadProperty rights to the user mseidl on the IT-Department OU in AD. It connects to the OU using its LDAP path and retrieves the current access control list ( ACL ). The user account is translated into a SID. A new access rule is created to allow the user to read properties on all objects within the OU, including child objects, by enabling inheritance. This rule is added to the ACL, and the updated security settings are written back to AD using CommitChanges() . This method is useful for delegating specific permissions while avoiding full administrative access: $ ouPath = " LDAP://OU=IT-Department,DC=demo,DC=local " $ entry = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.DirectoryEntry ($ ouPath ) # Get current security descriptor $ acl = $ entry.ObjectSecurity # Define the identity to delegate to $ ntAccount = New-Object System.Security.Principal.NTAccount (" demo.local\mseidl ") $ identity = $ ntAccount.Translate ([ System.Security.Principal.SecurityIdentifier ]) # Define rights and access $ rights = [ System.DirectoryServices.ActiveDirectoryRights ] ::ReadProperty $ type = [ System.Security.AccessControl.AccessControlType ] ::Allow $ inherit = [ System.DirectoryServices.ActiveDirectorySecurityInheritance ] ::All # Create the access rule $ rule = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.ActiveDirectoryAccessRule ( $ identity , $ rights , $ type , [ guid ] ::Empty , $ inherit ) # Add the rule and apply it $ acl.AddAccessRule ($ rule ) $ entry.ObjectSecurity = $ acl $ entry.CommitChanges () The added access rule at the AD object (OU) in the GUI can be seen in the following figure: Figure 7.15 : Access rule shown in GUI Remove permissions from an AD object To remove the permission, the following code first gets the current permissions from the IT-Department OU. It looks for permissions that are assigned to the user mseidl , then creates the exact same rule that gave ReadProperty access. After that, it removes this rule from the list of permissions and saves the changes back to AD, as shown in the following code: 1. $ouPath = "LDAP://OU=IT-Department,DC=demo,DC=local" 2. $entry = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.DirectoryEntry( $ouPath ) 3. $acl = $entry .ObjectSecurity 4. $acl .Access | Where-Object { $_ .IdentityReference -like "*mseidl*" } 5. 6. # Build identity 7. $ntAccount = New-Object System.Security.Principal.NTAccount( "demo.local\mseidl" ) 8. $identity = $ntAccount .Translate([System.Security.Principal.SecurityIdentifier]) 9. 10. # Rebuild the exact same rule 11. $ruleToRemove = New-Object System.DirectoryServices.ActiveDirectoryAccessRule ( 12. $identity , 13. [System.DirectoryServices.ActiveDirectoryRights]::ReadProperty, 14. [System.Security.AccessControl.AccessControlType]::Allow, 15. [Guid]::Empty, 16. [System.DirectoryServices.ActiveDirectorySecurityInheritance]::All 17. ) 18. 19. # Remove and commit 20. $acl .RemoveAccessRule( $ruleToRemove ) 21. $entry .ObjectSecurity = $acl 22. $entry .CommitChanges() Tips, pitfalls, and best practices Managing AD with PowerShell is efficient, but it also requires careful handling to avoid mistakes. A small error in a script can affect many users or objects at once. The following tips allow you to work more safely and effectively, highlight common mistakes to avoid, and outline the best practices for writing reliable scripts: Use -WhatIf before making changes : Most PowerShell cmdlets that change AD objects support the -WhatIf parameter. This shows what would happen without making the change. It is highly recommended to use -WhatIf when testing a new script or running a command that affects multiple objects, shown as follows: 1. Disable-ADAccount - Identity «auzejnovic» -WhatIf The aforementioned command will simulate disabling the account and show the result, but the user will remain active. Avoid hardcoding values : Instead of writing fixed values in the script (like usernames, OU paths, or passwords), use variables, configuration files, or parameters. This makes scripts easier to reuse and safer to maintain. Validate before updating : Always validate that the target user, group, or OU exists before making changes. For example, use Get-ADUser to confirm the account is correct before applying updates, shown as follows: if (Get-ADUser -Identity "auzejnovic" ) { Set-ADUser -Identity "auzejnovic" -Title "Updated Title" } Keep OU structure organized : When creating or moving objects, be sure to place them into the correct OU. A flat structure can quickly become unmanageable. Design OUs by role, location, or department, and be consistent. Watch out for DN changes : When moving or renaming objects, the DN changes. If the DN is used later in a script, re-fetch the object after the change to avoid errors. Protect critical OUs and objects : Enable protect from accidental deletion for important OUs and accounts. This prevents deletion or movement unless the protection is disabled. Test in a lab first : Always test new scripts in a test environment before using them in production. Even a small typo or incorrect filter can have wide-reaching effects in AD. Use logging : Log actions and errors to a file or the event log, especially for scripts running automatically. This helps with troubleshooting and keeps track of what changes are made. Conclusion This chapter covered the management of AD using PowerShell. It began with setting up the AD module and continued through essential tasks such as creating users, updating properties, managing group memberships, and searching for users and computers. It also covered organizing objects using OUs, assigning permissions, and delegating control. Advanced topics like time-based group membership and permission delegation were explained with practical examples. Throughout the chapter, best practices and safety tips were highlighted to reduce the risk of errors when working in live environments. The next chapter will focus on moving from on-premises to the cloud. Readers will explore the management of Entra ID (formerly Azure AD) using PowerShell. The chapter will cover connecting to Entra ID, working with cloud-based users and groups, assigning roles, and integrating PowerShell into modern cloud-based identity management.
