PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 8 Managing Entra ID with PowerShell Introduction Managing identities in the cloud is now part of everyday IT work. With more users working from anywhere and using cloud apps, the need for secure and flexible identity management has grown. Entra ID, previously known as Azure Active Directory ( AD ), is a Microsoft cloud-based identity and access service. It controls user sign-ins, access to applications, and overall identity security across Microsoft 365 and other services. In many environments, tasks like adding users, resetting passwords, managing licenses, or assigning roles are done daily. Doing all this manually on the portal can be time-consuming and can lead to errors, especially when working with many users. PowerShell helps solve this by giving you a fast and reliable way to automate these actions. This chapter will guide you through using PowerShell to manage Entra ID. You will learn how to connect to your tenant, find and update users, manage groups, assign roles, and control app access, all from the command line. Each example is explained step-by-step, so even if you are new to cloud automation, you can follow along easily. The goal is to help you save time, avoid mistakes, and create repeatable processes that make your work more consistent. Whether you are managing a small team or a large cloud environment, using PowerShell with Entra ID gives you more control and flexibility. By the end of this chapter, you will be able to automate the most important identity tasks and build a solid foundation for working with Entra ID through PowerShell. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Entra ID management with PowerShell Connecting to Entra ID with PowerShell Managing users in Entra ID Managing groups in Entra ID Managing applications and service principals Managing roles and access in Entra ID Objectives By the end of this chapter, the readers will know how to manage Entra ID using PowerShell in a fast and reliable way. They will start by learning to connect to your Entra ID tenant securely and check that everything is working. They will learn how to list, search, and update users in the cloud, just like one would with AD, but adapted for the cloud. Readers will also learn how to manage group memberships. This includes adding or removing users from groups, finding group members, and working with dynamic groups if needed. Another key part is managing app registrations and service principals. These are often used by apps or scripts to access Microsoft 365 resources. This chapter shows how to list, filter, and update them with PowerShell. Role assignments are also covered. They will see how to list available roles, check who has which role, and assign or remove roles when needed. These actions help keep their environment secure and well-organized. The examples in this chapter are made to be simple and ready-to-use. Each command can be tested directly in your environment and adjusted for their needs. Whether they are onboarding new users, updating permissions, or cleaning up old objects, this chapter will give them the tools to do it with PowerShell, quickly and without mistakes. Entra ID management with PowerShell Entra ID (formerly Azure AD) is the Microsoft identity platform in the cloud. It controls access to Microsoft 365, Azure, Teams, and many other services. PowerShell makes it possible to manage Entra ID quickly and is ideal for automation, onboarding, cleanup tasks, and more. There are a few ways to manage Entra ID with PowerShell or scripts. All of them are based on the Microsoft Graph API, which is the core service behind the scenes. You can use the Microsoft Graph API with a business account for full functionality. If you do not have one, a personal Microsoft account can be used to access free endpoints like OneDrive or Outlook data. Some Microsoft 365 features, however, require a valid license to be available through Graph. Microsoft Graph API The Microsoft Graph API is the central interface used to access all Microsoft 365 services. It connects to cloud services like Entra ID, Exchange Online, Teams, SharePoint, and more, using one single API endpoint: https: //graph.microsoft.com With this API, you can do almost anything you can do in the portal, but through scripts and automation. For Entra ID, that includes working with users, groups, apps, roles, devices, and more. The Graph API is the most flexible method because you are working directly with the API itself. You do not need any modules, just PowerShell and the built-in Invoke-RestMethod or Invoke-WebRequest cmdlets. With this method, you manually build the API requests. That includes writing the full request URL, setting the headers, handling the access token, and formatting the request body. This gives you full control and can be used in many different scenarios. For example, to get a list of users, the request would look like the following code: $uri = "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/users" $headers = @{ Authorization = "Bearer eyJ0eXAiOiJKV..." # Access token } Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $ uri -Headers $headers -Method GET Note: Do not try this right now. This code is only an example to show you what the code looks like. This method is based purely on the Microsoft Graph API and is also used internally by all other tools and modules. It is a good choice when building custom scripts or when working in systems where you cannot install extra modules. Microsoft Graph PowerShell SDK This is the official and most recommended way to work with Microsoft Graph from PowerShell. It simplifies everything by giving you ready-to-use cmdlets that are mapped directly to the Graph API. For example, instead of building a full REST call, you can simply run the code as follows: Get-MgUser The module handles all the background work, authentication, and request building. It supports almost everything available on Microsoft Graph and is constantly updated with new features. It also allows you to manage permissions by using scopes, switch between tenants, and even support app-based logins for automation. Entra module The Entra module is a newer PowerShell module that is designed to simplify identity management tasks in Entra ID. It provides easier and more readable cmdlets, which can be helpful in day-to-day tasks or for those who prefer simpler syntax. The commands are more aligned with how people think, i.e., the commands are less technical and more direct. This makes the Entra module attractive for simpler use cases or when you are writing quick scripts to manage users, groups, or roles. However, it is important to understand that the Entra module is built on top of the Microsoft Graph module. It does not replace it; instead, it wraps it. That means every Entra command runs a Microsoft Graph command in the background. So, when you use the Entra module, you are still using the Microsoft Graph API under the hood; you just do not see the technical details. This makes it easier to use in some cases, but if you want more flexibility, detailed control, or better performance, it is often better to go directly with the Graph module or the Graph API. Both modules can be used in the same script if needed, but in this chapter, we will focus fully on the Microsoft Graph PowerShell Software Development Kit ( SDK ) so that you get full access to everything the Graph API offers. Connecting to Entra ID with PowerShell Before connecting to Entra ID using PowerShell, it is important to understand how permissions work in Microsoft Graph. Microsoft Graph uses scopes to control what your session is allowed to do. Without the correct permission, your commands might fail. Understanding Microsoft Graph permissions When using Microsoft Graph, you need to tell it what parts of the directory you want to access. These are called scopes or Graph permissions. There are two types of permissions: Delegated permissions : These are used when you log in as a user. You only have access to the things that your user account is allowed to do. This is what happens when you use Connect-MgGraph and sign in with your account. It is perfect for interactive work or testing. Application permissions : These are used for scripts and automations that run in the background without a user signed-in. These permissions are granted to an app registration and can access data across the tenant. They are used when connecting with certificates or secrets, which we will cover in Chapter 12 , Working with APIs and Web Services . Each command you run in Microsoft Graph requires a specific permission. Some examples are given in the following table: Action Command Permission Get a list of all users in the tenant Get-MgUser User.Read.All Get a list of all groups in the tenant Get-MgGroup Group.Read.All Get members of a Microsoft 365 group Get-MgGroupMember -GroupId "&lt;GroupId&gt;" GroupMember.Read.All Get a list of devices joined to Entra ID Get-MgDevice Device.Read.All Table 8.1 : Permissions required for running specific commands If you try to run a command without the right scope, it may fail, even if you are a global admin. That is why it is important to know which scopes your session has and to request the correct ones when connecting. Viewing required permissions You can check the permission for any command on the Microsoft Docs site. For example, the following link provides the permission list for Get-MGUser : https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/module/microsoft.graph.users/get-mguser?view=graph-powershell-1.0#description To find out what access your assigned permission has, you can follow this documentation: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/graph/permissions-reference#user-permissions Installing the Microsoft Graph module Before you can connect to Microsoft Graph and run any commands, you first need to install the Microsoft Graph PowerShell SDK. This module gives you access to all Microsoft 365 and Entra ID resources. To install the module, open a PowerShell window as an administrator and run the following command: Install- Module Microsoft.Graph - Scope CurrentUser This will download and install all parts of Microsoft Graph for your user account. The installation might take a few minutes. If you only need a small part of Microsoft Graph, like managing users, you can install specific parts instead. For example, the Microsoft.Graph.Users permission scope is as follows: Install- Module Microsoft.Graph.Users - Scope CurrentUser In this chapter, we will use the full Microsoft.Graph module to have access to all commands without worrying about missing something. After installation, the module is automatically loaded into your session. This means we can immediately continue with the next step, which is connecting to Microsoft Graph. Connect to Microsoft Graph Before connecting, it is important to understand that you must have access to a valid Entra ID account (formerly Azure AD). Your account must have permissions inside Entra ID (for example, to read users, manage groups, or assign roles). Without an Entra ID account, it is not possible to sign in and manage resources through Microsoft Graph. Once you have an Entra ID account ready, you can connect to Microsoft Graph by running the following connection code: 1. Connect-MgGraph A sign in window will appear where you must enter your Microsoft 365 username and password. This sign in process uses delegated permissions, meaning you will act based on the rights assigned to your user account. If your account does not have enough permissions, you might not be able to run certain commands. In that case, you would need to request the required access from your administrator. When you connect to Microsoft Graph for the first time with PowerShell, a permission window will appear, as illustrated in the following figure: Figure 8.1 : Permission requested window When you connect to Microsoft Graph for the first time using PowerShell, a permission prompt appears. This prompt asks you to approve that PowerShell can access your Entra ID environment. In the preceding figure, you can see an example of this permission request. The app requesting access is called Microsoft Graph Command Line Tools . It asks for two main permissions: sign in and read user profile, which allow the app to sign you in and read basic details like your username and email address, and maintain access to data you have given it access to, which keeps the session active without needing to sign in again each time. There is also a checkbox labeled Consent on behalf of your organization . If you select this option, the permission will not only apply to you, but also to all other users in the tenant. This checkbox usually appears only if you are a global administrator. After reviewing the requested permissions, you can either click Accept to continue or Cancel to stop the connection process. If you accept, PowerShell will receive a security token to manage Entra ID resources through Microsoft Graph. If you cancel, the connection will not be established, and you will not be able to run any Graph commands. This step is necessary to ensure that only trusted applications and users can access your environment. Check the connection After signing in, you can verify the session by running the command as follows: Get-MgContext The output is shown in the following figure: Figure 8.2 : Output of MgContext command The preceding output shows important information about the session, such as: The signed-in username The tenant ID (your Entra ID environment) The permissions (scopes) granted The current Microsoft Graph endpoint If this information appears, you are connected successfully, and we are ready to manage the Entra ID environment. After connecting to Microsoft Graph with Connect-MgGraph , the session is active and ready. However, just being connected does not automatically give us access to manage users, groups, devices, or roles. This is because, by default, the session has very limited permissions. Usually, only the basic sign in permission is granted, meaning you can view your own user profile, but nothing else. If we try to run Get-MGUser we will receive an error as shown in the following figure: Figure 8.3 : Error message on running Get-MGUser The error appears because we have not assigned the right permissions, such as User.Read.All . So, now we will reconnect with the right permission set. To connect with additional permissions, you can specify the scopes you need by adding the -Scopes parameter when connecting, as shown in the following code: Connect-MgGraph -Scopes "User.Read.All , Group.ReadWrite.All , Device.Read.All , Directory.Read.All" When running the preceding code, the login window will pop up again and ask us to approve the requested permissions. Note: It is a good idea to only request the permissions you really need. This follows the principle of least privilege, making your sessions safer and easier to manage. The following figure shows that the app is requesting permissions to the scopes we have set in the Connect-MgGraph command: Figure 8.4 : Allow permissions to the scopes set in Connect-MgGraph command Now, check the Get-MgContext command and find out the permissions your session now has. Unattended connection with application permissions Until now, we have connected to Microsoft Graph as a signed-in user using delegated permissions. This is fine when running scripts manually. However, if you want a script to run without user interaction, for example, a scheduled task or an automation running every night, you need to use an unattended connection based on an app registration in Entra ID. This type of connection uses application permissions instead of delegated permissions. Working of an unattended connection Instead of signing in as a user, you create an app registration in Entra ID. The app gets its own identity and can be given permissions to read or manage users, groups, and other resources. The script will authenticate using the following: • The app (client) ID • The tenant ID • A client secret (like a password) or certificate There will be no manual sign in anymore. The app signs in automatically and can run in the background without anyone logged in. Requirements The requirements for an unattended connection are as follows: An app registration must be created in Entra ID. The app must be granted the correct application permissions (not delegated permissions). Admin consent must be given for permission. Save the client ID and the secret in a secure place (key vault or password manager). Connect with an app registration In the following script, an application connects to Microsoft Graph using its own identity instead of a signed-in user. The app (client) ID, client secret, and tenant ID identify the app within Azure Entra ID. The client secret is converted into a secure string to protect it in memory, and both values are combined into a PSCredential object. Finally, the Connect-MgGraph cmdlet uses these credentials to authenticate the app and establish a secure connection to Microsoft Graph. This approach enables app-only authentication, which is commonly used for automated scripts and background services that run without user interaction. To connect to Microsoft Graph, we need the given sample code, in which we must update the clientid , client secret , and the tenantid with the values of the application: # Define the Application (Client) ID and Secret $ApplicationClientId = 'clientid' # Application (Client) ID $ApplicationClientSecret = 'client secret' # Application Secret Value $TenantId = 'tenantid' # Tenant ID # Convert the Client Secret to a Secure String $SecureClientSecret = ConvertTo-SecureString -String $ApplicationClientSecret -AsPlainText -Force # Create a PSCredential Object Using the Client ID and Secure Client Secret $ClientSecretCredential = New-Object -TypeName System.Management.Automation.PSCredential -ArgumentList $ApplicationClientId, $SecureClientSecret # Connect to Microsoft Graph Using the Tenant ID and Client Secret Credential Connect-MgGraph -TenantId $TenantId -ClientSecretCredential $ClientSecretCredential The following figure shows the result after connecting Graph unattended: Figure 8.5 : Connection Graph unattended Note: Make sure to always use the right permission using delegated or application permissions in the next sections. Managing users in Entra ID Now that we are connected to Microsoft Graph, we can start managing users inside Entra ID. Users are one of the most important objects in every environment. They represent real people, service accounts. Managing users means being able to create, search, update, and sometimes delete user accounts when needed. In this section, we will focus on the most important and common tasks you need in daily work, such as: • Getting a list of users • Filtering users • Creating new users • Updating existing users • Deleting users • Restore Soft deleted users All actions will be done using some of the commands listed, such as Get-MgUser , New-MgUser , Update-MgUser , and Remove-MgUser . The Microsoft Graph module makes it easy to perform these tasks with just a few simple lines of PowerShell. Before we can manage users, we must make sure our session has at least the following permission: User.ReadWrite.All to view and modify all users. We will use the delegated scenario for the following example, so we connect to Graph with the needed permission: Connect-MgGraph -Scopes "User.ReadWrite.All" Getting a list of users The most basic action when managing users is to list all users in your Entra ID tenant. This can easily be done with the Get-MgUser cmdlet, shown as follows: Get-MgUser This command sends a request to Microsoft Graph and returns a list of user accounts. By default, it does not return all properties, only some default properties. The list will only show the first 100 users by default. Microsoft Graph automatically uses paging to handle larger results, but Get-MgUser handles this internally. If you need to retrieve all users, you can add -All to your command: Get-MgUser -All Viewing more user properties The following code will retrieve extra information about each user, depending on what data is filled in Entra ID: Get-MgUser -Property JobTitle, MobilePhone, Department Filtering users When working with larger environments, listing all users is not very practical. Instead of getting everything, you often want to filter users based on certain conditions, such as by name, department, or whether they are enabled or disabled. With Get-MgUser , you can use the -Filter parameter to only retrieve the users you really want. Filtering by display name If you want to find users whose name starts with Oliver , you can run the following command: Get-MgUser -Filter "startsWith(displayName,'Oliver')" The output is shown in the following figure: Figure 8.6 : Filtering users by name Filtering by department Use the following code if you want to get users from a specific department: Get-MgUser -Filter "department eq 'IT Department'" The aforementioned code will show all users who are in the IT Department . Filtering enabled or disabled users To find only users who are enabled, run the following command: Get-MgUser -Filter "accountEnabled eq true" To find only disabled users, run this: Get-MgUser -Filter "accountEnabled eq false" Filtering users when working with Microsoft Graph is very important, especially in larger environments. When you use a filter, the query is processed on the server side, directly inside Microsoft Graph, and only the results you really need are sent back to PowerShell. This reduces the amount of data being transferred, speeds up the script, and lowers the load on both your local machine and the Microsoft servers. Without filtering, all user data would first be loaded and then filtered locally, which wastes resources and takes much longer. By filtering directly in the query, you make your script faster, cleaner, and more efficient, especially when working with thousands of users. Creating new users in Entra ID After we list and filter users, the next important step is creating new users in Entra ID. This is often needed during onboarding processes, automation, or setting up test environments. In Microsoft Graph PowerShell, you can create new users using the New-MgUser cmdlet. Important requirement: To create users, the session needs the User.Read.Write.All permission. Without this permission, we will get an error when trying to create a new user. Creating a user The following code shows how to create a user with the New-MGUser cmdlet: New-MgUser - AccountEnabled ` -DisplayName "Max Mustermann" ` - UserPrincipalName "max.mustermann@8knn7n.onmicrosoft.com" ` - MailNickname "max.mustermann" ` -PasswordProfile @{ Password = "P@ssword1234" ; ForceChangePasswordNextSignIn = $ true } The AccountEnabled parameter defines if the user account is active when created. The DisplayName is the full name of the user that will be shown in directories and address books. The UserPrincipalName is the login name for the user and must be unique, usually following the email format: user@domain.com . The MailNickname is a shorter version of the user's name used for email aliases and internal routing. Finally, the PasswordProfile sets the initial password for the account and allows you to force the user to change their password at the next sign in, which is a common security best practice. Updating existing users in Entra ID After creating users, the next important task is knowing how to update existing user accounts. This is needed when something changes, like job titles, departments, phone numbers, or if you need to enable or disable accounts. In Microsoft Graph PowerShell, updating users is done using the Update-MgUser cmdlet. You can update just one or multiple properties at the same time, depending on what you need. Updating a single property The following code demonstrates how to update the property JobTitle : Update-MgUser -UserId "max.mustermann@8knn7n.onmicrosoft.com" -JobTitle "IT Administrator" To check if the update was successfully executed, use the following command: Get-MGUser -Userid "max.mustermann@8knn7n.onmicrosoft.com" | Select-Object displayname, JobTitle The output is shown in the following figure: Figure 8.7 : Updating a single property in Entra ID Updating multiple properties at once Using the following code, we can also update several fields in one command: Update-MgUser - UserId "max.mustermann@8knn7n.onmicrosoft.com" ` -Department "IT Department" ` - MobilePhone "+43123456789" ` - OfficeLocation "Building A - Room 102" To verify our update was successfully executed, use the following code: Get-MGUser -Userid "max.mustermann@8knn7n.onmicrosoft.com" -Property displayname, JobTitle, Department, MobilePhone, OfficeLocation | Select-Object displayname, JobTitle, Department, MobilePhone, OfficeLocation In the next figure, you can see the output of the preceding command: Figure 8.8 : Updating multiple properties in Entra ID Disabling or enabling a user account To disable or enable a user (for example, when offboarding or onboarding), update the AccountEnabled property to false. To enable it, switch the $false to $true , as shown in the following code: Update-MgUser -UserId "max.mustermann@8knn7n.onmicrosoft.com" -BodyParameter @{ AccountEnabled = $ false } Deleting users in Entra ID Sometimes it is necessary to delete users from Entra ID. This is common during offboarding processes, when cleaning up test accounts, or when removing service accounts that are no longer needed. Deleting a user will remove their account from the directory. However, Entra ID does not delete users immediately. Deleted users first go into a soft-deleted state (the Recycle Bin ) and stay there for about 30 days before they are permanently removed. Delete a user In Microsoft Graph PowerShell, you can delete a user with the Remove-MgUser cmdlet: Remove-MgUser - UserId "max.mustermann@8knn7n.onmicrosoft.com" After removing the user, we can verify this with the Get-MGUser cmdlet. The result of the Get-MgUser cmdlet after removing the user is as follows: Figure 8.9 : Removing a user through the Get-MgUser cmdlet Restoring deleted users in Entra ID When you delete a user from Entra ID, they are not immediately and permanently removed. Instead, the user goes into a soft-deleted state and stays there for about 30 days. During this time, you can restore the user if needed. This is very helpful in case a user was deleted by mistake or if plans change, and the user account needs to be reactivated. First, we need to find the deleted user object using the following command: Get-MgDirectoryDeletedItemAsUser The output is shown in the following figure, where the Displayname and ID of the soft deleted user can be seen: Figure 8.10 : Finding the deleted user object Now, use the command restore-MgDirectoryDeletedItem to restore the user and the parameter -DirectoryObjectID to specify the user who is to be restored: Restore-MgDirectoryDeletedItem -DirectoryObjectId ec1125f9 -50 d1 -461 d -927 e-d7d557263217 Managing groups in Entra ID After managing users, the next important topic is groups. Groups are used everywhere to manage access to resources, assign licenses, control app permissions, and organize users into logical units. Managing groups through PowerShell makes it much easier and faster to automate memberships, control access, and clean up old groups. In this section, we will cover the most common and important group management tasks, such as: • Getting a list of groups • Viewing group details • Creating new groups • Deleting groups • Managing group memberships Before working with groups, make sure your session has the following permissions: Group.Read.All to read groups. Group.ReadWrite.All to create, update, or delete groups. GroupMember.Read.All to view group memberships. GroupMember.ReadWrite.All to manage group memberships. Getting and searching groups The first step when managing groups is to list all groups in Entra ID. You can also search for specific groups based on their name, type, or other properties. This is done using the Get-MgGroup cmdlet. Listing all groups To get a basic list of groups, simply run the following command: Get-MgGroup Like in the Get-MGUser command, by default, this command delivers only the first 100 entries. To get all groups, use the -All switch. The following figure shows a part of the output of the Get-MGUser command: Figure 8.11 : Listing all groups in Entra ID Filtering groups by name To get a group by a specific property, use the -Filter parameter, as shown in the following command: Get-MgGroup -Filter "startsWith(displayName,'My Sample')" The output of the preceding command is illustrated as follows: Figure 8.12 : Filtering groups by a specific property It is also possible to find groups by a specific property using the following command: Get-MgGroup -Filter "displayName eq ‹My Sample Team 2›» This will return all groups with the DisplayName My Sample Team 2 , as shown in the following figure: Figure 8.13 : Filtering groups by display name Selecting specific group properties We can also request additional properties, just like we did with users in the preceding section: Get-MgGroup -Property DisplayName, MailEnabled, SecurityEnabled | select DisplayName, MailEnabled, SecurityEnabled This will return a list of all groups where we can see the values of the additional properties MailEnabled and SecurityEnabled : Figure 8.14 : Filtering groups by specific properties Creating a new group in Entra ID Creating groups is a common task when setting up new teams, projects, or access control structures. With PowerShell, you can quickly create groups without needing to click through the portal. You can create different types of groups, which are as follows: Security groups : Security groups are used to manage permissions to resources like SharePoint sites, applications, or file shares. Microsoft 365 groups (unified groups) : Unified groups are used for collaboration, connected to Teams, Outlook, Planner, and SharePoint. We will first focus on creating a basic security group. Creating a basic security group To create a simple security group, you can use the New-MgGroup cmdlet: New-MgGroup -DisplayName "IT Admins" ` - MailEnabled:$ false ` - MailNickname "itadmins" ` - SecurityEnabled ` - GroupTypes @() This will return the new group as a result. Creating a Microsoft 365 unified group If we want to create a Microsoft 365 group (used for Teams, shared mailboxes, etc.), the GroupTypes must include Unified , and MailEnabled must be $true , as shown: New-MgGroup -DisplayName "Marketing Team" ` - MailEnabled:$ true ` - MailNickname "marketingteam" ` - SecurityEnabled:$ false ` - GroupTypes @( "Unified" ) Use the following command to check if the group creation was successful: Get-MgGroup -Filter "displayName eq 'Marketing Team'" -Property displayName,GroupTypes, SecurityEnabled | select DisplayName, GroupTypes, SecurityEnabled In the following figure, the group is shown with the specified properties: Figure 8.15 : Creating a Microsoft 365 group Deleting groups in Entra ID Sometimes you need to delete groups from Entra ID. For example, when cleaning up old project groups, removing test groups, or reorganizing your structure. Deleting groups through PowerShell is easy and fast using the Remove-MgGroup cmdlet. Groups are permanently deleted right away; there is no soft-delete or recycle bin for security groups. For Microsoft 365 groups, a soft-delete can happen, but we will focus first on security groups. Deleting a security group To delete a security group, you need the GroupId (the ObjectId ). We will delete our group IT admins using the following command: # First, retrieve the group to confirm you are targeting the correct one $Group = Get-MgGroup -Filter "displayName eq 'IT Admins'" # Review the group details before removing it $Group | Select-Object Id, DisplayName, Description # Use the retrieved group ID to safely remove the correct group Remove-MgGroup -GroupId $Group .Id After the Remove-MgGroup command is executed, there will be no output, which means the command was successfully executed. Deleting a Microsoft 365 group If we delete a Microsoft 365 group (unified group), it goes into a soft-deleted state for about 30 days. During this time, you can restore it if needed. The restoring process is similar to the user restoring using the command Restore-MgDirectoryDeletedItem . Managing group memberships in Entra ID After creating and organizing groups, the next important step is managing who belongs to which group. Group memberships are key for controlling access to applications, resources, licenses, and more. With Microsoft Graph PowerShell, you can easily add users to groups, remove users from groups, and list group members. Adding members to a group To add a user to a group, you use the New-MgGroupMember cmdlet. We have to know the GroupId and the UserId ( ObjectId ) of the user we want to add. The following code shows how to add a user to a group: first, retrieve the IDs from the group and the user object, then add the user to the group: $Groupid = (Get-MgGroup -Filter "displayName eq 'My Sample Team 2'" ).ID $Userid = (Get-MGUser -Filter "startsWith(displayName,'Oliver Adams')" ).ID New-MgGroupMember - GroupId $ Groupid - DirectoryObjectId $ Userid The following figure shows that the user was added to the group: Figure 8.16 : Adding users to a group Removing members from a group To remove a user from a group, use the Remove-MgGroupMemberByRef cmdlet, shown as follows: $Groupid = (Get-MgGroup -Filter "displayName eq 'My Sample Team 2'" ).ID $Userid = (Get-MGUser -Filter "startsWith(displayName,'Oliver Adams')" ).ID Remove-MgGroupMemberByRef -GroupId $Groupid -DirectoryObjectId $userid The following figure shows that after running the preceding code, Oliver is not a member of the group anymore: Figure 8.17 : Removing users from a group Listing members from a group To list all members of a group, we are using the Get-MgGroupMember cmdlet. For this example, some more members are added to the My Sample Team 2 shown as follows: Get-MgGroupMember -GroupId $Groupid | Select-Object id,AdditionalProperties The following figure shows three results: the AdditionalProperties property lists user values for direct access and the ID for identification of the group member: Figure 8.18 : Listing all members in a group Managing applications and service principals Besides users and groups, applications and service principles are an important part of every Entra ID environment. Applications are registered in Entra ID to allow integration with external apps, custom-developed solutions, or background services. Each application in Entra ID automatically has a service principal, which acts as the identity of the app inside your tenant. Managing applications and service principals with PowerShell helps you automate tasks like assigning permissions, removing old apps, or controlling access to cloud resources. In this section, we will look at: Viewing registered applications Viewing service principals Creating a new application Removing applications and service principals Before working with applications or service principals, your session should have the following permissions: Application.Read.All to read apps. Application.ReadWrite.All to create, update, or delete apps. Directory.Read.All to view service principals. Directory.ReadWrite.All to manage service principals. Viewing registered applications To manage applications in Entra ID, the first step is to be able to view all applications that are registered in your tenant. This can easily be done using the Get-MgApplication cmdlet. This cmdlet shows all app registrations, i.e., apps that were manually created, apps registered automatically by some services, or apps registered for your own custom solutions. Listing all applications To get a list of all registered applications, run the following command: Get-MgApplication The preceding command will return a list of applications, shown as follows: Figure 8.19 : List of all applications Like in the other Get commands, use the switch -All to list all the applications. Viewing service principals While applications represent the registration blueprint, the service principal is the actual working identity of the application inside your tenant. Every app that interacts with your environment needs a service principal. Service principals are used when apps sign in, request tokens, or access resources. You can view all service principals using the Get-MgServicePrincipal cmdlet. Listing all service principals To get a list of all service principals, run the following command: Get-MgServicePrincipal The output is shown in the following figure: Figure 8.20 : Listing all service principals Understanding the difference between applications and service principals is crucial when working with Entra ID. The application is simply the registration or template that defines what an app can do, like what permissions it requests or where users get redirected after signing in. The service principal, on the other hand, is what actually lives inside your tenant and acts like the app's identity in the environment. It is the service principal that signs in, gets tokens, and accesses resources. Every application that needs to do something in your tenant, like reading emails, accessing Teams, or managing users, must have a service principal. Knowing the connection between the application and its service principal is important because when you manage permissions, role assignments, or troubleshoot access issues, you are almost always working with the service principal, not the application itself. Without understanding this, it can be very confusing to track which apps have access to your environment and how to control or remove them safely. Creating a new application in Entra ID Sometimes you need to create your own application registration in Entra ID. This is needed when you develop your own apps, want to automate background scripts, or connect third-party services manually. Creating an application registration gives you an AppId and allows you to control how the app can authenticate and what permissions it can request. In Microsoft Graph PowerShell, you create a new application using the New-MgApplication cmdlet. Creating a basic application registration To create a new application with the name Automation Script App , run the following command: $app = New-MgApplication -DisplayName "Automation Script App" -SignInAudience "AzureADMyOrg" The following figure shows the content of the $app variable, which is the newly created application: Figure 8.21 : Creating a new application After creating an app registration, an application object is created, which acts as the blueprint for the app. However, at this point, there is no service principal yet inside the tenant. The service principal will be created automatically later when the app is granted permissions, consented to, or actively used. The service principal is what represents the app inside the directory and allows it to authenticate and access resources. If you need the service principal immediately, for example, when setting up unattended scripts or automations, you must create the service principal manually after registering the application. Using the following command creates the service principal for the application immediately: New-MgServicePrincipal - AppId " a3b5031e-7629-44ec-a603-627af6384375" To verify the service principal, use the following command: Get-MgServicePrincipal -Filter "appid eq 'a3b5031e-7629-44ec-a603-627af6384375'" This will return the newly created service principal, illustrated as follows: Figure 8.22 : Create a new service principal After creating the service principal, the application is now officially recognized inside the tenant. This means you can assign permissions, roles, and API access to it. The service principal allows the app to authenticate and interact with resources using different authentication flows, such as client credentials for background scripts or delegated access for user-based operations. Without a service principal, the application cannot be used in the tenant because it has no identity to log in with or request tokens. The service principal acts as the bridge between the app registration and the actual operations the app needs to perform inside your environment. Removing applications and service principals Over time, you may need to remove applications and service principals that are no longer in use. This is an important part of keeping your Entra ID environment clean, secure, and easy to manage. You can remove applications and service principals separately using Microsoft Graph PowerShell. Applications represent the app registration (the blueprint), while service principals represent the app’s identity inside your tenant. Therefore, deleting the application automatically deletes the service principal. Removing an application registration To remove the application registration, use the Remove-MgApplication cmdlet: Remove-MgApplication -ApplicationId "9b0a50cf-6b83-42ed-9b05-66f44045e535" This removes both the application and the service principal. Removing a service principal To remove only the service principal, use the Remove-MgServicePrincipal cmdlet: Remove-MgServicePrincipal -ServicePrincipalId "46a19643-2499-4e08-95fa-b53597e74847" This removes only the service principal. Managing roles and access in Entra ID Besides users, groups, and applications, another critical part of Entra ID management is roles and access control. Roles define what permissions an identity (user, group, or service principal) has inside your tenant. Managing roles with PowerShell allows you to view all available directory roles in your Entra ID tenant and see which users or service principals are currently assigned to them. It also allows you to assign roles to users, groups, or applications when additional permissions are needed. Finally, PowerShell makes it easy to remove role assignments when they are no longer required, helping you maintain a clean and secure access environment. This is an important task for security and operational control. Before working with roles, make sure your session has the following permissions: RoleManagement.Read.All to view roles. RoleManagement.ReadWrite.Directory to assign or remove roles. Directory.Read.All to read user or service principal information. In the next sub-sections, we will cover how to manage directory roles in Entra ID using PowerShell. We will start by viewing all available directory roles to understand what built-in roles exist in the tenant. Then, we will look at how to view which users or service principals are assigned to specific roles. After that, we will see how to assign a directory role to a user to grant them additional permissions. Finally, we will cover how to remove a role assignment when it is no longer needed. Viewing available directory roles Before assigning or managing roles, it is important to know which roles are available in your Entra ID tenant. Entra ID comes with many built-in roles, such as global administrator, user administrator, application administrator, and many more. You can view all available directory roles using the Get-MgDirectoryRole cmdlet. Viewing roles To view all AD roles in the tenant, use the following command: Get-MgDirectoryRole In the following figure, you can see a small number of the roles in the result: Figure 8.23 : Viewing all AD roles Viewing role assignments Once you know which roles are available, the next important step is to see who is assigned to which roles. This is critical for security and audit reasons; you want to know exactly who has elevated permissions in your tenant. You can view role assignments using the Get-MgDirectoryRoleMember cmdlet. List members of a specific role First, you need to know the ID of the role you want to check. You can get the role ID by running the following command: Get-MgDirectoryRole The preceding command gives you the role ID, and you can list all the members assigned to that role as follows: Get-MgDirectoryRoleMember -DirectoryRoleId "ca7c54ca-6fd2-4b70-9a96-174a86d7a66d" When you retrieve the role assignments, the output will show all users, groups, or service principals that have been assigned to that role. This helps you understand exactly who or what has elevated permissions within your tenant. Assigning a role to a user Assigning roles to users is a key part of managing access in Entra ID. When you assign a directory role to a user, you are granting them additional permissions to perform tasks like managing users, groups, apps, or settings in your tenant. You can assign a role using the New-MgDirectoryRoleMember cmdlet. Before assigning a role, you need two important pieces of information: the ObjectId of the user you want to assign the role to, and the ObjectId of the directory role ( DirectoryRoleId ). To make the process easy and reusable across any environment, we built the example in a way that you only need to specify the role name and the UserPrincipalName at the beginning. We use Get-MgUser with a filter to retrieve the user’s ObjectId , and Get-MgDirectoryRole to find the DirectoryRoleId based on the role’s display name. This information is stored in variables, which we then use in the New-MgDirectoryRoleMemberByRef cmdlet to assign the user to the selected role. This structure makes it simple to adapt the script for different users and roles without needing to rewrite the logic every time. The example code is as follows: $rolename = "Global Reader" $userPrincipalName = "max.mustermann@8knn7n.onmicrosoft.com" $userid = (Get-MgUser -Filter "userPrincipalName eq '$($userPrincipalName)'" ).id $Roleid = (Get-MgDirectoryRole -Filter "displayName eq '$($rolename)'" ).id New-MgDirectoryRoleMemberByRef -DirectoryRoleId $Roleid -BodyParameter @{ "@odata.id" = "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/directoryObjects/$($userid)" } The @odata.id property in the preceding example ( line 8 ) defines the direct Microsoft Graph API URL of the user object. It acts as a reference that tells Graph exactly which directory object to associate with the specified role. Instead of passing user details directly, the API uses this URL to link the existing user object in Entra ID to the directory role efficiently and accurately. The following figure shows that the user was added to the role: Figure 8.24 : Assigning a role to the user Removing a role assignment Just like assigning a role, sometimes you need to remove a role assignment from a user, group, or service principal. This is important when users change roles, leave the company, or no longer need elevated permissions. You can remove a role assignment using the Remove-MgDirectoryRoleMemberByRef cmdlet. We will use the same logic as when assigning a role to the user script: $rolename = "Global Reader" $userPrincipalName = "max.mustermann@8knn7n.onmicrosoft.com" $userid = (Get-MgUser -Filter "userPrincipalName eq '$($userPrincipalName)'" ).id $Roleid = (Get-MgDirectoryRole -Filter "displayName eq '$($rolename)'" ).id Remove-MgDirectoryRoleMemberByRef -DirectoryRoleId $Roleid -DirectoryObjectId $userid This successfully removes the role assignment from the user. Managing roles and access is one of the most important parts of keeping an Entra ID environment secure and organized. Conclusion In this chapter, we learned how to manage Entra ID using PowerShell in a structured and efficient way. We started by connecting to Microsoft Graph, understanding how permissions and scopes work, and making sure our session had the right access. From there, we explored how to manage users by listing, filtering, creating, updating, and deleting accounts. We also covered how to manage groups, including creating groups, updating them, deleting them, and managing group memberships. Applications and service principals were explained in detail, showing how they connect, how to create them, and how to clean them up properly when they are no longer needed. Finally, we looked at managing directory roles, how to view available roles, check who has which roles assigned, and how to assign or remove roles using PowerShell. Using PowerShell to manage Entra ID is powerful because it allows you to automate common tasks, handle large environments more easily, and work in a repeatable and secure way. The examples in this chapter were built to be simple, reusable, and ready for real-world use. By mastering these techniques, you now have full control over users, groups, apps, and access in your Entra ID tenant. In the next chapter, we will continue by connecting PowerShell to SQL Server. You will learn how to run queries, update databases, and automate interactions with SQL environments.
