PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 9 Connecting to SQL Server Introduction In most IT environments, working with data is a common and essential part of daily operations. This data is often stored in an SQL Server database, which may be used for storing user records and logging system activities. PowerShell, which is already widely used for automation, can also connect to SQL Server and perform tasks such as running queries, updating records, or inserting new rows. This ability makes it possible to automate database tasks that would otherwise be manual and time-consuming. This chapter focuses on showing how to establish a connection between PowerShell and SQL Server. It introduces two primary methods for doing this. The first method uses the SqlServer module, which simplifies the process and is easy to start with. The second method uses native .NET libraries; this method does not require any additional modules and works well in restricted or controlled environments where installing extra components is not possible. You will learn how to perform practical actions such as selecting data from a SQL table, inserting new entries, and updating existing information. These actions are demonstrated using both connection methods. In addition, the chapter explains how to work with query results as PowerShell objects, allowing further processing such as filtering or formatting the data. This chapter is not intended to replace SQL Server Management Studio ( SSMS ), but to show how PowerShell can be used to automate repetitive database tasks in a clean, consistent, and secure way. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Installing the SqlServer module Connecting to SQL Server using PowerShell modules Connecting to SQL Server without modules Updating existing records Working with query results Error handling and cleanup Best practices with SQL in PowerShell Objectives This chapter is designed to help the readers automate common tasks with SQL Server using PowerShell. They will learn how to connect PowerShell to SQL Server in two different ways. One method uses the SqlServer module, which is quick to setup and great for day-to-day use. The second method connects natively using .NET, which requires no additional modules and works well in secure or minimal environments. Both options have their own strengths, and understanding both gives you more flexibility for real-world use. After building a working connection, the next part is about running SQL operations. This includes reading data from tables using SELECT queries, inserting new rows with INSERT statements, and modifying existing entries with UPDATE commands. Readers will also learn how to pass parameters into their SQL queries properly, so their scripts remain safe and clean. Finally, this chapter highlights the best practices for writing solid scripts. This includes error handling, avoiding hardcoded values, and making sure their connections are opened and closed properly. These small things can make a big difference in stability and reliability, especially when their scripts grow or become part of a larger automation process. Installing the SqlServer module Before using PowerShell to run queries against SQL Server, the first step is to install the required module. The SqlServer module is developed by Microsoft and includes the popular Invoke-Sqlcmd command. This module makes it easier to connect, query, and interact with SQL Server directly from PowerShell. Installing from the PowerShell Gallery The module can be installed using the Install-Module command. This command pulls the module from the PowerShell Gallery. To install the module for the current user, run the command in the following snippet: Install-Module -Name SqlServer -Scope CurrentUser If you want to install the module for all users on the system, use the following command: Install-Module -Name SqlServer -Scope AllUsers Note: Installing for all users requires administrator rights. Make sure to open PowerShell as an administrator. Verifying the installation After installing the SqlServer module, we have to confirm that the module is available. We can do this by running the command: Get-Module -ListAvailable -Name SqlServer If installed correctly, you will see output showing the installed version and module path. You can now load the module into your session by running the following command: Import-Module SqlServer To confirm the module is active and ready, check which commands are available with the Get-Command : Get-Command -Module SqlServer This will list all the commands included in the module. One of the most important ones is Invoke-Sqlcmd , which we will use in the next section to send SQL queries directly from PowerShell. Connecting to SQL Server using PowerShell modules After installing the SqlServer module, you can start using PowerShell to connect to SQL Server and run queries. The main command from this module is Invoke-Sqlcmd . It allows you to send SQL queries and get the results directly as PowerShell objects. In Chapter 2 , Enhancing Scripting with Visual Studio Code , we already created a SQL Server database named PowerShell_Test_DB . Inside that database, there is a table named PowerShell_SQL_Table . This table is currently empty. In the upcoming sections, we will use PowerShell to insert test data into it, then read and update the records. Basic connection and query You can run a SQL query from PowerShell with the module using the following snippet: $serverInstance = "localhost\SQLEXPRESS" $databaseName = "PowerShell_Test_DB" $query = "SELECT * FROM PowerShell_SQL_Table" Invoke-Sqlcmd -ServerInstance $serverInstance -Database $databaseName -Query $query -TrustServerCertificate The TrustServerCertificate switch is used to skip certificate validation when connecting to SQL Server over an encrypted connection. It is helpful if the server uses a self-signed or untrusted certificate, especially in test environments. In production systems, it is better to use trusted certificates and avoid this switch for better security. The preceding code connects to the SQL Server instance and reads all data from the table. Since there are no records yet, the result will be empty. That is expected at this point. To store the result in a variable and display it as a table, we can use the following code: $results = Invoke-Sqlcmd -ServerInstance $serverInstance -Database $databaseName -Query $query -TrustServerCertificate $results | Format-Table Once we add data to the table, this will return rows just like any other PowerShell object. Using SQL authentication with PowerShell If you prefer not to use Windows Authentication, or if you are running PowerShell scripts in a separate context or service account, you can use SQL authentication. This means logging in with a SQL username and password. To do this, a few things must be setup in SQL Server before it will accept these logins. Enable SQL Server and Windows Authentication mode By default, some SQL Server installations are set to use Windows Authentication only, which blocks all SQL logins. To allow logins like powershell_user , you must enable mixed authentication mode. To do this, you need SSMS installed. You can download it using the following link: https://download.visualstudio.microsoft.com/download/pr/d2fa077f-a814-4fb2-b903-1fca7658d17e/b5bd22685adb8e8ac81c53936c0a8bea85e9ad0aa7594d7c7b63eb388c910953/vs_SSMS.exe After the installation, follow these steps: Open SSMS and connect to your SQL Server instance. Right-click the server’s name in the Object Explorer , and select Properties . Go to the Security page. Select SQL Server and Windows Authentication mode , as shown in the following figure: Figure 9.1 : SQL Server connection Click OK . Restart the SQL Server service. Create a SQL login and database user Once SQL authentication is enabled, you need to create a login at the server level and a user in your database. In Chapter 2 , Enhancing Scripting with Visual Studio Code , we created a database named PowerShell_Test_DB . You can now add a login using the following SQL query: CREATE LOGIN powershell_user WITH PASSWORD = 'YourSecurePassword123!' ; USE PowerShell_Test_DB; CREATE USER powershell_user FOR LOGIN powershell_user; ALTER ROLE db_datareader ADD MEMBER powershell_user; ALTER ROLE db_datawriter ADD MEMBER powershell_user; This creates a SQL login that has read and write access to your database. Connect from PowerShell using SQL login You can now use this SQL login in your PowerShell scripts by creating a credential object. This is helpful when running scripts automatically, for example, in scheduled tasks, Azure Automation, or any environment where you cannot enter credentials manually each time. Instead of prompting for the username and password with Get-Credential , you create the credential once in the script using stored values. In the following example, we show how to use a hardcoded credential for unattended scripts. This is useful during the first phase of development when you need to test and automate quickly without interactive input. $username = "powershell_user" $password = "YourSecurePassword123!" | ConvertTo-SecureString -AsPlainText -Force $credential = New-Object System.Management.Automation.PSCredential ($username, $password) Invoke-Sqlcmd -ServerInstance $serverInstance -Database $databaseName -Query $query -Credential $credential -TrustServerCertificate This way, your script can connect to SQL Server automatically without asking for input. However, never use hardcoded usernames or passwords in production scripts. Storing sensitive information directly in code is unsafe and can lead to serious security risks. For production environments, consider one of the following secure alternatives: Windows Credential Manager : Store and retrieve credentials securely from the local machine. Encrypted credential file : Export credentials to an encrypted .xml file using Export-Clixml , and import them in your script with Import-Clixml . Azure Key Vault : If running scripts in the cloud or from Azure Automation, store and manage credentials securely in Azure Key Vault. Using one of these methods allows your script to run unattended while keeping credentials protected and outside of your codebase. Connecting to SQL Server without modules In some environments, installing modules like SQL Server is not allowed or not possible. This can happen on locked down systems, servers without internet access, or in cases where you want your script to be fully self-contained. In those situations, PowerShell can still connect to SQL Server using native .NET. This method does not require any extra installations and works anywhere PowerShell and the .NET Framework are available. The .NET approach uses a connection string to connect, sends the SQL command, and reads the result manually. It is a bit more code compared to Invoke-Sqlcmd , but it gives you full control and runs without dependencies. In the following code, we see how to insert data into the database with the .NET method: $sqlquery = "INSERT INTO PowerShell_SQL_Table (FirstName, LastName, Email, CreatedDate, Quantity) VALUES ('Ahmed', 'Uzejnovic', 'ahmed@example.com', GETDATE(), 5); INSERT INTO PowerShell_SQL_Table (FirstName, LastName, Email, CreatedDate, Quantity) VALUES ('Michael', 'Seidl', 'michael@example.com', GETDATE(), 10);" $connectionString = "Server=localhost\SQLEXPRESS;Database=PowerShell_Test_DB;User ID=powershell_ user;Password=YourSecurePassword123!;TrustServerCertificate=True" $connection = New-Object System.Data.SqlClient.SqlConnection $connectionString $connection.Open() $command = $connection.CreateCommand() $command.CommandText = $ sqlquery $reader = $command.ExecuteReader() $connection.Close() Basic SELECT example In the following script, we run a SELECT query and use the previous method, which retrieves the result from the table where we have already inserted data. After that, the data is loaded into a PowerShell object, and it can be used and processed like any other output in your script. $sqlQuery = @ " SELECT * FROM PowerShell_SQL_Table "@ $connectionString = "Server=localhost\SQLEXPRESS;Database=PowerShell_Test_DB;User ID=powershell_ user;Password=YourSecurePassword123!;TrustServerCertificate=True" $connection = New-Object System.Data.SqlClient.SqlConnection $connectionString $connection .Open() $command = $connection .CreateCommand() $command .CommandText = $sqlquery $reader = $command .ExecuteReader() $table = New-Object System.Data.DataTable $table .Load( $reader ) $connection .Close() $tableobject = $table | select -object FirstName, LastName, Email, CreatedDate, Quantity Selecting data using Windows Authentication If your SQL Server allows Windows Authentication, which is the default setting in most environments, you do not need to pass any credentials. PowerShell will automatically use your current Windows session to connect to SQL Server. In the following code sample, you only need to change the connection string. Replace the connection string from the previous example with the following one: $connectionString = "Server=localhost\SQLEXPRESS;Database=PowerShell_Test_DB;Integrated Security=True;TrustServerCertificate=True" This tells PowerShell to connect using Windows Authentication, without the need to handle usernames or passwords. The rest of the code stays the same and will return the records from the PowerShell_SQL_Table . You can see the records in the following figure: Figure 9.2 : SQL Server connection and records Updating existing records After inserting and selecting data, the next common task is to update existing records in the database. This is useful when values need to be changed, for example, correcting a name, updating a quantity, or marking something as modified. We can update records using the SqlServer module and the native .NET method. In this section, we will change the Quantity for a user in the PowerShell_SQL_Table . Update with PowerShell using the SqlServer module To update a SQL Server column, we only need to modify the SQL Query shown as follows: $serverInstance = "localhost\SQLEXPRESS" $database = "PowerShell_Test_DB" $query = "UPDATE PowerShell_SQL_Table SET Quantity = 25 WHERE FirstName = 'Ahmed' AND LastName = 'Uzejnovic'" Invoke-Sqlcmd -ServerInstance $serverInstance -Database $database -Query $query -TrustServerCertificate To check if the update was successful, use the following query: $query = "SELECT FirstName, LastName, Quantity FROM PowerShell_SQL_Table" Invoke-Sqlcmd -ServerInstance $serverInstance -Database $database -Query $query -TrustServerCertificate In the following figure, it can be seen that the Quantity has been modified to 25 : Figure 9.3 : SQL Server connection—Quantity Updating with the .NET method follows the same structure as the previous examples. You do not need to change how the connection or command is created; only the SQL query itself needs to be adjusted based on what you want to update. This makes it easy to reuse the same script for different update operations. Just modify the $query variable, pass new parameter values if needed, and the rest of the script will work the same way. Using parameters with .NET Native When working with SQL queries, it is strongly recommended to use parameters instead of directly inserting values into the SQL string. This becomes especially important when values come from outside sources like user input, files, application programming interfaces ( APIs ), or automation systems. Using parameters makes your queries much safer and more reliable. It prevents problems like broken queries caused by special characters or quotes, and more importantly, it protects against SQL injection. With .NET, we can use proper parameter binding that keeps the SQL logic separate from the data values. This method is also easier to maintain. You can safely reuse the same query with different values by just changing the parameter values, without rebuilding the SQL command every time. Refer to the following code: # Define SQL Server instance and database name $serverInstance = "localhost\SQLEXPRESS" $database = "PowerShell_Test_DB" # Build the connection string using integrated authentication $connectionString = "Server= $serverInstance ;Database= $database ;Integrated Security= True;TrustServerCertificate=True" # Define the values to be updated in the database $firstName = "Ahmed" $lastName = "Uzejnovic" $quantity = 50 # Define the SQL query with parameter placeholders to prevent SQL injection $query = "UPDATE PowerShell_SQL_Table SET Quantity = @quantity WHERE FirstName = @firstName AND LastName = @lastName " # Create and open the SQL connection using the connection string $connection = New-Object System.Data.SqlClient.SqlConnection $connectionString $connection .Open() # Create a SQL command object to run the query $command = $connection .CreateCommand() $command .CommandText = $query # Add parameter for Quantity and assign its value # Using parameters ensures secure data handling and avoids SQL injection $param1 = $command .Parameters.Add( " @quantity " , [System.Data.SqlDbType]::Int) $param1 .Value = $quantity # Add parameter for FirstName and assign its value $param2 = $command .Parameters.Add( " @firstName " , [System.Data.SqlDbType]::VarChar, 50 ) $param2 .Value = $firstName # Add parameter for LastName and assign its value $param3 = $command .Parameters.Add( " @lastName " , [System.Data.SqlDbType]::VarChar, 50 ) $param3 .Value = $lastName # ExecuteNonQuery() runs the prepared SQL command on the server # It is used for operations that do not return data (e.g., INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE) $command .ExecuteNonQuery() # Close the connection to release resources $connection .Close() Protecting scripts with parameterized SQL execution Using parameters avoids the risk of mixing SQL code with data. This protects your script from common bugs and makes it much harder for an attacker to inject malicious SQL commands. It also prevents syntax issues when values include special characters like quotes, commas, or semicolons. Building a reporting script or updating production data, using parameterized queries is one of the most important best practices when working with databases from PowerShell. Working with query results When you run a SQL query from PowerShell, the result is often just as important as the query itself. Whether you are reading user data, checking quantities, or pulling logs, the output needs to be usable. In PowerShell, this means converting the result into a usable object or table that you can filter, export, or reuse later in the script. Working with results using the SqlServer module Let us now explore the selection and filtering of data. We will retrieve the data as a PowerShell object, so it is easy to work with it. Refer to the following code: $serverInstance = "localhost\SQLEXPRESS" $database = "PowerShell_Test_DB" $query = "SELECT FirstName, LastName, Quantity FROM PowerShell_SQL_Table" $results = Invoke-Sqlcmd -ServerInstance $serverInstance -Database $database -Query $query -TrustServerCertificate $filtered = $results | Where-Object { $_.Quantity - gt 10 } $filtered | Format-Table The output will show all results where the Quantity is greater than 10 . Refer to the following code sample to select data using the .NET method: $connectionString = "Server=localhost\SQLEXPRESS;Database=PowerShell_Test_DB;Integrated Security= True;TrustServerCertificate=True" $query = "SELECT FirstName, LastName, Quantity FROM PowerShell_SQL_Table" $connection = New-Object System.Data.SqlClient.SqlConnection $connectionString $connection.Open() $command = $connection.CreateCommand() $command.CommandText = $query $reader = $command.ExecuteReader() $table = New-Object System.Data.DataTable $table.Load($reader) $connection.Close() $table | Where-Object { $_.Quantity - gt 10 } | Format-Table When you use .NET to run a SELECT query, the result is stored in a .NET DataTable object. This is different from what Invoke-Sqlcmd returns, which is a collection of PowerShell objects. To convert the DataTable object to a PowerShell object, we can use Select-Object . This will convert it to an object, and we can use it as a PowerShell object: $table | Select-Object * In Figure 9.4 , you can see the table types. The first one is the result after executing the .NET method, while the second one is the converted $table with Select-Object : Figure 9.4 : SQL Server connection and table types Error handling and cleanup When working with databases, things can go wrong. The server might be unavailable, the query might fail, or a value might be missing. That is why it is important to always include proper error handling in your PowerShell scripts. It helps prevent broken processes and allows you to act when something does not work as expected. Try catch for SQL queries Using Invoke-Sqlcmd or .NET, you should always wrap your database operations in a try catch block. In the following code, you can see an example where the database is modified with a non-existing name to force an error: try { $connectionString = "Server=localhost\SQLEXPRESS;Database=PowerShell_Test_DB1;Integrated Security= True;TrustServerCertificate=True" $connection = New-Object System.Data.SqlClient.SqlConnection $connectionString $ connection.Open() $command = $ connection.CreateCommand() $ command.CommandText = "SELECT * FROM PowerShell_SQL_Table" $reader = $ command.ExecuteReader() $table = New-Object System.Data.DataTable $ table.Load($reader) } catch { Write-Error "Database query failed: $_" } finally { if ($ connection.State -eq 'Open' ) { $ connection.Close() } } The try block holds the actual query. If something fails, the catch block will catch the error and print a message. The finally block ensures that the connection is always closed, even if an error occurs. Note: When working with SQL Server, it is good practice to always close your database connection, even if something fails. Leaving open connections can cause memory issues and block other operations. Always check if the connection is open and close it in a finally block or right after your query runs. Error handling with Invoke-Sqlcmd We can also use try , catch with Invoke-Sqlcmd , shown as follows: try { $ serverInstance = "localhost\SQLEXPRESS" $ databaseName = "PowerShell_Test_DB" $query = "SELECT * FROM PowerShell_SQL_Table" Invoke-Sqlcmd -ServerInstance $serverInstance -Database $databaseName -Query $query -TrustServerCertificate -ErrorAction Stop } catch { Write-Error "SQL query failed: $_" } Invoke-Sqlcmd handles opening and closing the SQL connection automatically, so you do not need to manage the connection yourself. When a query is executed, PowerShell creates a new connection to the SQL Server in the background. After the query finishes, it runs successfully or fails, and the connection is automatically closed. Best practices with SQL in PowerShell Refer to the following best practices for SQL in PowerShell: Use parameters instead of hardcoding values : Always pass values into your SQL queries using parameters. This avoids syntax errors and protects against SQL injection. Wrap queries in try catch blocks : Use try catch to handle errors properly. This helps you react to failed queries and prevents your script from stopping unexpectedly. Always close database connections : When using .NET, check if the connection is open and close it in a finally block. This prevents memory leaks or locked resources. Secure your connection strings : Do not hardcode usernames or passwords. Use Get-Credential , encrypted XML files, Windows Credential Manager, or Azure Key Vault. Use the SqlServer module for simpler scripts : If your needs are basic, Invoke-Sqlcmd is fast and easy. It automatically handles connections and formatting. Switch to .NET for more control : When you need fine control over parameters, error handling, or performance, use .NET and manage the connection directly. Log actions to a table or file : When your scripts change data, write a log entry to track what happened. This is helpful for audits and troubleshooting. Build reusable code : Store your connection string, query, and logic in variables or functions. This makes your script easier to maintain and reuse. Conclusion PowerShell makes it easy to connect to SQL Server and work with data directly from your scripts. Whether you use the SqlServer module with Invoke-Sqlcmd or the native .NET approach, both methods allow you to read, update, and insert data in a flexible and efficient way. In this chapter, we covered how to connect to SQL Server with and without modules, how to run queries, update records, insert new data, and how to use parameters safely to avoid common issues. We also looked at how to handle errors, clean up connections, and return query results. By following the best practices, like avoiding hardcoded credentials, using parameters, and handling errors, you can build scripts that are stable and ready for production. Whether you are pulling reports or automating updates, PowerShell gives you control over how you work with your SQL data. In the next chapter, we will take this further and look at how to schedule scripts and automate workflows. You will learn how to run your scripts at specific times or based on triggers using tools like Task Scheduler, Azure Automation, and more. This will help you turn your PowerShell code into reliable background jobs that run automatically and support your IT processes.

PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 10 Scheduling Tasks and Workflows Introduction Automation is very useful when it runs at the right time. Most tasks in IT need to be done on a regular schedule, like daily reports, weekly cleanups, or security checks. If you always had to start these jobs by hand, it would cost time and could easily be forgotten. That is where scheduling helps. It allows PowerShell scripts to run automatically, at the time you want, without needing anyone to trigger them. In this chapter, we focus on two tools that help us do this: Windows Task Scheduler and Azure Automation. Task Scheduler is perfect for local machines and works well when you want to run something on your own laptop or on-premises server. On the other side, Azure Automation is made for cloud environments and is great when you want to manage jobs across many systems or even mix on-premises with cloud. You will also learn about Hybrid Workers. These are used to run Azure Automation scripts directly on your local environment. It is a very powerful way to connect your cloud-based automation to your own servers or desktops. We will go through the steps to create scheduled tasks in both tools. You will see how to trigger scripts, set start times, and manage schedules the right way. At the end, we compare both tools and show when to use each one. After this chapter, you will be able to create fully automated jobs that run on time. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Importance of scheduling in automation Windows Task Scheduler for local automation Create, edit, and manage tasks with PowerShell Setting up Azure Automation and creating runbooks Adding schedules to runbooks Running local jobs with Hybrid Workers Choosing between Task Scheduler and Azure Automation Best practices for scheduled PowerShell scripts Objectives The goal of this chapter is to help the readers understand how to schedule PowerShell scripts in local and cloud environments. It starts by showing how to use Windows Task Scheduler, a built-in tool on every Windows machine. Readers will learn how to create scheduled tasks, set the time or trigger, and make sure your scripts run exactly when you want. Later in the chapter, the focus shifts to Azure Automation. This cloud-based tool allows them to manage and run scripts from one central place. They will learn how to create an Automation account, write a runbook, and connect it to a schedule. Azure Automation is especially useful when you want to manage many systems or work across cloud and on-premises environments. One important topic in this chapter is Hybrid Workers. These let them run scripts from Azure Automation directly on your servers. This helps when the scripts need access to systems that are not in the cloud. This chapter also compares Task Scheduler and Azure Automation, so that the readers can decide which one works better for their case. Whether they are running a script on one machine or across a whole environment, they will learn how to automate it in a clean and repeatable way. Importance of scheduling in automation In IT, many tasks happen again and again. Things like cleaning up temporary files, backing up folders, sending out reports, or checking for inactive users are all repeatable jobs. Doing them by hand works at first, but it does not scale. That is why scheduling becomes so important. When tasks run automatically, it saves time, reduces mistakes, and makes sure nothing is forgotten. PowerShell is a great tool for scripting these kinds of jobs. However, just writing a script is not enough. You need a way to make sure it runs at the right time. For example, a script that checks disk space should run every night. A script that disables accounts might run once a week. Instead of setting reminders or opening the terminal each time, we use scheduling tools. There are different tools available depending on where your script should run. If your script runs locally on a single machine, then Windows Task Scheduler is a good option. It is already built-into Windows, and with just a few clicks or PowerShell commands, you can create a schedule. If you are working with cloud resources or have a mix of cloud and on-premises systems, then Azure Automation is more powerful. It allows you to run scripts across different systems from one place, and when you need to connect both worlds, Hybrid Workers allow your cloud-based automation to reach your local systems. Windows Task Scheduler for local automation Windows Task Scheduler is a built-in tool that allows you to run scripts, programs, or commands at specific times or based on certain triggers. It is simple, reliable, and works without needing any internet connection or external services. That makes it perfect for local automation on workstations or servers. You can use Task Scheduler to run a PowerShell script every day at 06:00 AM, once a week on Monday, or when the computer starts or a user logs in. This flexibility is one of the reasons why it is still used in many IT environments. Creating a task can be done in two ways. You can use the graphical interface, where you click through the steps and define the trigger, action, and conditions, or you can use PowerShell itself to create the task, which is helpful if you want to automate the creation of schedules across many systems. Each task in Task Scheduler has the following three main parts: Trigger : Defines when the task should run. This could be at a specific time, on login, or on startup. Action : Specifies what must happen by setting the command or script that should be executed. Conditions and settings : These are extra options, like whether the task should run only if the computer is idle, or if it should stop after a certain time. To run a PowerShell script, the action usually looks like this: powershell.exe -ExecutionPolicy Bypass -File "C:\PowerShell Automation\Script.ps1" You can also include arguments if needed. It is important to always use full paths for the script and ensure that the user account running the task has the required permissions. In the next section, we will create a simple scheduled task step-by-step with PowerShell. Create, edit, and manage tasks with PowerShell While using the graphical interface of Task Scheduler is fine for one or two machines, it becomes slow and repetitive when working across many systems. That is where PowerShell makes a big difference. With just a few lines, you can create, update, or delete scheduled tasks on any Windows machine. To create a scheduled task using PowerShell, you need to define the action (what will run), the trigger (when it will run), and some basic settings like the task name and the account that will run it. PowerShell uses built-in cmdlets from the ScheduledTasks module to manage this. In the following example, we will create a task that runs a PowerShell script every day at 07:00 AM: $action = New-ScheduledTaskAction -Execute "powershell.exe" -Argument "-ExecutionPolicy Bypass -File C:\PowerShell Automation\scheduledscript.ps1" $trigger = New-ScheduledTaskTrigger -Daily -At 7 am $principal = New-ScheduledTaskPrincipal -UserId "SYSTEM" -LogonType ServiceAccount Register-ScheduledTask -TaskName "CreateFile" -Action $action -Trigger $trigger -Principal $principal This creates a task named CreateFile that runs under the SYSTEM account. The script scheduledscript.ps1 will be executed every day at 07:00 AM in the morning. The -ExecutionPolicy Bypass argument is used to make sure the script runs even if script execution is restricted. In the following figure, you can see the newly created task in the Task Scheduler : Figure 10.1 : Newly created scheduled task To update a task, for example, to change the time or the script path, you can unregister it first and then create it again. Or you can use Set-ScheduledTask if you only need to change parts of it. If you want to remove a task, the command is just one line, which is as follows: Unregister-ScheduledTask - TaskName "CreateFile" - Confirm:$ false To list all tasks on the system, you can use Get-ScheduledTask . In the following figure, you can see a part of the tasks after executing Get-ScheduledTask : Figure 10.2 : Output of Get-ScheduledTask Using PowerShell for task management is ideal for automation scripts. You can include these steps in your deployment scripts, startup scripts, or even monitoring tools. In larger environments, this method also helps with compliance. Every task is defined in the same way, nothing is forgotten, and everything can be tracked in logs or scripts. No more clicking through windows and guessing if the right options were selected. Setting up Azure Automation and creating runbooks To use Azure Automation, you need an Azure subscription. This is required to create an Automation account and to run your scripts in the cloud. If you already use Azure services like virtual machines ( VMs ), storage accounts, or Microsoft Entra ID, then you already have a subscription. If not, creating one is easy and can be done directly through the Azure portal. The official Microsoft documentation for Azure Automation is available at the following link: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/automation/overview Once your subscription is ready, the first step is to create an Automation account. This account is your central workspace. It holds your runbooks, schedules, variables, credentials, modules, and everything else related to automation. Think of it like your control center for building and running PowerShell jobs in the cloud or on-premises using Hybrid Workers. In this section, we will not go too deep into how to write or design complex runbooks in Azure Automation. That would go beyond the scope of what we need here. Instead, we will focus only on the essential parts you need to get started, creating the Automation account, setting up your first runbook, scheduling it, and connecting it to Hybrid Workers if needed. The goal is to get your automation running as quickly and as simply as possible. More advanced topics like input parameters and shared resources can come later. For now, we focus on what matters most—getting your scripts into Azure Automation and making them run safely and reliably. Authenticating to Azure using managed identity When your runbook needs to access other Azure services, like a storage account, a VM, or Microsoft Graph, it needs permission. Instead of storing passwords or secrets in your script, you can use something much better and safer—a managed identity. When you create an Automation account, you can enable a system-assigned managed identity with just one click. This gives your Automation account its own identity inside Azure. You can then assign roles to this identity, just like you would to a user. For example, if a runbook needs to read files from a storage account, you can give the Automation account's managed identity the Storage Blob Data Reader role. If it needs to start or stop a VM, assign the VM Contributor role. This is done using role-based access control ( RBAC ), which is built-into Azure. It makes access management secure, simple, and very easy to maintain. Once the managed identity has the right permissions, you can use Connect-AzAccount -Identity inside your runbook, and it will automatically log in using the assigned identity. Create an Automation account Follow the outlined steps to create an Automation account: Open the Azure portal ( https://portal.azure.com/ ). Search for Automation accounts , shown as follows: Figure 10.3 : Search for Automation accounts Click on Automation accounts under Services . Click on Create , as illustrated in the following figure: Figure 10.4 : Create an Automation account 5. Now, we will fill in the basic settings for creating an Automation account: a. Subscription : Select your existing Azure subscription. b. Resource group : Create or select an existing resource group. c. Name : Choose something that fits into your Azure naming convention. We will use PowerShell-Automation . d. Select your Region . e. Under Advanced , leave Managed identity enabled, which allows your runbooks to securely access other Azure services without needing to store passwords. Only your identity needs to have the right role assignment for the Azure service. f. Click on Review + Create . g. Review the settings and check if the validation is green. h. Click on Create to create the Automation account. In the following figure, you can see the aforementioned configuration of the Azure Automation account: Figure 10.5 : Azure Automation account configuration 6. After the deployment is complete, click on Go to resource . We have created the Automation account and can start to create runbooks. A runbook is a PowerShell script that lives in Azure. You can create it directly in the browser and develop the code directly in the browser or in VS Code with the Azure Automation extension. As the next step, we will create a runbook. For that, navigate to the Process Automation section and click on Runbooks , which will show all available runbooks. Now, click on Create , and follow the inputs shown in Figure 10.6 : Figure 10.6 : Creating a runbook After you have filled in the basic information, click on Create . The runbook will open automatically in edit mode. Copy the following code into your runbook: 1. Write-Output "Hello from Azure Automation!" 2. 3. # Check if it runs on a Hybrid Worker 4. if ($ env:COMPUTERNAME - notlike "*SANDBOXHOST*" ) { 5. Write-Output "Running on a Hybrid Worker (local machine): $($env:COMPUTERNAME)" 6. } else { 7. Write-Output "Running in Azure Sandbox Environment : $($ env:COMPUTERNAME)" 8. } 9. # Output the Job ID 10. $jobId = $PSPrivateMetadata.JobId.Guid 11. Write-Output "Job ID: $jobId" In this example, we print out basic information to confirm where the runbook is running and also display the jobId , which helps with tracking and debugging. This is useful when testing Azure Automation in different environments or when using Hybrid Workers. Azure Automation uses different environments to run scripts. If no Hybrid Worker is used, the script runs in a Microsoft-managed cloud environment. These machines usually have a name that includes the word SANDBOXHOST . So, this line checks the COMPUTERNAME of the machine running the script. If the name does not contain SANDBOXHOST , we assume it is running on a Hybrid Worker, which means it is running on a local machine or server. This is helpful when testing or debugging because it tells you exactly where the script is running. Every time a runbook starts, Azure Automation gives it a unique job ID. This ID helps track the specific run of the script. You can use this ID when looking at logs, troubleshooting errors, or checking run history in the Azure portal. The variable $PSPrivateMetadata.JobId.Guid accesses that ID directly. Then, we print it with Write-Output into the Log . The following link provides access to the official Microsoft documentation for managing runbooks: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/automation/manage-runbooks Using the Test pane in Azure Automation The Test pane in Azure Automation lets you run a runbook manually from the portal to see what it does. It is useful for checking if your script works as expected and for debugging if something goes wrong. However, it is important to know that the Test pane runs the script in the real production environment. Note: That means if your script starts VMs, deletes files, or sends emails, it will do those things. It is not a simulation or dry run. So, always be careful when testing runbooks that affect live systems. Click on the Test pane , shown as follows: Figure 10.7 : Click on the Test pane A black window will appear where we can click on run. As we have no Hybrid Worker configured, we do not need to configure the run settings, and we can directly click on run. This will start our runbook and show the logs during the execution, as shown in the following figure: Figure 10.8 : Starting the runbook Now, close the Test Pane and Publish the runbook, as shown in the following figure: Figure 10.9 : Publish the runbook After confirming the Publish , the script is ready to use. Now, you can assign schedules to it or trigger it from other frameworks over the Azure Automation API or trigger it manually from the Azure Automation interface. Adding schedules to runbooks Once your runbook is tested and published, the next step is to assign it to a schedule. Instead of running the script manually every time, you can attach a schedule to it. This feature of Azure Automation turns a simple script into a reliable process that runs without needing anyone to start it. A schedule is a separate resource in your Automation account. Schedules can be found under Shared Resources , as shown in the following figure: Figure 10.10 : Schedules in Azure Automation You can use the same schedule for multiple runbooks, or create different schedules for different needs. You have full control over when, how often, and in which time zone the script should run. Scheduling in Azure Automation When you attach a schedule to a runbook, Azure takes care of the rest. At the specified time, it starts the job, runs the script, and logs the output. The schedule can be configured as: • Once at a specific time. • Every day or week. • Every few hours. This is perfect for tasks such as: Starting or stopping VMs on a daily schedule. Cleaning up old resources once a week. Sending a report every morning. Running system checks every hour. It is also possible to edit or remove Schedules later if your configuration changes. Adding schedules to runbooks The first step is always to create a schedule. You can do that by following the outlined steps: Click on Shared Resources | Schedules in your Automation account. On the top left side, click Add Schedule . Give the schedule a clear, good, readable name. Select a start time. At this time, the script will start for the first time. Choose how often the runbook should be executed (hourly, daily, or weekly). Select the right time zone so it runs at the correct time. Click Create , then confirm by clicking OK . The following figure shows the aforementioned steps: Figure 10.11 : Create a schedule Now, refer to the following steps as we need to add our runbook to the schedule: Go to Runbooks and select the one you want to add to the schedule. Open Schedules under Resources on the left side. Click on Add a schedule , as shown in the following figure: Figure 10.12 : Adding a schedule Click on Link a Schedule to your runbook. Select the created schedule. Click OK . The following figure shows the schedule attached to the runbook: Figure 10.13 : Attached schedule Your runbook is now linked to the schedule. It will automatically run based on the schedule settings, and you will see the run history in the Jobs tab, just like with manual runs. Feel free to modify and test the schedule settings. You can also try to execute the runbook manually and investigate the Azure Automation service. Scheduled scripts with parameters If your runbook accepts input parameters, you will be asked to enter default values when linking the schedule. These values will be used every time the runbook is triggered. So be aware, only link schedules where you have no input parameters or the script logic is taking care of default or empty parameters. Keep an overview of schedules It is good practice to regularly check which runbooks are scheduled and when: View all schedules under Shared Resources | Schedules . See which runbooks are linked to each schedule. Unlink or delete schedules that are no longer needed. For bigger environments, it also helps to keep a small table or documentation that lists all important runbooks, their purpose, and how often they run. So, you do not need to open the Azure Automation environment to check this every time. Running local jobs with Hybrid Workers Sometimes your automation script needs access to something that is not available in the cloud. This could be a shared folder, a local database, a printer server, or an internal API. In those cases, you cannot run the script in Azure directly. Instead, you need to run it on-premises, and that is exactly what Hybrid Workers are made for. Hybrid Workers allow Azure Automation runbooks to run on your local machines, such as a Windows Server in your datacenter or a VM in your network. This means your scripts can connect to anything on your internal network while still being triggered, logged, and managed from the Azure portal. Working with Hybrid Workers In the past, Hybrid Workers were installed using a special Microsoft agent. That method is now deprecated. Today, the recommended way to set up Hybrid Workers is to use Azure Arc-enabled servers. Azure Arc connects your non-Azure servers to Azure. Once connected, you can turn them into Hybrid Workers and run Azure Automation jobs on them just like you would in the cloud. This approach is more secure, more flexible, and fully supported. There are many benefits of using Azure Arc, like managed identity for the Hybrid Workers. Setting up a Hybrid Worker with Azure Arc The following steps describe how to connect a machine to Azure Arc: In the Azure portal, search for Azure Arc . Open the Azure Arc service. Click on Azure Arc Resources , and move to Machines . Then click on Add/Create . Click on Add a machine , as shown in the following figure: Figure 10.14 : Add a machine with Azure Arc At the A dd a Single Server Card , click on Generate Script . Fill in the Basics settings setup. Click on Download and run script , as shown in the following figure: Figure 10.15 : Download and run the script Save or copy the script for later use. Check that the server has HTTPS access to Azure services. Check local administrator permissions on the server. Copy the script to the server and execute it as administrator. The script will download the agent from the Microsoft Download Center and install the agent on the server. It will create the Azure Arc-enabled server resource and associate it with the agent. You will be prompted to log in to the Azure account during the script execution. The following figure illustrates the script output after execution: Figure 10.16 : Azure Arc-enabled server connected after agent installation After the script is executed successfully and no error occurs, we can switch back to the Azure portal. Open the Azure Arc | Machines and click Refresh . Now, the newly joined server should appear as illustrated in the following figure: Figure 10.17 : Newly onboarded server visible in Azure Arc | Machines overview After successfully adding the server to Azure Arc, switch back to the Automation account. Follow the outlined steps to add this server as a Hybrid Worker to the Automation account: 1. Under Process Automation , click on Hybrid worker groups . 2. Under User hybrid worker groups , select Create hybrid worker group . 3. Select a name for the Hybrid worker group . 4. Use default credentials: a. Default credentials : Scripts will run under the system account. b. Custom : Scripts will run under the custom user credentials. 5. Click Next . 6. Select Add machines . 7. Select the newly created machine and click Add . 8. Click Next . 9. Click Create . Now, the Hybrid Worker should appear under the newly created Hybrid worker group , as shown in the following figure: Figure 10.18 :Hybrid Worker group with the newly added Arc-enabled machine Now we are able to run runbooks locally using Hybrid Workers . This means that scripts are no longer limited to the cloud. We can reach file shares, internal systems, all while still managing everything from the Azure portal. The next step is to run a runbook and check the output. For that, follow these steps: Open the runbook RB_001_DEMO . Click on Start . At the right side, the Start Runbook sidebar appears, as shown in the following figure: Figure 10.19 : Starting a runbook In the Run Settings , select run on Hybrid Worker . Choose the Hybrid worker group . Click OK . Now, the runbook will be executed for the first time on the local Hybrid Worker. After refreshing, the runbooks can be seen running, as shown in the following figure: Figure 10.20: Runbook execution in progress on the Hybrid Worker After some seconds, the status should change to completed. Now, click on Completed , as shown in Figure 10.21 . This will open the job history: Figure 10.21 : Completed runbook Here, inputs, outputs, errors, and exceptions that happened while the script was executed can be inspected. On clicking Output , it can be seen that the script has nearly the same output as tested before. However, this time, it was executed on a Hybrid Worker. The output is shown in the following figure: Figure 10.22 : Completed runbook job showing output Keep in mind If you add more machines to your Hybrid Worker group, Azure will use a round-robin method to decide which machine runs the job. You cannot choose a specific Hybrid Worker once there is more than one in the group. So, even if you start with just one server, keep in mind that you might add a second or third one later for load balancing or failover. That is why it is important to design your runbooks in a smart and flexible way. Never store files or logs locally on the Hybrid Worker itself. Instead, always send your logs to Azure Automation or use central storage, like Azure Storage accounts, Azure Tables, or Log Analytics. This way, no matter which machine runs the script, all data stays in one place and is easy to access. Also, avoid using hardcoded files or paths on the Hybrid Worker. Use Azure Automation variables or shared cloud storage to store input and output data. This makes your scripts more portable, reliable, and easier to manage in the long-run. Before building complex automation , it is also a good idea to review the current limits and quotas of Azure Automation. This helps avoid surprises later, especially with job timeouts, output size, or schedule frequency. You can find the latest limits and restrictions here: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/automation/automation-subscription-limits-faq Choosing between Task Scheduler and Azure Automation When it comes to running PowerShell scripts automatically, there are many tools that can help. You can use tools like System Center Orchestrator, Jenkins, or even third-party platforms. But in this chapter, we focus on the two most common and widely used ones: Windows Task Scheduler and Azure Automation. These two tools cover most use cases, working on a single machine or across many systems in the cloud. The following list discusses key differences to help you decide which one fits your situation better: Runtime : Task Scheduler runs your scripts directly on the local machine. It is perfect for jobs on desktops or servers. Azure Automation runs scripts in the cloud, and with Hybrid Workers, it can also run them on your on-premises machines. Setup and requirements : Task Scheduler is built-into every Windows machine, so you can start right away without installing anything. Azure Automation needs an Azure subscription and a bit more setup, like creating an Automation account, but it offers much more control and flexibility. Central management : With Task Scheduler, each machine is managed separately. If you have ten servers, you create the same task ten times. With Azure Automation, everything is in one place. You manage all scripts centrally, which makes it easier and more consistent. Scalability : Task Scheduler works fine for small environments. Azure Automation is designed to scale. You can run the same script across many machines and manage large environments from the cloud. Security and identity : Task Scheduler runs under a local or domain account. You must manage passwords or service accounts manually if you do not use a group Managed Service Account. Azure Automation supports managed identity, which allows secure access to Azure resources without storing any credentials in your scripts. Logging and monitoring : Task Scheduler has basic logs stored locally. Azure Automation offers full job history with detailed output, error messages, and job IDs. This is helpful for debugging and audits. Costs : Task Scheduler is completely free and has no limits. Azure Automation has a free tier for a limited number of runbook minutes. For more usage, there can be small costs, especially when using many Hybrid Workers. Using Task Scheduler and Azure Automation Use Task Scheduler when: You want to run a script on just one or two machines. You do not need cloud integration. You want something fast and easy. You are working in an offline or restricted environment. Use Azure Automation when: You want to manage many scripts from one place. You need cloud or hybrid environments. You want strong logging and monitoring. You need a secure identity and access to other Azure services. You want to schedule and manage everything through the Azure portal. Best practices for scheduled PowerShell scripts When working with scheduled scripts in either Task Scheduler or Azure Automation, it is important to follow a few simple best practices. The following best practices help to keep your automation clean, reliable, and easy to maintain: Use Write-Output , Write-Verbose , or Write-Error to track what your script is doing. Good logging makes it easier to understand what happens if something goes wrong. Make sure script paths, file locations, and resource names are clear and complete. This helps avoid errors when the script runs on a different machine or under a different user. Never store secrets or fixed values inside the script. Use Azure variables, Key Vault, or credential assets to manage this data securely with the use of managed identity. Each script should do one thing well. Smaller scripts are easier to test, update, and reuse. Always test your runbook or local script manually before adding a schedule. In Azure, use the Test pane, and locally, run it in a PowerShell session. Especially when using Hybrid Workers, do not store logs locally. Use Log Analytics, storage accounts, or write results back to a database. Azure Automation has limits on job output size, runtime, and number of jobs. Always check the official documentation before building large-scale automation. Let Azure handle authentication with managed identity instead of storing usernames and passwords in your scripts. Keep a small overview of what jobs are running and when. This helps with tracking, support, and later changes. Scheduled jobs should be monitored. A simple health view or dashboard helps confirm that the jobs run consistently and makes it easier to detect failures, which is especially important in larger environments. Conclusion In this chapter, we explored how to schedule and run PowerShell scripts automatically using Windows Task Scheduler and Azure Automation. Both tools allow scripts to run in the background, based on time or specific triggers, which is a key part of building reliable automation. We started by working with Task Scheduler using PowerShell only. You learned how to create scheduled tasks by defining actions, triggers, and security contexts, all done in script form. This makes it easy to deploy and reuse across different machines or environments. After that, we moved into the cloud and worked with Azure Automation. We created an Automation account, built and tested simple runbooks, and attached schedules to them so they run fully unattended. Then, we looked at Hybrid Workers, which allow runbooks to run on your local servers while still being controlled from Azure. This is helpful when your scripts need access to local resources like file shares or internal systems. We also discussed important points like how Azure Automation scales, how jobs are logged centrally, and how to prepare for future growth by using Azure Arc for Hybrid Worker setup. You also learned that once multiple Hybrid Workers are added, job execution follows a round-robin logic and cannot be targeted to a specific machine. With this chapter, you now have a full understanding of how to schedule PowerShell jobs across local and cloud systems in a structured and reliable way. In the next chapter, we will look at automating Azure VMs using PowerShell. You will learn how to start, stop, and manage VMs directly from your scripts.
