PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 11 PowerShell and Azure for VM Automation Introduction Working with virtual machines ( VMs ) is a big part of IT today, especially when using cloud services like Microsoft Azure. However, creating, configuring, and managing VMs manually in the Azure portal can quickly become a lot of work, especially when you must do it again and again. That is why automation makes so much sense here. With PowerShell, we can do all these steps much faster, more consistently, and without even opening the portal. In this chapter, we will focus on how to automate the most important parts of VM management in Azure. We will look at how to create a VM using PowerShell, how to apply tags for better structure, and how to start or stop machines based on your needs. We will also use PowerShell Desired State Configuration ( DSC ) to make sure each VM is always setup the same way, without extra manual steps. We will also look at how to use Azure Automation and runbooks to run our scripts in the cloud, even on a schedule. This is very useful when you want tasks to run automatically in the background, like turning off unused VMs during the night to save money. We will keep everything as simple as possible. This chapter is not about learning every single detail of a VM, but about giving you the tools and ideas to start automating your work in a smart way. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Concept of cloud virtualization Creating virtual machines in Azure with PowerShell Tagging and organizing VM resources PowerShell Desired State Configuration Start, stop, and deallocate VMs automatically Objectives The goal of this chapter is to show how to manage Azure VMs in a better, faster, and more reliable way using PowerShell. The readers will learn how to create a VM with all the required options by writing a script, instead of clicking through the Azure portal each time. This saves time and helps avoid small mistakes that often happen when things are done manually. Another goal is to make sure their machines always look and behave the same. For this, we will use PowerShell DSC. With DSC, the readers will define how a system should look, and it will make sure that their VM matches that state, even if someone changes something by accident. We will also show how to control the VM lifecycle. That means automatically starting, stopping, or deallocating machines, so they only run when needed. This can help reduce costs and keep their environment cleaner. Finally, we will use Azure Automation runbooks. This time with real VM tasks. Readers will see how to schedule scripts to stop or start VMs at specific times. These are small steps that can save a lot of money, especially if their machines do not need to run all day. Concept of cloud virtualization Cloud virtualization provides a flexible and scalable way to run computing workloads without relying on physical hardware. Instead of managing servers yourself, the cloud offers VMs, networks, and storage that can be created, configured, and automated on demand. This foundation makes it possible to deploy applications quickly, adapt to changing requirements, and integrate powerful automation tools like PowerShell. In the following figure, observe the diagram and its description to build a comprehensive understanding: Figure 11.1 : Cloud virtualization architecture diagram Figure 11.1 illustrates the basic idea of how VMs run in the cloud and how people connect to them. It does not matter if the cloud is Azure, Amazon Web Services ( AWS ), or Google Cloud Platform ( GCP ); the setup always follows the same structure. At the top is the cloud provider, which gives you all the building blocks you need, such as virtual networks ( VNets ) and VMs. Inside the cloud, your machines are placed in a VNet. You can imagine this as your own private network in the cloud. This network is split into subnets, so you can separate machines used for applications from machines used for management tasks. The VM sits inside one of these subnets. It is just like a normal computer, but running in the clouds. Next to it, there can be a Bastion host or jump host, which is a secure way to connect to the VM without exposing it to the internet. Around the VM is the security layer. This includes firewalls and security groups that control which network traffic is allowed and which traffic is blocked. They make sure only the right ports and the right IP addresses can reach the machine. The following are the connection options. There are three common ways to connect: • Virtual private network • Bastion • Public IP Public IP allows direct access from the internet, but it should be used only temporarily and with strict IP restrictions. At the bottom is the client or administrator, meaning any person who needs to connect to the machine, usually from a laptop or on-premises environment. Tip: The entire diagram teaches one important idea, which is that a cloud VM is never alone. It always lives inside a network, protected by security layers, and accessed through controlled paths. This big picture helps us to understand why networking and security matter before we start using PowerShell or automation in the cloud. As a next step, we can use PowerShell to start creating and managing VMs in the Azure Cloud, now that we understand how the overall architecture works. Creating virtual machines in Azure with PowerShell Creating VMs in Azure using PowerShell gives you full control and helps avoid manual work. This is very useful in test and demo environments, but also in production if you want things to be repeatable and reliable. In this section, we will go through the full process step-by-step and explain what to use only for testing, and what is better for production. Install required PowerShell modules Instead of installing the full Az module package, which is very large and includes many things we do not need, we will only install the specific modules required for this chapter. This keeps everything smaller and faster, especially useful when working on local systems or in automation environments. In the following example, you can see exactly which modules we need to create and manage VMs with PowerShell. However, be mindful that if you plan to run these scripts on Azure Automation Hybrid Runbook Workers, these modules must also be installed on those machines. If they are missing, the script will fail. $Modules = ( "Az.Accounts" , "Az.Resources" , "Az.Network" , "Az.Compute" ) $SCOPE = "AllUsers" foreach ($module in $ modules){ Install-Module $module -Scope $SCOPE -AllowClobber -force Import-Module $module } Note: Only use -Scope CurrentUser if you do not have admin rights. For production or shared scripts on servers, prefer -Scope AllUsers (require admin rights). Connect to Azure and set subscription You need to authenticate against the Azure Cloud before you can run any Azure command. For that, we will use Connect-AzAccount . After executing, you will be asked to log in to your Azure account. After successful authentication against the Azure Cloud, select the subscription where your resources will be created with the following command: Connect-AzAccount Set-AzContext - SubscriptionId "subscription-id" This step opens a login window where you enter your Azure credentials. Then you define which subscription to use. This is important when you have access to more than one, for example, a dev subscription and a production one. Note: In production, you might run this inside an Azure Automation runbook or with a managed identity. In that case, do not use Connect-AzAccount . You use Connect-AzAccount -Identity instead. Define all variables We first define everything in one place, so we can reuse this script for other machines later by just changing the names: $resourceGroup = "PSAutomation-RG" $location = "westeurope" $vmName = "PSAuto-VM" $vmSize = "Standard_D2s_v3" $vnetName = "$vmName-VNET" $subnetName = "$vmName-Subnet" $ipName = "$vmName-IP" $nicName = "$vmName-NIC" $osDiskName = "$vmName-OSDisk" $ PublisherName = " MicrosoftWindowsServer " $ Offer = " WindowsServer " $ Skus = " 2019-Datacenter " $ Version = " latest " When crafting a VM in Azure, it is important to choose the right size based on the use case. The needs for production and testing are often different. The following are the details: Demo : Standard_D2s_v3 is a relatively cheap VM for testing. Production : Choose a size based on your actual workload. Use Standard_D or E-series for better performance. Create the resource group A resource group is like a container for your Azure resources. You can delete it, and everything inside it will be removed. It also helps with organizing and tracking costs. Use the following to create a resource group: New-AzResourceGroup -Name $ resourceGroup -Location $location After successful creation, you will get a result in the output, as shown in the following figure: Figure 11.2 : Create a resource group Note: Always use resource groups to group related components like VM, NIC, IP, and disks together. Create a virtual network and subnet Every VM needs to be inside a VNet so it can communicate with other resources: $vnet = New-AzVirtualNetwork -Name $vnetName -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup -Location $location -AddressPrefix "10.0.0.0/16" Add-AzVirtualNetworkSubnetConfig -Name $subnetName -AddressPrefix "10.0.1.0/24" -VirtualNetwork $vnet | Set-AzVirtualNetwork $ vnet = Set-AzVirtualNetwork - VirtualNetwork $ vnet The VNet covers a larger IP range. Inside that, we define a smaller subnet where the VM will live. This is just like splitting a home network into smaller parts. Notice the following for the environment selection: Demo : Use any IP range. Production : Plan IPs to avoid conflicts with on-premises networks or other VNets. Create a public IP address A public IP address allows you to connect directly to your VM from the internet. For example, using Remote Desktop or SSH. This is very helpful during testing or when setting something up quickly. So, for demo or lab environments, a public IP is fine, but in production, it is usually not a good idea to expose your VM directly to the internet. It can be a security risk if someone finds the open port and tries to attack it. Even if the password is strong, it is always better to reduce the attack surface. That is why in production, you should avoid public IPs. A better and safer option is to use Azure Bastion, which lets you connect to your VM securely through the Azure portal, without opening any ports to the internet. Another good option is to connect to your Azure network through a VPN or private link, so you can reach the VM over a private connection, just like inside your office network. Using the following command to create the public IP address: $publicIp = New-AzPublicIpAddress -Name $ipName -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup -Location $location -AllocationMethod Dynamic Note: Make sure you do not use it in production environments unless you fully understand the security implications, and always apply IP restrictions so that only specific, trusted IP addresses can access the VM through the public IP. Create the network interface The network interface cards ( NICs ) connect the VM to the network and give it an IP address, shown as follows: $subnet = Get-AzVirtualNetworkSubnetConfig -Name $subnetName -VirtualNetwork $vnet $nic = New-AzNetworkInterface -Name $nicName -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup -Location $location -SubnetId $subnet.Id -PublicIpAddressId $publicIp.Id Always name your NICs after the VM. This makes troubleshooting much easier. Enter the admin credentials This will be the username and password you use to log into the VM after it is created. Use the following command: $cred = Get-Credential -Message "Enter local admin account for VM" Handling credentials depends on the environment you are working in: In test environments, you can use Get-Credential . In production, do not use hardcoded or manually entered credentials. Use Azure Key Vault or Automation assets. Configure the virtual machine The first step is to verify if the feature UseStandardSecurityType is enabled. If it is not, our VM creation will fail. For this, we can execute the code in the following example. This will automatically check if the feature is enabled. If not enabled, the script will enable it. The execution of this code is only needed once. After that, this feature will stay enabled. $feature = Get-AzProviderFeature -FeatureName UseStandardSecurityType -ProviderNamespace Microsoft.Compute if ($ feature.RegistrationState -ne "Registered" ) { Write-Output "Registering feature 'UseStandardSecurityType'..." Register-AzProviderFeature -FeatureName UseStandardSecurityType -ProviderNamespace Microsoft.Compute Write-Output "Feature 'UseStandardSecurityType' registred..." while ((Get-AzProviderFeature -FeatureName UseStandardSecurityType -ProviderNamespace Microsoft.Compute).RegistrationState -ne "Registered" ) { Start-Sleep -Seconds 5 } } else { Write-Output "Feature 'UseStandardSecurityType' is registered." } Now, we prepare the VM settings, its OS, size, network, etc: $vmConfig = New- AzVMConfig - VMName $ vmName - VMSize $ vmSize | Set-AzVMOperatingSystem -Windows -ComputerName $vmName -Credential $cred -ProvisionVMAgent -EnableAutoUpdate | Set-AzVMSourceImage -PublisherName "MicrosoftWindowsServer" -Offer "WindowsServer" -Skus "2022-datacenter" -Version "latest" | Add- AzVMNetworkInterface -Id $ nic.Id We are telling Azure to create a VM with the following configuration: Use Windows Server 2022 Set the VM name Add the local admin user Attach the NIC we just created Turn on updates and the agent for later extension support Note: This step is flexible. You can also use Linux images or custom ones if needed. Create the virtual machine Finally, we create the VM using everything we defined previously: New-AzVM -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup -Location $location -VM $vmConfig This command sends the full configuration to Azure, and it starts building your VM. After a few minutes, you can see it in the portal as shown in the following figure: Figure 11.3 : VM configurations The connection via Remote Desktop (if using a public IP) will not work directly. For that, we must create a network security group ( NSG ) and allow Remote Desktop Protocol ( RDP ) connections: # Create NSG $nsgName = "$vmName-NSG" $nsg = New-AzNetworkSecurityGroup -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup -Location $location -Name $nsgName # Allow RDP Inbound $nsgRule = New-AzNetworkSecurityRuleConfig -Name "Allow-RDP" -Protocol "Tcp" -Direction "Inbound" -Priority 1000 ` -SourceAddressPrefix "*" -SourcePortRange "*" -DestinationAddressPrefix "*" -DestinationPortRange 3389 -Access "Allow" # Add the rule to the NSG $nsg.SecurityRules.Add($nsgRule) Set-AzNetworkSecurityGroup - NetworkSecurityGroup $ nsg # Associate NSG to NIC $nic = Get-AzNetworkInterface -Name $nicName -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup $nic.NetworkSecurityGroup = $ nsg Set-AzNetworkInterface -NetworkInterface $ nic This is it. Your VM is now live and ready to use. This is the cleanest way to create repeatable and consistent deployments. You can use this same script across environments just by changing a few variables at the top. For testing or demo environments, it is fine to allow RDP from any source so you can quickly connect and try things out, and remove the access again. However, this setup is not safe for production. Note: Exposing RDP to the internet makes your VM vulnerable to brute-force attacks. Here are safer options for production: Limit the source IP in the NSG to your company network or home IP. Example : "SourceAddressPrefix = '202.6.126.11'" Use Azure Bastion, which allows RDP through the Azure portal with no public IP. Setup a VPN and use private IPs only. Tagging and organizing VM resources When you create resources in Azure, things can get messy very quickly. You might have many VMs, network interfaces, disks, public IPs, and more, and they all start to look the same if you do not organize them properly. That is where tags come in. Tags are small labels you can add to any Azure resource. Each tag is just a name and value, like Owner = Ahmed or Environment = Test . They help you group resources, filter them in the portal, track costs, and apply policies. Tags are essential due to the following reasons: Clear structure when you look at your resources. Easier cost tracking per project or department. Helps with automation, like cleanup or reporting scripts. Required for governance in some companies (mandatory tags). Add tags with PowerShell You can add tags when creating a resource or update them later. In the following examples, we are using the variables from the VM creation: Set-AzResource -ResourceId (Get-AzVM -Name $vmName -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup).Id ` - Tag @{ "Owner" = "Ahmed" ; "Environment" = "Demo" ; "Project" = "VM-Automation" } ` -Force In the following figure, you can see the result after the tag was created: Figure 11.4 : Adding tags with PowerShell Also, in the following figure, we can see the created tags in the Azure portal: Figure 11.5 : Created tags in Azure portal You can also tag the resource group or other parts like NICs, disks, and IPs: Set-AzResource -ResourceId (Get-AzNetworkInterface -Name $nicName -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup).Id ` -Tag @{ "VM" = $ vmName; "Type" = "NIC" } -Force The following figure shows the tagged NIC: Figure 11.6 : Tagged network interface PowerShell Desired State Configuration PowerShell DSC is a tool that helps you define how a machine should be configured, and then makes sure it stays that way. You do not just run a script once and hope everything stays the same. With DSC, you describe the desired state (like IIS must be installed or this file must exist ), and DSC applies it and monitors it. If someone removes a feature or changes a setting, DSC can fix it automatically. The advantages of using DSC are as follows: It makes sure machines always stay in the correct configuration. DSC is great for servers that must follow company rules or compliance. It avoids drift when changes are made manually and break something. It is easy to reapply or update configurations later. The following code presents an example of how DSC works: Configuration InstallIIS { Import- DscResource - ModuleName 'PSDesiredStateConfiguration' Node "localhost" { WindowsFeature IIS { Name = "Web-Server" Ensure = "Present" } } } # Create the .mof file $path = "C:\DSC\config" InstallIIS - OutputPath $path Start-DscConfiguration -Path $path -Wait -Force This will install the Web-Server role on the local machine and keep it installed. If you have never used DSC on your server, you will receive the following warning after executing the preceding code: WARNING: Falling back to the previous MOD file or system defaults because the meta configuration mof does not exist or has either been corrupted or an invalid mof property has been set. This warning shows up because the meta configuration is missing. For that, we have to execute the following code: [ DSCLocalConfigurationManager() ] Configuration SetLCM { Node "localhost" { Settings { RefreshMode = 'Push' AllowModuleOverwrite = $ true ConfigurationMode = 'ApplyAndAutoCorrect' } } } # Save and apply meta config SetLCM - OutputPath "C:\DSC\Meta" Set-DscLocalConfigurationManager -Path "C:\DSC\Meta" You only need to set the Local Configuration Manager ( LCM ) once per machine (unless you reset it). After that, you can safely apply .mof files as needed. Now, you can run the Start-DscConfiguration command again. After enforcing a DSC configuration, you can check what is currently applied by running Get-DscConfiguration . The ConfigurationState after a consistency check as shown in the following figure: Figure 11.7 : DSC configuration status This command shows all settings that are being enforced on the machine, such as which Windows features are installed, what services should be running, which files must exist, or even specific registry values. For example, if your configuration ensures that IIS is installed, it will appear in the output when you run Get-DscConfiguration . You can see exactly what was applied and what DSC is watching. Now, imagine someone logs into the server and removes IIS manually using Uninstall-WindowsFeature -Name Web-Server . Normally, this kind of change could go unnoticed and might break websites or automation tasks without anyone realizing. However, if DSC is configured with the mode set to ApplyAndAutoCorrect , it will notice this change during its next consistency check (by default every 15 or 30 minutes, depending on how the LCM is set). When it sees that IIS is missing, it will automatically reinstall it, without asking, and bring the system back into the correct state. This correction process is not silent. DSC logs everything it does. You can review these actions by using Get-DscConfigurationStatus , which shows if a correction was made, when it happened, and what resources were affected. If you want deeper insight, you can also open the event viewer and look under Microsoft | Windows | Desired State Configuration | Operational . There you will find detailed log entries showing when drift happened, what DSC detected, and how it was fixed. So even if people make manual changes or forget something, DSC acts like a safety net. It brings everything back to the way it should be, keeps your machines stable, and makes sure nothing gets out of sync without you knowing. This is very helpful in environments where consistency, compliance, or uptime is important. Azure Machine Configuration Azure Machine Configuration is the modern way to check and control the configuration of VMs in Azure. It replaces the older Azure Automation DSC feature, which is retired. With Azure Machine Configuration, you can make sure that your VMs follow certain rules, like having specific software installed, certain services running, or settings applied. A significant benefit is that it works together with Azure Policy, so you can assign configuration checks across many machines from one central place. It also supports Azure Arc, which means you can use it not only for Azure VMs but also for on-prem and multi-cloud machines. The checks run directly inside the VM through a built-in agent, and you do not need to manage .mof files anymore. Most use cases today are focused on auditing. For example, you can check if a machine is missing a feature or if something has been changed. Some policies also support automatic remediation, which means they can fix problems when a machine drifts from the desired state. If you still like using DSC-style logic, Azure Machine Configuration supports DSC v3-compatible resources in the background. So, you can create custom rules if needed, but for most cases, Microsoft already provides ready to use policies. Azure Machine Configuration gives you a cleaner, more modern way to handle configuration checks and compliance in your cloud environment, without needing to setup extra infrastructure. Start, stop, and deallocate VMs automatically After the VMs are deployed and configured, the next step is to think about how to manage them in daily operations. Especially when it comes to cost, power management plays a big role. Azure charges for VMs based on the time they are running. Even if nobody is logged in or using the machine, the costs are running, as long as the VM is in a Running state. That is why it is important to shut down or deallocate VMs that are not being used. This can easily save hundreds of euros per month in environments with multiple test, dev, or training machines. Doing this manually every day is boring and easy to forget. With PowerShell, we can automate all of it. Difference between stop and deallocate Let us quickly clarify some concepts that often cause confusion: If you stop a VM from inside the operating system (like using shut down), it still counts as running in Azure. You still pay for the compute resources. If you stop using Stop-AzVM , Azure deallocates the VM, which means the hardware is released, and you are not charged for the compute anymore. Only the disk and storage stay. So, when we talk about stopping automation, we usually mean deallocating. In the following command, you can see the real cost-saving action: Stop-AzVM - ResourceGroupName $ resourceGroup -Name $ vmName -Force However, it is not bad before stopping or deallocating the VM to check the actual PowerState of the VM. Use the following command to check the state of the VM: (Get-AZVM -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup -Name $vmName -Status).Statuses[ 1 ] The PowerState after running the preceding command is as follows: Figure 11.8 : PowerState of the VM Now, we will run Stop-AzVM and check the state again. This command may take a few seconds to complete because it waits until the stopping action is finished. The good part is that it returns a result with useful details, like the status, start time, end time, and any errors that may have happened during the process. So, it is easy to work with and understand what is going on. We do not need to check in advance if the machine has already stopped. The following command will try to stop it, and if it is already deallocated, nothing breaks. If something goes wrong, we get the error directly in the response. Stop-AzVM - ResourceGroupName $ resourceGroup -Name $ vmName -Force Just like Stop-AzVM , the Start-AzVM command works in a similar way. It starts the VM and waits until the process is finished. Once the VM is fully running, the command returns a result that includes useful information like the status, start time, end time, and any errors if something went wrong during startup. This behavior is perfect for automation because it gives you full control and feedback in one step. You always know what happened, and when the machine is ready to use again. Refer to the following command: Start-AzVM - ResourceGroupName $ resourceGroup -Name $ vmName Stop all non-production VMs every night You can create a PowerShell script that finds all VMs with a specific tag, for example, Environment = DEMO , and stops them automatically. This is a simple but effective way to reduce costs, especially in dev or test environments that are not needed after working hours. As we have tagged our VM before with the Environment tag, we can now easily find our Demo VMs and stop all VMs tagged with demo in a simple foreach loop: $vms = Get-AzVM -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup | Where-Object { $_.Tags[ "Environment" ] -eq "Demo" } foreach ($ vm in $ vms) { Stop-AzVM -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup -Name $vm.Name -Force } You can run this script manually, or even better, schedule it to run every evening using Azure Automation. This way, your test machines will automatically shut down at 08:00 PM, saving money overnight. Then, in the morning, you can schedule another runbook that starts them again at 07:00 or 08:00 AM, so they are ready to use when the team arrives. You do not have to remember it, and no one must log in to do it by hand. This kind of setup is ideal in companies where VMs are often forgotten or left running for days. With just a bit of scripting and scheduling, you gain control and reduce unnecessary costs without affecting the daily work of your team. Pasting the previous code directly into Azure Automation will not work right away because the runbook needs permission to manage your resources. By default, the managed identity of the Automation account does not have access to start or stop VMs. So, before using the code, we must assign the right role to the managed identity: 1. Give the managed identity the right role. 2. Go to the Resource groups or subscription where your VMs are located and assign the Contributor or Virtual Machine Contributor role to the Automation account's managed identity, as shown in the following figure: Figure 11.9 : Add the role to the managed identity This gives it the needed permissions to start and stop VMs. The following code can now be used in Azure Automation. It connects with the managed identity, filters VMs by tag, stops them, and logs each result. If anything goes wrong, you get a clear message. param ( [ Parameter(Mandatory = $true) ] [ string ] $resourceGroup, [ Parameter(Mandatory = $true) ] [ string ] $tagName, [ Parameter(Mandatory = $true) ] [ string ] $tagValue ) $ErrorActionPreference = "stop" # Connect using the managed identity of the Automation Account try { Connect- AzAccount -Identity Write-Output "Connected to Azure using managed identity." } catch { Write-Error "Failed to connect to Azure. $_" return } # Get all VMs in the resource group with the matching tag try { $vms = Get-AzVM -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup | Where-Object { $_.Tags[$ tagName] -eq $tagValue } if (- not $ vms) { Write-Output "No VMs found with tag '$tagName = $tagValue'. Nothing to stop." return } Write-Output "Found $($vms.Count) VM(s) with tag '$tagName = $tagValue' in resource group '$resourceGroup'." } catch { Write-Error "Failed to get VMs. $_" return } # Loop through the VMs and stop them foreach ($ vm in $ vms) { try { Write-Output "Stopping VM: $($vm.Name)" $result = Stop-AzVM -ResourceGroupName $resourceGroup -Name $vm.Name -Force Write-Output "VM $($vm.Name) stopped. Status: $($result.Status)" } catch { Write-Error "Failed to stop VM $($vm.Name). $_" } } 3. The result in the following figure shows the output of the runbook code after it has been executed in Azure Automation: Figure 11.10 : Output of runbook code You can clearly see that the connection to Azure using the managed identity was successful. It then lists the VMs that matched the given tag and starts stopping them one-by-one. For each VM, you get a clear message showing the name, the action taken, and the final status that came back from Azure. This output is helpful not only to confirm that the script worked, but also to troubleshoot if something goes wrong. If an error happens, it will be shown with details right away. That makes this output great for automation because you get immediate feedback, success, or a specific error message, without needing to check each VM manually. You can also go one step further and setup alert rules in Azure Automation. These alerts can be triggered by runbook job failures or specific error conditions. For example, if a runbook fails or logs an error, Azure Monitor can detect that and send you an email notification automatically. This way, you do not have to constantly check logs; you will be notified if something breaks, and you can react quickly. Alerts can be filtered by runbook name, status, severity, and even specific text in the error message, making this a powerful and smart addition to your automation setup. Conclusion In this chapter, we focused on how to automate the full lifecycle of VMs in Azure using PowerShell. We started by creating VMs in a structured way. We learned how to configure those machines with PowerShell DSC, keeping them in a stable and repeatable state, even if someone changes things manually. We then moved on to handling the day-to-day operations, like starting, stopping, and deallocating VMs, to reduce costs and take control of our resource usage. With the help of Azure Automation runbooks, we automated these tasks. We also used tags to organize our resources better and to group them in a way that makes automation and filtering much easier. This chapter showed how powerful and flexible PowerShell can be when it comes to managing infrastructure in the cloud. With just a few scripts and the right setup, you can fully automate your VMs, save money, and reduce manual work. In the next chapter, we will leave the VM world behind and look at how to connect PowerShell with external services using REST APIs. This includes using Microsoft Graph API, authenticating with API keys or OAuth, and building scripts that interact with the cloud in a smart and secure way. This is where we connect PowerShell to the World Wide Web ( WWW ).
