PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 12 Working with APIs and Web Services Introduction PowerShell is not just about managing local systems or automating servers. One of its most powerful features is the ability to interact with application programming interfaces ( APIs ) and web services. With just a few lines of code, you can connect to cloud platforms, send or receive data, automate user management, and trigger external services. Almost every modern system offers some kind of Representational State Transfer ( REST ) API, and learning how to work with them opens up a whole new level of automation. In this chapter, we will look at how to use PowerShell to send requests to APIs, handle responses, and work with different authentication methods. You will learn how to pass headers, body content, and handle status codes. We will explain the basics and then move into real-world examples. We will also show how to connect to third-party services using API keys or bearer tokens. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Introduction to APIs and PowerShell Basics of REST APIs and HTTP methods Using PowerShell to call APIs API keys and bearer tokens Examples of calling Microsoft Graph API Calling a third-party API Error handling and logging API responses Best practices for secure and clean API scripts Objectives In this chapter, the goal is to understand how PowerShell can be used to connect and work with APIs. The readers will learn what APIs are, how they work, and how PowerShell can send requests and handle responses using standard tools like Invoke-RestMethod . We will also look at how data is sent and received in JSON format, and how to extract and work with that data inside your scripts. We will also cover authentication, because most APIs require it. Readers will see how to use simple API keys and bearer tokens, and how to setup access to the Microsoft Graph API with an app registration. These are the most common methods used in real projects, so that the readers will be ready for both internal tools and public services. Practical examples will show them how to connect to Microsoft 365, read user data, and interact with third-party APIs. Then, we will go through basic error handling and logging, so your scripts can deal with problems and still run reliably. By the end of this chapter, they should be able to write their own scripts to talk to any API, send and receive data, handle errors, and securely authenticate. This opens the door to many automation ideas where PowerShell connects directly to the cloud or other platforms. Introduction to APIs and PowerShell One of the most powerful and useful features in PowerShell is the ability to work with APIs. This opens the door to automation far beyond your local environment. An API is a way for different systems to talk to each other. Instead of manually clicking buttons or filling out forms in a web interface, your script can send a command directly to another system and get a result back. This is perfect for automation. The best part is that most modern platforms support this. Microsoft 365, Azure, GitHub, Jira, weather services, chat apps, and even smart devices like washing machines or robot lawnmowers all offer APIs. PowerShell can send requests to these APIs, receive data in return, and then do something with that data. For example, you can get a list of users from Microsoft Entra ID, post a message in Microsoft Teams, create a ticket in a helpdesk system, or pull data from a web service and turn it into a report. These tasks are all based on using an API, and PowerShell can handle them with just a few lines of code. Most of the time, these services provide what is called a REST API. These APIs work over HTTP, the same protocol used by web browsers. That means PowerShell does not need anything special; you can use the built-in cmdlets like Invoke-RestMethod or Invoke-WebRequest to connect. To call an API, you just need to provide a few things. The Uniform Resource Locator ( URL ) of the API, the HTTP method (like GET, POST, PUT, or DELETE), any required headers or authentication details, and, if needed, some data in the body of the request. Once PowerShell sends the request, the API will respond, often with data in JSON format, and your script can then use this data just like any other PowerShell object. At first, this might sound a bit technical. Do not worry, however, we will go step-by-step. Once you see it in action, it will start to make sense. And before you know it, you will be using PowerShell to interact with APIs just as easily as you manage files or users locally. Basics of REST APIs and HTTP methods Before we start writing PowerShell scripts to call APIs, it is helpful to understand how REST APIs work. It is a standard way for systems to communicate over the internet using simple HTTP requests, the same kind your browser makes when you open a website. Every REST API works around the idea of resources. A resource can be a user, a file, a device, or anything else the system manages. You interact with these resources using URLs as shown in the following sample code: https://api.company.com/users https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/me To work with these resources, REST APIs use standard HTTP methods. Each method has a different purpose: GET : Used to read or retrieve data. For example, get all users or get one ticket. POST : Used to create something new. Like creating a user or submitting a form. PUT : Used to update something that already exists. For example, change a user’s display name. PATCH : Like PUT, but only updates specific fields instead of replacing everything. DELETE : Used to remove a resource, like deleting a user or a record. You use these methods together with the correct URL to tell the API what you want to do: GET | https://api.example.com/newuser?name=username | Retrieves the user whose name matches the value of username. POST | https://api.example.com/users | Create a new user DELETE | https://api.example.com/removeuser?name=username | Delete the user with ID 1234 When you send a request to the API, you will get a response back. This response contains a status code, and often some data, usually in JSON format. Here are some common status codes you should know: 200 OK : The request was successful. 201 Created : A new resource was created successfully. 204 No Content : The request was successful, but no data was returned. 400 Bad Request : Something was wrong with the request. 401 Unauthorized : You are not allowed (missing or wrong credentials). 404 Not Found : The resource could not be found. 500 Internal Server Error : Something went wrong on the API side. PowerShell can help you check the status code and work with the response data. In most cases, the response is in JSON format, which PowerShell can convert automatically into an object. Understanding these basics will help you make better API requests and troubleshoot when something goes wrong. Now that you know what REST APIs are and how HTTP methods work, let us look at how to actually call them using PowerShell. Using PowerShell to call APIs PowerShell makes it very easy to call APIs. The main cmdlet we use for this is Invoke-RestMethod . It sends an HTTP request to the API and returns the response as a PowerShell object. This allows you to work with the data just like any other variable or object in your script. Let us look at a very basic example. The following command calls a public API that returns a random joke: Invoke-RestMethod -Uri "https://official-joke-api.appspot.com/random_joke" The following figure shows the output of the command invoked: Figure 12.1 : Calling APIs with PowerShell You can also save the result into a variable and access the returned values: $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri "https://official-joke-api.appspot.com/random_joke" $response.setup #-&gt; What did the Dorito farmer say to the other Dorito farmer? $response.punchline #-&gt; Cool Ranch! This is one of the best features of Invoke-RestMethod . It removes the need to convert JSON manually. You can treat the API response just like any other object in your script. If you want to send data, for example, to create a new user, you can do it as shown in the following example: $uri = "https://api.example.com/users" $headers = @{ Authorization = "Bearer eyJxyz..." Accept = "application/json" } $body = @{ name = "Ahmed" email = "ahmed @example .com" } | ConvertTo-Json $apiParams = @{ Uri = $uri Method = "Post" Headers = $headers Body = $body ContentType = "application/json" } Invoke-RestMethod @apiParams In this case, we are converting a hash table into JSON using ConvertTo-Json , because the API expects the body in that format. The headers tell the API who we are (with a token), and what kind of data we are sending. You can also use other methods like PUT, PATCH, or DELETE, depending on what the API supports. The structure of the request stays mostly the same. Sometimes, you may want to look at raw JSON yourself, for example, when reading from a file or a web response that is not automatically parsed. In that case, you can still use ConvertFrom-Json , as shown in the following code: $json = Get-Content -Path ".\data.json" -Raw $data = $json | ConvertFrom-Json However, with most APIs, this is not needed. PowerShell does it for you already when using Invoke-RestMethod . To sum up: Use Invoke-RestMethod to send requests and get responses. Pass body data with ConvertTo-Json when sending POST or PUT. Use the returned object just like any other PowerShell variable. No need to parse JSON manually, PowerShell does that for you. API keys and bearer tokens Most APIs are protected. They do not allow just anyone to access their data or services. That is why you usually need to authenticate before using them. In PowerShell, this is done by passing authentication details in the request headers. There are different ways to authenticate, but the most common ones are elaborated upon in the following sub-sections. API keys Some APIs give you a simple key, like a long string, that you must include in every request. You usually add it to the header or as a query parameter. The following example shows the api-key in a header: $headers = @{ "x-api-key" = "1234567890abcdef" } Invoke-RestMethod -Uri "https://api.example.com/data" -Headers $headers It is also possible to add the api-key directly into the query, as shown in the following code: $uri = "https://api.example.com/data?apikey=1234567890abcdef" Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri Always check the API documentation to see how the key should be used. Some use headers, some use query parameters. Bearer tokens Bearer tokens are more secure and often used with services like Microsoft Graph, Azure, or other modern APIs. You usually get the token by signing in or registering an application. The token is then passed in the Authorization header, as shown in the following code: $headers = @{ "Authorization" = "Bearer eyJ0eXAiOiJKV1QiLCJhbGci..." } Invoke-RestMethod -Uri "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/me" -Headers $headers The word Bearer must come before the token. This is required by most APIs that use OAuth. JSON Web Tokens A JSON Web Token ( JWT ) is a compact token that contains information in a small JSON structure. The token is digitally signed, so the receiver can check that the data is correct and has not been changed. A JWT can be signed with a shared secret or with a public and private key pair. JWTs can also be encrypted, but in most automation cases, signed tokens are used. The signature makes it possible to verify who created the token and ensures that the content inside the token is safe to trust. You can find the official Microsoft documentation about JWT access tokens at the following link: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/entra/identity-platform/access-token-claims-reference Note: JWT is not a Microsoft product, but an open standard, and this documentation explains how Microsoft uses that standard in its access tokens. Tips for working with tokens Users need to consider the following factors when working with tokens: Tokens are often valid only for a short time (like one hour). Make sure to refresh them when needed. Never hardcode tokens in scripts that are shared or stored in version control. Use secure methods like Azure managed identity when possible, so you do not have to deal with passwords or secrets. Store a token when working with PowerShell In production, a vault should be used to store tokens or client secrets. For example, with the SecretManagement module. You can find the documentation under the following link: https://learn.microsoft.com/de-de/powershell/module/microsoft.powershell.secretmanagement/?view=ps-modules In the next sample, you can see how to use the PowerShell SecretManagement module to store tokens or secrets: # run only if modules are not installed # Install-Module Microsoft.PowerShell.SecretManagement -Scope CurrentUser # Install-Module Microsoft.PowerShell.SecretStore -Scope CurrentUser Import-Module Microsoft.PowerShell.SecretManagement Import-Module Microsoft.PowerShell.SecretStore # Register vault if (- not (Get-SecretVault -Name "PowerShellVault" -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue)) { Register-SecretVault -Name "PowerShellVault" -ModuleName Microsoft.PowerShell.SecretStore -DefaultVault } #Unlock the Secret Store Unlock-SecretStore # Save the token in the vault Set-Secret -Name "ApiToken" -Secret "eyJ0eXAiOiJKV1QiLCJhbGci" -Vault "PowerShellVault" # List available secrets Get-SecretInfo # Read the token back as plain text $token = Get-Secret -Name "ApiToken" -Vault "PowerShellVault" -AsPlainText SecretManagement does not only work with the built-in local vault. It can also be extended with additional vault modules, which means you can use real, production ready vaults such as Azure Key Vault. These extensions allow the same commands, like Get-Secret and Set-Secret , to work against external secret stores without changing your script. This makes it easy to develop locally with the simple local vault and then switch to a secure cloud vault in production by only changing the vault module and configuration. You can see how to do this following the given link: https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/powershell/utility-modules/secretmanagement/how-to/using-azure-keyvault?view=ps-modules Testing without authentication Some public APIs are openly accessible and do not require any form of authentication. These APIs are ideal for beginners who want to explore how APIs work without needing to worry about tokens or credentials. Examples include APIs for retrieving weather data, exchange rates, or sample JSON structures. These services allow developers to quickly test HTTP methods like GET and POST, understand response formats, and learn how to parse and work with returned data. However, in most production environments and enterprise systems, APIs are protected to ensure that only authorized users or systems can access them. Authentication mechanisms, such as API keys, OAuth tokens, or signed requests, are commonly used to secure access and prevent misuse. Understanding how to securely pass credentials is crucial when moving beyond simple public APIs. This involves learning how to store secrets securely, avoid hardcoding sensitive data in scripts, and use secure communication protocols such as HTTPS to protect credentials in transit. While public, unauthenticated APIs are great for learning, transitioning to secure, authenticated APIs is essential for working with real data and integrating with enterprise systems such as Microsoft Graph, Azure REST APIs, or ServiceNow. Examples of calling Microsoft Graph API Microsoft Graph is a unified API that gives access to all Microsoft 365 services, like users, groups, mailboxes, calendars, Teams, and more. It is one of the most powerful tools you can use in PowerShell when working with the Microsoft cloud. To call the Graph API, you first need to authenticate and get a token. If you are not sure how to set this up, you can review Chapter 8 , Managing Entra ID with PowerShell , especially the section Unattended connection with application permissions , which explains how to register an application in Azure, assign the correct permissions, and configure everything needed for unattended access. Once the app registration is ready, you can generate a token using the clientid , client secret , and tenantid . Here is how to do that with PowerShell: $ApplicationClientId = 'clientid' # Application (Client) ID $ApplicationClientSecret = 'client secret' # Application Secret Value $TenantId = 'tenantid' # Directory (Tenant) ID $TokenUrl = "https://login.microsoftonline.com/$TenantId/oauth2/v2.0/token" $Body = @{ grant_type = 'client_credentials' client_id = $ApplicationClientId client_secret = $ApplicationClientSecret scope = 'https://graph.microsoft.com/.default' } $Response = Invoke-RestMethod -Method Post -Uri $TokenUrl -Body $Body -ContentType 'application/x-www-form-urlencoded' $AccessToken = $Response.access_token After running this, the $AccessToken variable will hold your valid Microsoft Graph token. This token is then passed in the header of each API call. Now that you have your token, you can start calling the Microsoft Graph API. Every request needs the token in the header, as shown here: $headers = @{ Authorization = "Bearer $AccessToken" } Note: Before going on, you need to know that the Graph API works differently from a normal REST API. REST endpoints usually return a fixed response, while the Graph API lets you select, filter, and request only the data you need. This makes the output much more flexible and easier to work with. Get all Entra ID users Let us start with something simple, which is getting a list of all users in Entra ID. Refer to the following code: $uri = "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/users" $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri -Headers $headers $response. value This returns the first 100 users. PowerShell automatically converts the JSON response into an object, and the users are inside the .value property. Get a specific user If you want to get details about one user, just use their user principal name or ID in the URL: $upn = "ahmed@contoso.com" $uri = "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/users/$upn" $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri -Headers $headers $response.displayName $response.jobTitle $response.department This is useful for building admin scripts that look up individual users or sync data from another system. Get user group memberships To see which groups a user is a member of, call the memberOf endpoint, as shown in the following code: $uri = "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/users/$upn/memberOf" $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri -Headers $headers foreach ($ group in $response. value ) { $ group .displayName $ group .id } Create a new user You can also create users using the Graph API. This is done with a POST request and a JSON body. Refer to the following code: $uri = "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/users" $body = @{ accountEnabled = $ true displayName = "Test User" mailNickname = "testuser" userPrincipalName = "testuser@yourtenant.onmicrosoft.com" passwordProfile = @{ forceChangePasswordNextSignIn = $ true password = "SecurePassword123!" } } | ConvertTo-Json -Depth 3 Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri -Headers $headers -Method Post -Body $body -ContentType "application/json" The following figure shows the result after executing the request. The user was created successfully: Figure 12.2 : Successful creation of a user You can also check the Entra ID portal to check if the user is present, as shown in the following figure: Figure 12.3 : Check if the user is present in the Entra ID portal Handle pagination By default, Microsoft Graph only returns a limited number of results per request. Most endpoints will return a maximum of 100 items, even if there are more available. This is done to improve performance and reduce the load on the system. If more results exist, Graph will include a special property in the response called @odata.nextLink . This is a URL that points to the next page of results. You can keep calling this URL until there are no more pages left. PowerShell does not handle this automatically, so you need to write a small loop to fetch all pages. In the following example, you can see how this works: $uri = "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/users" $users = @() do { $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri -Headers $headers # Go through each item in the current page foreach ($user in $response. value ) { $users += $user.displayName } # Get the link to the next page ( if there is one) $uri = $response .' @odata.nextLink ' } while ($uri) $Users.count # result -&gt; 799 Now we have received all 799 users from the Graph API. To sum it up: You start with the initial URL (e.g. all users). You run the request using Invoke-RestMethod . The results are inside $response.value . If the API response includes @odata.nextLink , it means more pages exist. You set $uri to that link and run the loop again. If there is no next link, the loop ends. This is the cleanest way to get all results if there are users, groups, devices, or any other list that could be larger than 100 items. Set page size with top Microsoft Graph also lets you define how many items you want per page using the $top query. For example, use the following code to get 50 items per page: $uri = "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/users?`$top=50" $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri -Headers $headers $response. value .count # result -&gt; 50 However, there is a small detail here. In PowerShell, the dollar sign $ normally means a variable. So, if you write $top , PowerShell will try to find a variable named top . This would cause an error or send the wrong URL. To avoid this, you need to escape the dollar sign by putting a backtick ( `$ ) in front of it, like this: ` $top . This tells PowerShell that you do not mean a variable and want to use the actual characters $top in the string. Always remember the following: Use `$top=50 when building the query string to get only 50 results. This works the same for other query options like $filter , $select , or $orderby . Escaping it correctly keeps your script from breaking and makes sure the API receives the correct parameters. A small mistake that breaks everything In the following example, you will see how a simple mistake, missing one backtick, can cause the Graph API call to fail. This shows just how easy it is to run into problems when using query parameters like $filter in PowerShell. Let us say you want to get all users whose display name starts with the letter A . You might try this: # This looks fine at first, but it will fail $uri = "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/users?$filter=startswith(displayName,'A')" $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri -Headers $headers The preceding code looks correct, but because $filter is not escaped, PowerShell tries to find a variable named filter. If that variable does not exist, the $filter part disappears from the URL completely, or worse, it gets replaced with an empty string or the wrong value. That means the response would contain all users or the first 100 users if paging is not used. The correct version looks almost the same but has a backtick before the $ : # This version works $uri = "https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/users?`$filter=startswith(displayName,'A')" $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri -Headers $headers The response is illustrated in the following figure: Figure 12.4 : Output of the correct code It is just one tiny character, but it makes all the difference. Forgetting the backtick means your filter is never applied, and the results are not what you expect. This is a great example of how easy it is to break an API query in PowerShell without even noticing. So, always remember to escape the $ when using query parameters inside double quotes. If you are not sure, you can print the URL before running the request to double-check it: $uri # this shows the final URL with all variables and escaping in place The following figure shows the content of $uri : Figure 12.5 : Output of $uri Taking a second to verify can save a lot of debugging later. Calling a third-party API PowerShell does not only work with Microsoft APIs. You can also use it to connect to any other service that offers a REST API, like ticketing systems, monitoring tools, human resource ( HR ) platforms, weather services, or even smart home devices. Most of these APIs follow the same basic pattern. Send a request over HTTP and get a response, usually in JSON. To explain how this works, we use a free test API called JSON Placeholder. This API does not need authentication and is perfect for learning. It gives us fake data for posts, comments, users, and tasks (todos). You can use this API to test how PowerShell interacts with other systems. Sending a simple GET request Let us start by reading data. In PowerShell, we use Invoke-RestMethod to call APIs. To keep it simple, we call the /posts endpoint. The following code will return a list of posts as a PowerShell object: $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri 'https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts' -Method GET $response | Format-Table id, title The request does not need authentication or headers. It just pulls a list of blog posts and shows their ID and title. The Invoke-RestMethod cmdlet handles the conversion from JSON into PowerShell objects, so we can work with the data easily. In the following figure, we can see the data returned from the JSON placeholder API: Figure 12.6 : API Each post object contains the fields userId , id , title , and body . We can work with each post like any other PowerShell object, as demonstrated in the following code, where we are using foreach to loop through the response and write the title into the terminal: foreach ($post in $response) { Write-Host "Title: $($post.title)" } This is the base for most API work. Call the endpoint, receive the data, and process it in PowerShell. Sending a POST request with data Now, we create a new post. In the real-world, this could be creating a new task in a ticketing system or adding a record in a database. Here, we just sent some dummy text. We define the data in a PowerShell object and convert it to JSON before sending, as shown here: $postData = { userId = 1 title = "My first blog post from PowerShell" body = "This post was sent using Invoke-RestMethod." } | ConvertTo-Json $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri 'https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts' -Method POST -Body $postData -ContentType 'application/json' $response Even though this API does not store data permanently, it shows how the POST request works. We send the JSON data and receive a response. The server replies with the data we sent and a fake ID. This is very useful when working with real APIs that create tickets, update users, or send messages. Updating data with a PUT request The PUT method is used to update data. In the following example, we update the title of a post, and we send a full post object back to the server, even if only one field changes: $updateData = { userId = 1 id = 1 title = "Updated title via PowerShell" body = "Updated content using a PUT request" } | ConvertTo-Json $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri 'https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts/1' -Method PUT -Body $updateData -ContentType 'application/json' $response This shows how you can update existing records by targeting a specific endpoint with an ID and sending new content. Deleting data with a DELETE request To delete something, use the DELETE method and give the correct resource ID. In this case, we delete the post with ID 1 : Invoke-RestMethod -Uri 'https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts/1' -Method DELETE There is no output if it is successful. In real APIs, you may get a message or a status code. Working with status codes and error handling When calling APIs, it is important to handle errors and check if your call worked. The easiest way to see the HTTP status code is to use Invoke-WebRequest instead of Invoke-RestMethod : $response = Invoke-WebRequest -Uri 'https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts/1' -Method GET $response.StatusCode We can also add a try and catch block to handle if an error happens, as shown in the following: try { $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri 'https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts/invalid' -ErrorAction Stop $response } catch { Write-Host "Request failed: $($_.Exception.Message)" } This makes sure that if something goes wrong, your script does not just crash. Invoke-RestMethod vs. Invoke-WebRequest PowerShell provides two main cmdlets to call APIs. The Invoke-RestMethod and Invoke-WebRequest . Both send HTTP requests, but they behave differently. Invoke-RestMethod is made for working with REST APIs. It automatically converts JSON responses into PowerShell objects. That means when you call an API, and it returns JSON, you can use the data directly like normal PowerShell variables. This makes it easier and faster when you work with APIs that send structured data. Invoke-WebRequest is more general. It gives you the full HTTP response, including headers, cookies, and raw content. It does not convert JSON for you. You have to parse it manually with ConvertFrom-Json . Here, a short example for converting the response from the WebRequest is as follows: $response = Invoke-WebRequest -Uri 'https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts/1' $data = $response.Content | ConvertFrom-Json $data.title Note: Use Invoke-RestMethod when you want to work with API data directly as PowerShell objects. Use Invoke-WebRequest when you need full control over the request and response. Handling errors and logging API responses When working with APIs, things can go wrong. Sometimes the server is down. Sometimes, the request is not accepted. To make your script more stable, it is important to catch these errors and log useful details. Using try and catch With try and catch , you can catch errors and react to them. This avoids crashes and gives you a chance to log what happened. The following code showcases try and catch . It will directly jump into the catch block if an error occurs: try { $response = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri -Method Get -ErrorAction Stop Write-Host "Request successful" $response } catch { Write-Host "Request failed" Write-Host $_.Exception.Message Add-Content -Path "C:\Temp\errorlog.log" -Value ( "[$(Get-Date)] $_" ) } Figure 12.7 shows the logged error in the errorlog file. Make sure the file is created before adding the content: Figure 12.7 : Logged error in the file Handling status code errors Many APIs return a status code in response that tells you what happened. It is a good idea to check the status code before processing the response. The Basics of REST APIs and HTTP methods section in this chapter covered the common status codes. Use the following code, which you can use to check the status code using Invoke-Webrequest and using the status code response to verify if the request was successful or not: try { $response = Invoke-WebRequest -Uri $uri -Method Get -ErrorAction Stop if ($response.StatusCode -eq 200 ) { Write-Host "Request was successful" } else { Write-Host "Unexpected status code: $($response.StatusCode)" } } catch { Write-Host "API call failed: $($_.Exception.Message)" } This gives you more control and helps you handle errors based on the response code. Logging with a function or logging module For better structure, it is a good idea to use a logging function instead of writing Add-Content everywhere. This keeps your code clean and easier to update. Refer to the following example: function Write-Log { param ( [ string ]$Message, [ string ]$Level = "INFO" ) $timestamp = Get-Date -Format "yyyy-MM-dd HH:mm:ss" Add-Content -Path "C:\Temp\log.txt" -Value "[$timestamp] [$Level] $Message" } Now, we can use it as shown in the following example: Write-Log -Message "API call started" Write-Log -Message "API call failed" -Level "ERROR" There are also PowerShell logging modules that offer advanced features like log levels, rotating files, or writing to different outputs. One example is the PowerShell Logging Module available on the PowerShell Gallery, as illustrated in the following figure: Figure 12.8 : Powershell Logging Module Using a function or module keeps your scripts consistent and helps when you need to change how or where logs are written later. Logging the full response Sometimes, you may also want to log the full content of a successful API response. This can help with troubleshooting or auditing. Refer to the following command: $response | ConvertTo-Json -Depth 5 | Out-File -FilePath "c:\temp\apidata.json" -Encoding utf8 -Depth 5 makes sure nested objects are included. You can adjust this number depending on how deep your data goes. By handling errors and writing logs, you make your scripts more reliable. If something fails, you get useful information instead of just a red error message. This is especially important when scripts run in the background or in automation jobs. Best practices for secure and clean API scripts Writing a working script is one thing. Making sure it is secure, easy to maintain, and runs without issues in different environments is just as important. Here are some best practices when working with APIs in PowerShell: Always use error handling : Use try and catch blocks to catch unexpected problems. This helps avoid crashes and gives you a chance to write the error into a log or return useful messages. It is better to know that something failed and why, instead of just guessing. Keep sensitive data out of your script : Avoid putting API keys, tokens, or passwords directly in your scripts. Use environment variables, secure files, or services like Azure Key Vault to store secrets. This protects your data and makes it easier to manage across teams. Use meaningful variable names : Choose variable names that describe what they hold. For example, $apiResponse , $authHeader , or $jsonBody are easier to understand later than $x or $temp . Keep JSON structures readable : When sending data to an API, write the JSON structure as a PowerShell hashtable and then convert it with ConvertTo-Json . This way, it is easier to read and edit the data. Refer to the following example: $body = { name = "New user" email = "user@example.com" role = "admin" } | ConvertTo-Json Validate responses before processing : Always check if the response contains the data you expect. Do not assume a property exists. Use if statements to make sure the data is there. Refer to the following code: if ($response - and $response.name) { Write-Host "User found: $($response.name)" } else { Write-Host "No user found or response was empty" } Add logging and comments : Add comments in your script to explain why something is done a certain way. Add logging where it makes sense, especially for error messages and when sending data. Keep scripts modular : If you call the same API multiple times, create a function and reuse it. This keeps your code clean and easier to manage. Refer to the following code: function Get-WeatherData { param ($city) $uri = "https://api.weather.com/data/$city" $result = Invoke-RestMethod -Uri $uri -Method Get return $result } Use -Depth with ConvertTo-Json : If you are sending or logging complex JSON data, always set the -Depth parameter to a higher number, like 5 or 10 . Otherwise, nested objects may be cut off. An example is as follows: $body | ConvertTo-Json -Depth 5 Following these tips will make your PowerShell API scripts much more reliable, safer, and easier for others (or yourself) to maintain in the future. Conclusion This chapter showed how to work with external APIs using PowerShell. We started with the basics of sending and receiving data using REST methods. We used a public test API so you can try it out without any setup. This chapter covered authentication using API keys and bearer tokens, and how to check status codes to catch errors. It also covered logging using a simple logging function, so you always know what happened and when. Logging is important for later review or debugging. We explained how to write cleaner scripts by keeping secrets out of the code, using better variable names, and checking responses before using them. All these techniques help you build safe and clean scripts that run well in production. You now have the tools to call third-party APIs, handle responses, and keep things under control even when something goes wrong. In the next chapter, we will look at how to use .NET libraries inside PowerShell. PowerShell is built on top of .NET, which means all the features and functions from .NET can be used directly in our scripts. This gives us more control and opens the door to things that are not possible with PowerShell commands alone. You will see how to load .NET classes, call methods, and create objects through real examples like working with dates, files, and network requests using native .NET. This is helpful when there is no PowerShell cmdlet for something or when you need better performance or more advanced options.

PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 13 Using .NET Libraries in PowerShell Introduction PowerShell is built on top of the .NET platform, and that gives us access to a much wider range of tools and features. Many tasks that seem difficult or even impossible in PowerShell alone can be solved easily by calling .NET classes directly. This is especially useful when you need to go beyond standard cmdlets or want more control over how something works. The good thing is you do not have to become a C# developer to use .NET in PowerShell. You can call .NET classes and methods just like you work with any object in PowerShell. In this chapter, we will learn how to use .NET features inside scripts to add more power and flexibility. We will look at common use cases like compressing files into ZIP format, encrypting and securing text, and working with date and time in more advanced ways. These are real tasks that come up often in automation projects. You will learn how to create .NET objects, call their methods, and work with their properties. Everything will be explained in simple steps, with practical examples that show how things work. This will help you solve problems in new ways and give you more tools for your PowerShell toolbox. Once you get used to working with .NET, you will see how it can make your scripts more powerful and more professional. Structure This chapter will cover the following topics: Using .NET classes and methods in PowerShell Compressing files and folders using .NET Securing data using .NET in PowerShell .NET streams and files using PowerShell Best practices for using .NET in PowerShell scripts Objectives The goal of this chapter is to show how .NET can help the readers go further with PowerShell. They will learn how to use built-in .NET classes to perform tasks that are not easily done with cmdlets alone. This includes working with files, compressing folders into ZIP archives, handling secure data, and formatting or calculating with date and time values in more advanced ways. We will use real-world examples that show how these features can be used in daily scripts. By the end of this chapter, they will understand how to access and use .NET types directly in PowerShell, how to pass parameters to methods, and how to get results that they can work with like any other PowerShell object. Readers will also learn when it makes sense to use .NET, and when to stick with built-in PowerShell cmdlets. With this knowledge, their scripts will become more flexible, more secure, and more capable of handling advanced tasks. Using .NET classes and methods in PowerShell PowerShell can directly use any class from the .NET Framework or .NET Core. This provides additional functionality that is not included in PowerShell by default. To use a .NET class, write its full name, usually starting with system, then use the access operator to call static members. To create a new instance, use New-Object or call a .NET factory method such as: [Activator]: CreateInstance() Let us look at an example. The class System.DateTime lets us work with date and time in a much more detailed way than the basic PowerShell Get-Date cmdlet. Calling static members from .NET classes Static members belong to the class itself, not an object. You do not need to create anything; you just call it. This is shown as follows: $currentTime = [System.DateTime]::Now Write-Host "Current date and time: $currentTime" We can also generate random numbers, as shown in the following example: $randomNumber = [System.Math]::Round((Get-Random) * 100 , 2 ) Write-Host "Random number (0 to 100): $randomNumber" Creating instances of .NET classes If you want to use a class that needs to hold values or maintain state, you need to create an object. That is called an instance. Now, we will create a new DateTime object: $customDate = New-Object System.DateTime( 2025 , 12 , 24 , 18 , 30 , 0 ) Write-Host "Custom date: $customDate" The result is shown in the following figure: Figure 13.1 : Creating an instance of a .NET class Exploring .NET objects in PowerShell When working with .NET objects, it is good to explore what they can do. Use Get-Member to see all methods and properties: $currentTime = [System.DateTime]::Now $currentTime | Get-Member The output will show a full list of everything that the DateTime object has. Methods like AddDays() , ToString() , CompareTo() , and properties like Day , Month , and Year . This is how you figure out what is possible with a .NET class in PowerShell. You do not need to memorize everything. Just use Get-Member or search the class name on Microsoft Docs . In the following figure, we can see some of the members that the .NET time object has: Figure 13.2 : Members of .NET time object Working with methods and properties Most of the time, you will use dot notation to work with .NET objects. This is just like PowerShell objects. For example, to use the ToUpper() method on a string, the code is as follows: $text = "hello world" $upperText = $text.ToUpper() Write-Host $upperText Behind the scenes, $text is a .NET string object ( System.String ). You can use any method from the .NET class on it. We can also check if a file exists using .NET, as shown here: $path = "C:\temp\MyFile.txt" $exists = [System.IO.File]::Exists($path) if ($exists) { Write-Host "The file exists." } else { Write-Host "The file was not found." } A very useful .NET function is as follows: $folder = "C:\Logs" $file = "output.log" $fullPath = [System.IO.Path]::Combine($folder, $file) Write-Host "Combined path: $fullPath" We are creating a path by providing the folder path and the filename. The following shows the path: Figure 13.3 : Folder path The next example shows the PowerShell equivalent of the .NET method before, and after demonstrates how paths can be combined safely without relying on string concatenation: $folder = "C:\Logs" $file = "output.log" $fullPath = Join-Path -Path $folder -ChildPath $file Write-Host "Combined path: $fullPath " PowerShell should be used for readability, while .NET is preferred when more control or performance is required. Type casting and conversions Sometimes you need to make sure a value is of a specific .NET type. You can cast a value like this: [ int ]$number = "42" Write-Host "Number: $number" We can also convert a string to a date. That is often necessary when we get JSON objects with time records, which are strings . If we want to use them as time objects, we have to convert the string into a DateTime object. Refer to the following code: $date = [DateTime]::Parse( "2025-30-04" ) Write-Host "Parsed date: $date" There are also safer methods like TryParse() when working with user input or unknown formats. When you get input from a user, from a file, or from an API, the value is often just text, but you might need it as a number or a date. If you use something like [int]$value = "abc" , PowerShell will throw an error because "abc" is not a number. That is where TryParse helps us. TryParse is a method from many .NET types like Int32 , Double , or DateTime . It tries to convert a string into that type. If it works, you get the result. If not, you do not get an error, just a $false , as shown in the following code: $stringValue = "123" [ int ]$output = 0 $success = [ int ]::TryParse($stringValue, [ ref ]$output) if ($success) { Write-Host "Parsed number: $output" } else { Write-Host "Invalid number input" } TryParse checks if $stringValue can be turned into an integer. There are two situations: If yes, $success is $true and $output holds the number. If no, $success is $false and $output stays at 0 . Now, let us try to TryParse dates, as shown here: $dateString = "2025-06-28" [ DateTime ]$parsedDate = Get-Date if ([DateTime]::TryParse($dateString, [ ref ]$parsedDate)) { Write-Host "Date is valid: $parsedDate" } else { Write-Host "Invalid date format" } This is safer than using [DateTime]::Parse() , which would throw an error if the date string is wrong. Compressing files and folders using .NET PowerShell has a built-in cmdlet called Compress-Archive , but sometimes it does not give enough control. If you need better performance or more options, you can use the .NET classes from System.IO.Compression to create or extract ZIP files. This gives you full control and works well in scripts that run on servers or in automation jobs. Creating a ZIP file using .NET We use the System.IO.Compression.ZipFile class to create a ZIP archive from a folder. This class is part of the .NET Framework and gives us an easy way to compress entire folders, including all files and subfolders, into a single ZIP file. This is especially useful in automation tasks, like archiving logs, creating backups, or packaging files before sending them via email. An example is as follows: Add-Type -AssemblyName System.IO.Compression.FileSystem $sourceFolder = "C:\Temp\source" $zipPath = "C:\Temp\Backup.zip" [ System.IO.Compression.ZipFile ]::CreateFromDirectory($sourceFolder, $zipPath) Write-Host "ZIP file created at $zipPath" The method CreateFromDirectory() takes two main inputs: the folder you want to compress and the path where the ZIP file should be saved. You do not need to loop through files manually. The method handles all files and folders inside the source directory. The following figure shows the newly created Backup.zip file: Figure 13.4 : Creation of the Backup.zip file Extracting a ZIP file using .NET Unzipping an archive is also straightforward when using the .NET class System.IO.Compression.ZipFile . Just like creating a ZIP file, extracting one is simple and requires only one method call. The method is called ExtractToDirectory() , and it takes two inputs—the path to the ZIP file and the folder where you want the contents to be unpacked, refer to the following code to build a comprehensive understanding: Add-Type -AssemblyName System.IO.Compression.FileSystem $zipPath = "C:\Temp\Backup.zip" $extractFolder = "C:\Temp\RestoredBackup" [ System.IO.Compression.ZipFile ]::ExtractToDirectory($zipPath, $extractFolder) Write-Host "ZIP file extracted to $extractFolder" Securing data using .NET in PowerShell In many automation tasks, we need to work with sensitive data. This could be passwords, authentication tokens, API keys, or any personal information that should not be stored as plain text. PowerShell has some basic tools for handling this, like ConvertTo-SecureString , but those are mostly useful when storing data locally on the same machine and are not made for more complex tasks like sending encrypted content across systems or storing it in external files. When you need to encrypt and decrypt data with real encryption algorithms, the .NET libraries give you exactly that. The namespace System.Security.Cryptography in .NET provides strong, reliable tools for encryption. It supports common algorithms like Advanced Encryption Standard ( AES ), which is used worldwide and is safe for real use. Using .NET for encryption gives you full control. You can define your own key and initialization vector ( IV ), choose the cipher mode, and decide how the data is stored or transferred. This makes it ideal for automation jobs that handle secure input, create encrypted backups, or pass secure tokens between systems. One of the most common patterns is encrypting a string using AES, saving the result in a base64 format (so you can store or send it as text), and decrypting it later using the same key and IV. That is exactly what we will do in the following example. Let us define two small functions. One for encrypting a string, and another for decrypting it. They both use the AES algorithm from .NET, and everything is handled directly in PowerShell: function Encrypt-Text { param ( [ string ]$plainText, [ byte[ ]]$key, [ byte[ ]]$iv ) $aes = [System.Security.Cryptography.AesManaged]:: new () $aes.Key = $key $aes.IV = $iv $encryptor = $aes.CreateEncryptor() $buffer = [System.Text.Encoding]::UTF8.GetBytes($plainText) $encryptedBytes = $encryptor.TransformFinalBlock($buffer, 0 , $buffer.Length) [ Convert ]::ToBase64String($encryptedBytes) } This function takes three inputs, which include the text to encrypt, a key, and an IV. Both the key and IV are byte arrays and must have correct lengths for AES (usually 16 bytes for 128-bit encryption). To decrypt it, we use the same key and IV, as shown in the following code: function Decrypt-Text { param ( [ string ]$cipherText, [ byte[ ]]$key, [ byte[ ]]$iv ) $aes = [System.Security.Cryptography.AesManaged]:: new () $aes.Key = $key $aes.IV = $iv $decryptor = $aes.CreateDecryptor() $encryptedBytes = [Convert]::FromBase64String($cipherText) $plainBytes = $decryptor.TransformFinalBlock($encryptedBytes, 0 , $encryptedBytes.Length) [ System.Text.Encoding ]::UTF8.GetString($plainBytes) } Let us generate a sample key and IV and test the functions, as shown here: $key = ( 1..16 ) | ForEach-Object { Get-Random -Minimum 0 -Maximum 256 } $iv = ( 1..16 ) | ForEach-Object { Get-Random -Minimum 0 -Maximum 256 } $secret = "My super secret text" $encrypted = Encrypt-Text -plainText $secret -key $key -iv $iv Write-Host "Encrypted: $encrypted" $decrypted = Decrypt-Text -cipherText $encrypted -key $key -iv $iv Write-Host "Decrypted: $decrypted" This lets you protect any string with real encryption. The result can be saved or sent securely. Just remember, without the correct key and IV, you cannot decrypt the text. Note: In automation, encryption is useful when working with sensitive data such as API tokens, personal records, or important configuration values. It is often used to protect data before it is written to a log file or sent between systems. Backups and reports are also encrypted when they contain information that should not be visible to everyone. Encryption ensures that even if someone gets the data, it cannot be read without the correct key. This only works when the key is stored safely. If the key or the IV is hardcoded, shared, or placed in an open location, the encryption becomes useless. Keys and IVs should never be stored in a repository. The best practice is to use a vault to store sensitive information, because encryption is not a replacement for a vault. A vault is still needed as the secure place for secrets, while encryption adds extra protection for the data that your automation scripts process or transfer. .NET streams and files using PowerShell PowerShell gives us simple commands like Get-Content , Set-Content , and Add-Content to work with files. These are great for quick tasks, like reading a config file or writing some output. However, when you need more control, like writing large amounts of data, handling binary content, or working with files that should not be fully loaded into memory, then .NET provides much better tools. .NET gives access to classes like StreamWriter , StreamReader , FileStream , and BinaryReader , which let you read or write data exactly how you want. For example, you can open a file once, keep the stream open, write line-by-line, and close it only when you are done. This is helpful when building logs, writing reports, exporting large data sets, or handling memory-sensitive tasks. Writing to a file with StreamWriter The class System.IO.StreamWriter gives you full control when writing text to a file. It lets you open a file once, write multiple lines, and choose the encoding. This is faster and safer than calling Add-Content repeatedly in a loop. The same is shown in the following sample code: $logPath = "C:\Temp\stream-log.txt" $stream = New-Object System.IO.StreamWriter($logPath, $ true , [System.Text.Encoding]::UTF8) $stream.WriteLine( "Log entry started at $(Get-Date)" ) $stream.WriteLine( "Something important happened" ) $stream.WriteLine( "Log entry ended at $(Get-Date)" ) $stream.Close() The second parameter $true means append to the file instead of overwriting. This is useful for log files that grow over time. Reading from a file with StreamReader To read a file line-by-line using .NET, we use System.IO.StreamReader , as shown here: $reader = [System.IO.StreamReader]:: new ( "C:\Temp\stream-log.txt" ) while (!$reader.EndOfStream) { $line = $reader.ReadLine() Write-Host "Line: $line" } $reader.Close() This is great for large files that you do not want to fully load into memory. It reads one line at a time, which keeps memory usage low and performance stable, especially when working with log files, export files, or reports that are several megabytes or even gigabytes. Instead of opening the entire file at once, the stream reads each line only when it is needed. This approach is safer on servers or remote systems where memory is limited, and it also helps avoid script timeouts or crashes in long-running automation tasks. It gives you full control over how the data is processed, so you can scan, filter, or process each line as it comes in without waiting for the whole file to load. Working with binary files When handling binary data, like images, documents, audio files, or any non-text format, use the .NET FileStream or file helper methods in System.IO.File . PowerShell’s normal file cmdlets, like Get-Content and Set-Content , are made for text and may corrupt binary data if used incorrectly. That is why .NET is the safer and more reliable way when working with binary files. The following code sample reads the full content of a binary file into a byte array: $bytes = [System.IO.File]::ReadAllBytes( "C:\temp\source\AA.png" ) Write-Host "Loaded $($bytes.Length) bytes" This method loads the full file as raw bytes. The result is an array of numbers (each byte from the file), and you can work with it directly in memory. You can also write that same byte array back to a new file, as shown here: [ System.IO.File ]::WriteAllBytes( "C:\temp\source\AA-copy.png" , $bytes) The copied picture is shown in the following figure: Figure 13.5 : Copied picture This is perfect for copying or moving binary files in scripts, or for saving data you have received from an API or generated inside your automation logic. You can also use this method for backing up configuration files, exporting attachments from a database, or writing encrypted blobs. Since the content is never interpreted as text, it stays 100% clean and unmodified. For large binary files or custom reading logic, you can use the FileStream to read or write part of the file in chunks. This is useful when you do not want to load the full file into memory, or when you want to process data as it comes in, similar to how you handle large logs with StreamReader . Using .NET for string and data conversion In PowerShell, working with strings is simple for basic tasks. However, when you need more control, like formatting numbers, converting between types, changing cultures, or building strings efficiently, .NET gives you much more power. One very useful .NET class for string handling is System.Text.StringBuilder . It is designed to build or modify large strings without slowing down your script. Unlike regular string concatenation, which creates a new string every time, StringBuilder keeps everything in memory and adds to the same object. This makes it ideal when you need to build long logs, reports, or structured content like JSON or CSV lines. You can also use .NET to convert data between formats. For example, turning a number into a currency string, changing a date format, converting between bytes and base64, or formatting a decimal with a specific number of digits. These conversions are handled by .NET methods like ToString("format") , Convert.ToBase64String() , and BitConverter . In the example that follows, we will see how to use StringBuilder to build strings efficiently and then explore some useful formatting and conversion techniques using .NET. Building strings with StringBuilder In PowerShell, you can join strings using + or by using string interpolation like “$var something else.” This works fine when you only need to build a few lines. However, if you are building a large string with many parts, like generating a full HTML report, a big JSON file, or a log with thousands of lines, basic string operations can slow down your script. That is because strings in .NET are immutable, which means every time you change or add something, a new string is created in memory. This gets slow and eats up memory when done many times. The .NET class System.Text.StringBuilder was made to solve that. It lets you build a string step-by-step in the same memory space, which is faster and cleaner. Refer to the following example: $stringBuilder = New-Object System.Text.StringBuilder [ void ]$stringBuilder.AppendLine( "Report Summary" ) [ void ]$stringBuilder.AppendLine( "---------------" ) [ void ]$stringBuilder.AppendLine( "Date: $(Get-Date)" ) [ void ]$stringBuilder.AppendLine( "Status: Completed" ) $finalText = $stringBuilder.ToString() Write-Host $finalText Notice the use of [void] , this hides the output of the AppendLine() method, which normally returns the updated object. It is not required, but it keeps the script output clean. You should use StringBuilder when generating output line-by-line, especially inside a loop, or when working with large sets of strings such as logs, HTML, JSON, or even dynamically built code. It is also helpful when performance and memory usage matter, like in large automation scripts. Instead of joining strings repeatedly, which creates new objects every time, StringBuilder lets you keep adding content in parts using the same memory space. This makes it ideal for building things like large CSV exports from databases, structured email bodies, or any output that grows step-by-step during a script run. The next example illustrates how to create a CSV file by building the content through a StringBuilder : $folder = "C:\Logs" $file = "report.csv" $path = Join-Path $folder $file # we are creating the folder if it does not exist if (-not (Test-Path $folder )) { New-Item -Path $folder -ItemType Directory | Out-Null } # then we starting to Build the CSV $stringBuilder = New-Object System.Text.StringBuilder # Header [ void ] $stringBuilder .AppendLine( "Name,Age,City" ) # Rows [ void ] $stringBuilder .AppendLine( "John,32,London" ) [ void ] $stringBuilder .AppendLine( "Anna,27,Berlin" ) [ void ] $stringBuilder .AppendLine( "Mark,45,Salzburg" ) # Convert to text $csvText = $stringBuilder .ToString() # Save the file $csvText | Set-Content -Path $path -Encoding UTF8 Write-Host "CSV created at: $path " Formatting and converting data using .NET In automation, data often needs to be shown or saved in a very specific way, whether it is a number that should show two decimal places, a date that should follow a particular format, or an ID that needs to be encoded before sending it to a web API. One of the most powerful and commonly used methods in .NET is .ToString("format") . This method works across many data types like numbers, dates, and even Booleans. You can control exactly how the output looks, which is important when sending data to systems that expect a certain structure, or when generating logs and reports that need to be clear and readable. For example, when working with numbers, you may want to show a currency value, round a number to two decimal places, or pad a number with leading zeros. With dates, you might need different formats depending on your region or the system you are sending the data to. Some systems want yyyy-MM-dd , others prefer MM/dd/yyyy , and some require timestamps like yyyy-MM-ddTHH:mm:ss . The good thing is that .NET formatting methods are very flexible and easy to use in PowerShell. You do not need to write long custom functions. You just call .ToString() with the right pattern. These small formatting changes can make a big difference in real-world scripts. They help prevent errors, improve readability, and make your automation feel more professional and well thought out. Formatting numbers Now, we will show some typical use cases for formatting numbers in scripts. Refer to the following code: $price = 1999.5 $currencyFormat = $price.ToString( "C" ) Write-Host "Price: $currencyFormat" This prints the value with a currency symbol and formatting based on your system settings. On a German system, it might show 1.999,50 €, on a US system, it will show $1,999.50. The following code limits the output to exactly two digits after the decimal point, which is great for clean output: $number = 3.14159 $rounded = $number.ToString( "F2" ) Write-Host "Rounded: $rounded" Formatting dates Dates and times often need to be shown in different formats depending on the target system or region. Refer to the following example: $now = [DateTime]::Now $isoDate = $now.ToString( "yyyy-MM-dd" ) Write-Host "ISO Date: $isoDate" # -&gt; ISO Date: 2025-06-27 # US style format $usDate = $now.ToString( "MM/dd/yyyy" ) Write-Host "US Format: $usDate" # -&gt; US Format: 06/27/2025 # Full timestamp $timestamp = $now.ToString( "yyyy-MM-ddTHH:mm:ss" ) Write-Host "Timestamp: $timestamp" # -&gt; Timestamp: 2025-06-27T14:30:00 # Human-readable $longDate = $now.ToString( "dddd, MMMM dd, yyyy" ) Write-Host "Long Date: $longDate" # -&gt; Long Date: Friday, June 27, 2025 Each format serves a different purpose. Use ISO or timestamp formats when saving to files, naming backups, or sending to APIs. Use human-readable formats for display or notification emails. These formatting tools might look small, but they have a big impact on how clear and professional your output feels. They also help reduce errors when working with systems that expect data in a fixed structure. Converting between bytes and base64 strings In automation, there are many cases where you need to work with raw data, like file content, passwords, images, or binary exports. Sometimes you cannot send this data directly, especially if you are working with APIs or need to store it in a text-based system like a JSON file or a database. This is where base64 encoding comes in. Base64 takes binary data and converts it into a plain string that contains only safe characters. This string can be stored or sent without problems. On the receiving side, you can decode it back into the original byte data. .NET makes this easy with two methods, which are Convert.ToBase64String() and Convert.FromBase64String() . Convert bytes to base64 Now, we will read a file into memory as bytes: $path = "C:\temp\source\AA.png" $bytes = [System.IO.File]::ReadAllBytes($path) $base64 = [Convert]::ToBase64String($bytes) Write-Host "Base64 string (first 100 chars): $($base64.Substring(0,100))" Now, the file content is saved in a plain string. You can write this to a log, send it to an API, or include it in a JSON body. This is often done when uploading documents or images via REST APIs that expect base64 input. The following figure shows the output of the preceding code: Figure 13.6 : Converting bytes into base64 Convert base64 back to bytes When the file needs to be restored, decode the string and write the bytes back to disk as shown here: $decodedBytes = [Convert]::FromBase64String($base64) [ System.IO.File ]::WriteAllBytes( "C:\temp\source\AA-decoded.png" , $decodedBytes) Write-Host "File has been restored" This process is clean and reversible. The original file and the restored one are exactly the same. To ensure numbers maintain a uniform format with leading zeros, especially in scenarios like ticketing systems or file naming, you can use .NET’s numeric formatting in PowerShell, as shown here: $invoiceNumber = 42 $padded = $invoiceNumber.ToString( "D5" ) Write-Host "Invoice Number: $padded" This will output 00042 , which is helpful for IDs, ticket numbers, or filenames that should always have a consistent length. Best practices to use .NET in PowerShell scripts Using .NET inside PowerShell gives you more power, but it also adds complexity. To keep your scripts clean, safe, and easy to maintain, it is important to follow a few best practices, which will help you avoid confusion, reduce errors, and make sure others can understand your code too, even months or years later. First, only use .NET when you really need the extra control or functionality. If PowerShell has a simple cmdlet that solves your task, use that, but if you need better performance, advanced formatting, or low-level control, then .NET is the right tool. Next, keep your code readable. Always use clear variable names and add comments when something might not be obvious. If you are calling a .NET method that is not commonly used, add a short comment above it explaining what it does or why you are using it. When creating objects, prefer using New-Object or ::new() for clarity, and avoid overly complex one-liners. Use helper functions if you need to repeat the same .NET code in multiple places. This makes your script modular and easier to manage. Always check the documentation if you are not sure how a class works. The official .NET docs are detailed and show every method, parameter, and return type. When possible, test in small steps and use Get-Member to explore an object before using it. Finally, be careful when mixing .NET with PowerShell output. Some .NET methods return objects that are not formatted well for display. Use ToString() when needed and always test your output before sending it to logs, emails, or other systems. Conclusion This chapter showed how .NET and PowerShell work hand in hand. You learned how to go beyond built-in cmdlets by calling .NET classes directly, giving you access to powerful tools for tasks like formatting data, compressing files, encrypting strings, and working with streams and binary content. We walked through real examples and explained how and when to use each feature. Using .NET in PowerShell can make your scripts faster, more flexible, and more professional. With a bit of practice, working with .NET becomes a natural part of your scripting process. It lets you solve more advanced problems without needing to leave PowerShell. In the next chapter, we will focus on remote management and how to do it securely with PowerShell. You will learn how to manage remote systems using PowerShell remoting, how to setup Just Enough Administration ( JEA ), and how to create constrained endpoints to limit what users can do. These techniques help you control access, reduce risks, and keep systems secure, even when they are managed from a distance. Real examples will show how this works in practice, such as giving contractors limited access without exposing the full system.
