PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 14 Remote Management and Security with PowerShell Introduction Remote management is one of the key features that PowerShell delivers. No one has time to log in to every server by hand. PowerShell remoting makes this easy. It allows you to run commands or scripts on other computers without sitting in front of them. This saves time and is much faster than doing things manually. However, with remote access comes risk. You do not want to give full admin rights to everyone. That is where constrained endpoints and Just Enough Administration ( JEA ) come in. These features help you lock down what others can do when they connect remotely. You can give them access to only the tasks they need, and nothing more. We will start with PowerShell remoting basics. Then we will learn to build a constrained endpoint, which is a special PowerShell session where you control which commands are allowed. You will learn how to use Constrained Language Model, which limits advanced scripting features and adds another layer of protection. After that, we introduce JEA, which is a framework from Microsoft that builds on constrained endpoints. It adds a clear way to define roles and permissions and link them to users or groups. With JEA, you can give people just enough access to do their job, without exposing the full system. We will also build a real example where a service desk team can restart services on remote machines, but they cannot run other commands. This shows how to give helpful access without giving too much control. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: PowerShell remoting basics Enabling and configuring PowerShell remoting Connecting to remote machines Working with constrained endpoints Understanding Constrained Language Model Just Enough Administration Constrained endpoints vs. JEA Objectives This chapter will show how to use PowerShell remoting in a secure way. The readers will first learn how to enable and use remoting to manage computers without logging in directly. Then we move to security features like constrained endpoints, which let them build locked down PowerShell sessions where only certain commands are allowed. Next, we look at Constrained Language Model, which stops advanced features like script blocks and .NET access. It adds extra safety when letting others connect remotely. After that, we dive into JEA. Readers will learn how to split access into roles and create clear rules for who can do what. JEA makes it easy to control tasks like restarting services or running specific scripts, and keeps everything else blocked. The chapter ends with a full example giving a service desk team the right to restart services on remote computers. This shows how they can give useful access without giving full admin rights. They will learn how to create the endpoint, set the permissions, test it, and keep it secure. PowerShell remoting basics PowerShell remoting is one of the most used features in PowerShell. It allows you to run commands or entire scripts on other computers without having to physically access them or open a remote desktop connection. Instead of clicking through a graphical interface or logging in to every machine one-by-one, you can open your terminal and manage everything from one place. This is especially useful in large environments where you have many servers or client machines that need to be updated, checked, or fixed regularly. With remoting, you can run a script on 50 machines at the same time and collect results in seconds. That saves a lot of time and removes the chance for human error that often comes with manual work. PowerShell remoting uses a technology called Windows Remote Management ( WinRM ), which is a service in Windows that handles remote communication. When remoting is enabled, it listens for incoming connections and lets you run PowerShell commands in a secure way. The connection is encrypted, and you can control who is allowed to connect and what they can do once connected. You can use PowerShell remoting in two main ways, depending on what you need to do. The first method is called one-to-one remoting . This means you open a remote session to a single machine and work inside that session, almost like you are sitting at that machine. Every command you type runs directly on the remote computer, and you see the output in your local terminal. The second method is called one-to-many remoting . In this case, you send the same command to multiple remote machines at once. PowerShell takes care of connecting to each machine, running the command, and bringing all the results back to you in one combined list. This is very helpful when you want to check something or apply a change across several systems quickly and consistently. These two modes cover most remote tasks, from managing a single server to updating all machines in a department. Before using PowerShell remoting, you need to make sure it is enabled and properly configured. In the next section, we will walk through how to do that step-by-step, so you can start using remoting in your own environment safely and easily. Enabling and configuring PowerShell remoting Before remote PowerShell sessions can be used, PowerShell remoting must be enabled on the systems that will be managed. This feature allows commands or scripts to be run from one computer on another one, without using Remote Desktop or third-party tools. PowerShell Remoting is built on top of WinRM, which is a Microsoft service used for remote communication. On Windows Server, remoting is often already enabled by default. On Windows client systems, such as Windows 10 or Windows 11, it usually needs to be turned on manually. Once enabled, the system becomes ready to receive remote PowerShell connections from other authorized computers. The following command enables remoting on a local machine: Enable-PSRemoting -Force The -Force switch skips the confirmation prompt and applies all settings directly. This command is performing some changes in the background. If the WinRM service is not already running, it will be started and set to start automatically whenever the system boots. A network listener is setup to accept incoming connections on port 5985 (HTTP). This is the standard port for basic remoting. For secure connections using HTTPS, port 5986 is used. A firewall rule is added automatically to allow incoming traffic on port 5985 , but only for private or domain networks. Trusted hosts for workgroup machines If the computers are not joined to the same domain, and instead are part of a workgroup, then WinRM may block connections because the machines do not trust each other by default. In these cases, it is necessary to add the remote computer name (or IP address) to the trusted hosts list. This can be done using the following command: Set-Item WSMan:\localhost\Client\TrustedHosts "RemoteComputerName" In the example above, RemoteComputerName is a placeholder that must be replaced with the actual name of the remote computer. PowerShell versions and remoting When PowerShell remoting is enabled using a specific PowerShell version, it is only enabled for that version. For example, if the Enable-PSRemoting command is run in PowerShell 7, it will only configure remoting for PowerShell 7 sessions and not for Windows PowerShell 5.1. Each version maintains its own remoting configuration. Connecting to remote machines Once PowerShell remoting is enabled and properly configured, it becomes possible to connect to other systems and run commands as if working on them directly. This is useful for quick tasks and large automation jobs across many machines. There are two main ways to connect to a remote computer using PowerShell, which will be discussed in the following sub-sections. Interactive session For working directly on one machine in an interactive way, the Enter-PSSession command is used. This creates a live remote session where commands run just like on a local terminal. The session prompt changes to show that the commands are being run on the remote system. In the following figure, we enter our RemotingServer DC01 : Figure 14.1 : PowerShell remoting server DC01 Once connected, any command typed is sent and executed on the remote computer. To exit the session and return to the local shell, use Exit-PSSession . One-to-many remoting To send a command to multiple machines at once, Invoke-Command is used. This command takes a list of target computers and a script block. PowerShell will run the code on each machine and return the results in a combined list, as shown in the following figure: Figure 14.2 : Output of Invoke-Command This is useful for gathering information, restarting services, or applying changes to many systems at the same time. Using credentials When running remote commands in a domain environment, authentication usually works automatically if the current user account has permission to access the remote system. However, in many environments, especially when working across domains, in workgroups, or when using different service accounts, the remote computer may not recognize the logged-in user. In such cases, credentials must be provided manually. This can be done using the -Credential parameter along with the Get-Credential cmdlet, which opens a secure prompt for entering a username and password. Refer to the following example: $cred = Get-Credential Invoke-Command -ComputerName DC01 -Credential $cred -ScriptBlock { whoami } The username entered can be something like DEMO\max.mustermann , depending on the setup. However, it is important to know that providing credentials alone is not enough. The remote system must also be configured to allow that user to connect. By default, only members of the Administrators group or the Remote Management Users group are allowed to use PowerShell remoting. To test this, a domain user named max.mustermann was created. This user was not added to the Remote Management Users group at first. When attempting to run this command with Max’s credentials, the following happened: Figure 14.3 : Error when running PowerShell remoting without permissions The connection failed with an access denied error. This shows that even though the credentials were valid, the user did not have permission to connect remotely through PowerShell. Next, the user max.mustermann was added to the Remote Management Users group in Active Directory, as shown in the following figure: Figure 14.4 : Adding a user to the Remote Management Users group After adding the user to the group, the same command was run again. This time, the connection worked successfully, and the output from the whoami command was returned from the remote system. This example shows clearly that credentials alone are not enough. The remote computer must also explicitly allow the user to connect. For non-admin users, this means they must be part of the Remote Management Users group. This step is often forgotten and can cause confusion when setting up remoting for the first time. Creating and reusing sessions In many cases, it is useful to create a session once and then reuse it for multiple commands. This is especially helpful when working on the same remote machine again and again, or when performance matters, and opening a new connection each time would slow things down. To create a reusable remote session, the New-PSSession cmdlet is used: $RemotingServer = "DC01" $session = New-PSSession - ComputerName $ RemotingServer This creates a session object that holds the connection to the remote machine: Figure 14.5 : Creating a reusable remote session Now, the session is saved in the $session variable and can be used in other remoting commands. Instead of providing a computer name each time, the session object is passed directly. Refer to the following command: Invoke-Command -Session $session -ScriptBlock { Get-ComputerInfo } The Invoke-Command will run the command Get-Computerinfo on DC01 using the existing session. There is no need to reconnect or reauthenticate. The following figure illustrates an example: Figure 14.6 : PowerShell remoting Once we are finished with all tasks, the session should be closed to free up system resources. This can be done by running the following command: Remove-PSSession -Session $session Every time a session is created using New-PSSession , it uses memory and network resources on the local machine and the remote computer. If the session is not removed, it will stay open in the background until it times out (two hours) or is manually closed. This can cause problems, especially when many sessions are left open over time. For example, too many open sessions can slow down the system, cause WinRM errors, or hit the maximum allowed session limit on the remote machine. In production environments or when using scripts that run on many machines, always closing the session properly is a good habit. It keeps things clean and avoids hitting resource or connection limits, especially in environments with strict security or performance rules. In general, cleaning up sessions is just like closing files or stopping services after use. It keeps the system stable and avoids trouble later. Open sessions can be listed using the following command: Get-PSSession This shows all active sessions created in the current PowerShell session. It is a good way to check if there are still open connections that were not closed properly. To close all open sessions at once, the following command can be used: Get-PSSession | Remove- PSSession However, be careful with this. This command removes all active sessions, including ones that might still be in use. It should only be used when it is clear that no one else is working on the same server or that no important automation is running in the background. In shared or production environments, it is always better to remove only the specific session that was created. This cleanup helps avoid leaving behind unused sessions, which can consume system resources and eventually hit session limits. Working with constrained endpoints PowerShell remoting is powerful, but it also opens up risks if not managed carefully. When remoting is enabled, users who have permission can run many commands on remote systems. If a user has too many rights, it can lead to mistakes or security issues. That is why PowerShell allows creating constrained endpoints, special locked down sessions that only allow certain commands or actions. A constrained endpoint is a custom PowerShell session that limits what a user can do when connected remotely. It is like giving someone a toolbox with only the tools they need and nothing else. This makes it much safer to give access to non-admin users or external staff, like helpdesk or contractors. By default, a user with remoting access can do everything their permissions allow. If they are a local admin, they have full control. Even standard users can still run commands that may expose system information. With constrained endpoints, it becomes possible to build a controlled environment where only safe and approved actions are allowed. Here are some examples of what can be controlled with constrained endpoints: Which cmdlets can be used (like only Get-Service , Restart-Service ). Which modules are available. The scripts or advanced code that can be executed. Which users or groups are allowed to connect. What PowerShell language features are available. Working of constrained endpoints A constrained endpoint is created by registering a PowerShell session configuration. This is defined in a .pssc file, which contains the rules for the session, what the user can run, what is visible, and what the session allows. Once registered, a user connects to the endpoint using the -ConfigurationName parameter, shown as follows: Enter-PSSession -ComputerName DC01 -ConfigurationName LimitedSession The session will open, but only the allowed cmdlets and features are available. We will now create a session configuration for our limited session. Creating a constrained endpoint To start, a session configuration file needs to be created using the New-PSSessionConfiguration File cmdlet on the remoting server. Here is a basic example that only allows Get-Service and Restart-Service : New-PSSessionConfigurationFile ` - SessionType RestrictedRemoteServer ` - VisibleCmdlets 'Get-Service' , 'Restart-Service' ` -Path 'C:\LimitedSession.pssc' The preceding code creates a configuration file named LimitedSession.pssc . It defines a restricted session where only those two cmdlets are allowed. Other commands will be blocked, and advanced features like calling .NET methods or loading external modules are not available. Figure 14.7 shows the created session configuration file. Note that the # sign means it is commented out. Only configuration lines without # will be used. Figure 14.7 : Session configuration file After the .pssc file has been created, the next step is to register it so that the system knows about it and others can start using it. This is like telling the system, here is a new remote session type, allow connections to it with these rules . The command is as follows: Register-PSSessionConfiguration -Name LimitedSession -Path 'C:\LimitedSession.pssc' This registers a new endpoint with the name LimitedSession . Once it is registered, it appears in the list of session configurations, which can be checked with Get-PSSessionConfiguration . Be aware that Register-PSSessionConfiguration may need to restart the WinRM service to work correctly. The following figure shows the registered PSSessionConfiguration : Figure 14.8 : PSSessionConfiguration Now, when specifying -ConfigurationName LimitedSession , they will enter that special session instead of the default one: $RemotingServer = "DC01" Enter-PSSession -ComputerName $RemotingServer -ConfigurationName LimitedSession Inside this session, only the commands that were defined in the .pssc file will work. Everything else is blocked. If a user tries to run something like Get-Process , and it is not allowed, the result will be the same as in the following figures: Figure 14.9 : Blocked command execution when attempting to run unauthorized commands in a constrained session The next figure shows that we also have no access to the dir command, which would normally list all files in the directory: Figure 14.10 : Denied directory listing caused by restricted access in the PowerShell remoting session The preceding figure shows that only the Get-Service command works. We tried to execute a parameter, -first , to show only the first 20 Services, but that is also restricted. That means the user can only execute specified commands without any parameters. When using Get-Command in the session, we can clearly see what functions and cmdlets are allowed. An example is illustrated in the following figure: Figure 14.11 : Output of Get-Command This might seem strict, but that is exactly the point. To build a safe place where users can only do what they are trusted to do. In many environments, even trusted users do not need full access to a system. Most of the time, they only need to perform one or two specific tasks, like restarting a service, checking the status of something, or running a report. If users are allowed into a full PowerShell session without any limits, there is always a risk, not only from bad intentions but also from simple mistakes. A small typo or a copy-paste error could stop a critical service, delete files, or change something important. In shared environments or production systems, even one wrong command can cause serious damage. These restrictions also help if a user account becomes compromised, because the limited session reduces the risk of exposure and prevents an attacker from running harmful commands. Constrained endpoints help avoid this. They give users a safe space where only the commands that are needed for the job are available. Everything else is hidden or blocked. There is no access to sensitive parts of the system, no way to load dangerous modules, and no way to run complex scripts that could cause harm. Think of it like letting someone borrow a toolbox that only has a screwdriver and a flashlight, just the tools they need to tighten a few screws and check something. There are no chainsaws or blowtorches inside that could accidentally break something. This kind of design makes remote access much safer and easier to control. Assigning access to the endpoint Let us imagine a situation where there is a service desk team that needs to restart a Windows service when it stops working. They are not admins, and they should not have access to things like restarting the firewall or uninstalling software. They just need permissions to perform one task, which is to restart a specific service. This is where the constrained endpoint comes in. The right commands are allowed, and the endpoint is ready, but the service desk team still cannot use it unless access is given. By default, only administrators can use new session configurations. So, the final step is to assign the correct permissions to the users or groups who are allowed to connect, as shown here: Set-PSSessionConfiguration -Name LimitedSession -ShowSecurityDescriptorUI This opens a window where access control can be configured, as shown in the following figure: Figure 14.12 : Configuring access controls From here, it is possible to add groups like DEMO\ServiceDesk and give the members in the group read and execute permissions. This means they can open the session and run the allowed commands, and nothing more. This small but powerful step is what makes it possible to safely delegate tasks without giving away full system access. So, it is not necessary to give every user who needs remote access full remoting rights on the server by adding them to the Remote Management Users group. Instead, access can be controlled more safely through the session configuration itself. With a constrained endpoint, specific users or groups can be given permissions to connect to only that custom session, without touching the default remoting setup. This way, they cannot accidentally connect to the default full access session like Microsoft.PowerShell , where everything is open. This approach is much safer because it puts the control exactly where it is needed. Only the approved session is available, and only the allowed commands inside that session can be used. It reduces risk, avoids giving away broad permissions, and makes it much easier to follow security policies in larger environments. It is also a great solution for automated systems and unattended tasks, where scripts are triggered by service accounts or group Managed Service Accounts ( gMSAs ). If such an account were ever compromised, the attacker would still be locked into the restricted session and could only run the specific commands that were permitted in the session configuration, nothing more. There would be no access to sensitive functions, external commands, or dangerous scripting features. Disabling or removing an endpoint Sometimes, a custom session is only needed for a short project or test case. Once it is no longer needed, it should be turned off or removed to keep the system clean. To disable the endpoint, use the following command: Disable-PSSessionConfiguration -Name LimitedSession This makes the configuration unavailable, but will not delete it. To remove the configuration, the following command is used: Unregister-PSSessionConfiguration -Name LimitedSession This will unregister the session and delete it from the system. The .pssc file will still be there unless deleted manually. Constrained endpoints are a powerful way to give people just the access they need, and nothing more. It is like setting up a secure workbench with only the right tools. When someone connects, they can do their job without the risk of breaking something else. Before moving on to JEA, there is one more important topic: the Constrained Language Model feature, which adds another layer of safety by limiting what PowerShell can do. It is especially useful in locked down environments or when working with sensitive systems. Understanding Constrained Language Model PowerShell is a very powerful scripting language. It can run native commands, call .NET methods, access the file system, load custom modules, and even talk to the Windows API. While that flexibility is great for system administrators, it is not always safe to expose it in shared or restricted environments. This is where Constrained Language Model comes in. It is a built-in security feature in PowerShell that limits what the language can do. When PowerShell runs in Constrained Language Model, many advanced features are blocked. This includes access to the .NET Framework, COM objects, adding types, defining classes, and other things that could be used to bypass restrictions or run unsafe code. Importance of Constrained Language Model Imagine a helpdesk user is allowed to restart a service using a constrained endpoint. Without extra protection, that user might still try to load a .NET class or use reflection to access hidden parts of the system, even if those commands were not part of the allowed cmdlets list. Constrained Language Model stops this kind of behavior. It locks down the scripting environment so that only basic PowerShell usage is allowed, mostly core cmdlets, standard variables, and simple logic. This is especially useful when giving remote access to users, external teams, or Automation accounts. Even if someone tries to get creative or run something unexpected, PowerShell will block it at the language level. Blocked code in Constrained Language Model When running in this mode, PowerShell blocks or limits the following: Calling .NET methods (like [System.IO.File]::Delete() ) Creating new types or classes Using Add-Type Accessing COM objects Loading external libraries Script blocks that use complex expressions Instead, only basic scripting is allowed, things like Get-Service , Restart-Computer , and simple loops or if statements. To check what language mode is currently active, use the following command: $ExecutionContext.SessionState.LanguageMode If it returns FullLanguage , then everything is allowed. If it returns ConstrainedLanguage , then the session is running in restricted mode. In a custom PowerShell session (like a constrained endpoint or JEA session), this mode is usually already set to ConstrainedLanguage automatically, especially when using the session type RestrictedRemoteServer . Activation of Constrained Language Model PowerShell enters Constrained Language Model in a few different ways: When AppLocker or Device Guard is enabled on the system. When running inside a JEA session. When using the NoLanguage or RestrictedLanguage options in a session configuration. When certain remoting rules are applied. It is not something that needs to be configured directly in most cases. Instead, it is applied automatically based on the session configuration or system policy. Combining with constrained endpoints Constrained Language Model is often used together with a constrained endpoint. While the endpoint limits what cmdlets and modules are available, the language mode makes sure that advanced scripting features cannot be used to break out of those limits. It is like locking the toolbox (endpoint), and then putting rubber covers over the sharp tools inside (language mode), just in case someone tries to take them apart. This extra layer makes it much harder for anyone to misuse the session, even if they know PowerShell very well. Now we will look at JEA, a Microsoft-supported framework that builds on everything we have just learned and helps organize it in a clean and reusable way. Just Enough Administration After learning about PowerShell Remoting, constrained endpoints, and Constrained Language Model, it is time to bring everything together into a more structured solution. This is where JEA comes in. JEA is a security feature in PowerShell that allows systems to be managed by users who have only the minimum permissions needed to do their tasks. It builds on top of constrained endpoints, but adds a clean framework for defining roles, allowed commands, and who can connect, all using configuration files. With JEA, there is no need to manually build and maintain custom session configurations from scratch. Microsoft provides a way to organize access using role capability files and session configuration files, which are easy to read and reuse. Idea behind JEA The main idea behind JEA is very simple. Most users do not need full admin rights. They just need to perform a few specific tasks. However, in most systems, access is all or nothing: either a user is an admin, or they are not. JEA solves this by allowing precise control over what each user or group is allowed to do. Instead of giving someone full remote PowerShell access, JEA lets them connect only to a special session, where only approved commands are available. This is useful for many scenarios, such as the following: Helpdesk teams restarting services or resetting passwords. Compliance staff running read-only reports. Backup teams starting or stopping jobs. Developers restarting test environments. It is not just for people; JEA works great with Automation accounts, gMSAs, and scheduled tasks that need to perform system actions without full admin permissions. Components of a JEA configuration A full JEA setup is made from two key configuration files. These files work together to define who can connect and what actions are allowed once connected. This separation of responsibilities makes the setup more flexible and easier to manage. Role capability file This file describes what the user is allowed to do inside the JEA session. It defines which cmdlets, functions, scripts, parameters, or external commands are permitted. This is the actual role part of the setup. For example, a role file might allow only the commands Get-Service , Restart-Service , and one custom script in a shared tools folder. Role capability files are stored under the Modules folder, usually in C:\Program Files\WindowsPowerShell\Modules\YourModuleName\RoleCapabilities\ . This means each JEA role must be part of a PowerShell module, even if it is a simple one created just for this purpose. Session configuration file This file defines who can connect to the JEA session and which role or roles they receive when they connect. It also controls other settings, like language mode, module imports, and transcript logging. The session configuration brings everything together. It connects users or groups to the defined role capabilities and creates an endpoint that can be registered on the system. Creating a role capability file This file always ends with .psrc and must be placed inside a module folder. That is because JEA works by loading a PowerShell module and looking for role definitions inside it. Even if the module is empty and used only for JEA, this structure must be followed. As a first step, we create a custom Modules folder, and in it, the RoleCapabilities folder: New-Item -Type Directory -Path "C:\Program Files\WindowsPowerShell\Modules\ServiceDeskTools\RoleCapabilities\" This is required for JEA to find later the role file ServiceDeskRole.psrc . The second step consists of generating the role file. To create the role file, we use the New-PSRoleCapabilityFile cmdlet. This creates a template file that contains all the available settings, most of them commented out at first. Refer to the following command: New-PSRoleCapabilityFile -Path "C:\Program Files\WindowsPowerShell\Modules\ServiceDeskTools\RoleCapabilities\ServiceDeskRole.psrc" The following figure shows the PowerShell RoleCapabilities file: Figure 14.13 : RoleCapabilities file This file can now be filled in to define the allowed commands. We will open the .psrc file in any text editor (like Visual Studio Code ( VS Code ) or PowerShell ISE). Then fill in the parts that describe what is allowed in the session. In the following example, we will allow only two cmdlets and one external command: VisibleCmdlets = @( 'Get-Service' , 'Restart-Service' ) VisibleExternalCommands = @( 'C:\Tools\RestartPrinterService.bat' ) It is also possible to allow only specific parameters for a cmdlet, shown as follows: VisibleCmdlets = @( @{ Name = 'Restart-Service' ; Parameters = @{ Name = 'Name' } }, 'Get-Service' ) This would allow the Restart-Service command only if the user provides the -Name parameter. There are more options in the role file to consider, such as: VisibleFunctions : To allow only custom functions. VisibleAliases : To allow specific aliases like gsv for Get-Service . ScriptsToProcess : To run specific scripts when the session starts. Environment variables : To define fixed environment variables inside the session. ModulesToImport : To preload specific PowerShell modules. Each of these options lets the session be customized in a safe way. Use case Let us say the goal is to allow service desk users to only restart the spooler service. This can be controlled by defining exactly which cmdlet is allowed, which parameter can be used, and which value is accepted for that parameter. Refer to the following code: VisibleCmdlets = @( @{ Name = 'Restart-Service' Parameters = @{ Name = 'Name' ValidateSet = @( 'Spooler' ) } }, 'Get-Service' ) In the example, the Restart-Service cmdlet is allowed, but only under very specific conditions. The only permitted parameter is -Name , meaning no one can use -InputObject or other options like -DisplayName . On top of that, the ValidateSet limits the value of -Name to just one choice, which is the spooler. This means the user is only allowed to restart the print service. If they try to restart any other service, like winrm or wuauserv , the command will fail with a validation error. This setup creates a very strict session, which is exactly the goal, as it prevents mistakes and blocks everything outside the intended task. Even if someone knows how PowerShell works or tries to explore other options, they are fully restricted. Creating a session configuration file After the role capability file is ready and defines what the user is allowed to do, the next step is to create a session configuration file, also called a .pssc file. This file connects everything together. It defines the following: Which users or groups are allowed to connect. The roles they get when they connect The PowerShell settings used inside the session. Which modules are loaded. Things like transcripts or language restrictions are enabled. Think of this file as the main control panel for the JEA endpoint. Use the following command to create a new session configuration file: New-PSSessionConfigurationFile -Path 'C:\JEA\ServiceDeskSession.pssc' This will generate a template file that contains many available options, most of which are commented out. This file can be opened again in a text editor, shown as follows: Figure 14.14 : Session configuration file Define the role assignments In this file, the most important part is the RoleDefinitions section. This links users or groups to specific JEA roles. Each role points to a .psrc file inside a module. Refer to the following code: RoleDefinitions = @{ 'DEMO\ServiceDesk' = @{ RoleCapabilities = 'ServiceDeskRole' } } Any user in the DEMO\ServiceDesk group is allowed to connect to this session. When they connect, they will receive the ServiceDeskRole from the module. That role controls which cmdlets, parameters, and values they can use. Multiple groups or users can be listed here, and each one can be assigned one or more roles. Setting additional options There are other useful options inside the .pssc file that can help lock down the session even further, such as: SessionType = 'RestrictedRemoteServer' : This disables things like the file system, custom functions, and advanced language features, and enables a more secure default session. We will enable this in the .prsc file. RunAsVirtualAccount = $true : This runs all commands in the session under a temporary admin account (a virtual account). The connecting user never gets actual admin rights or direct access. We will enable this in the .psrc file. TranscriptDirectory : This tells PowerShell to save a full log of every session, including every command the user runs. LanguageMode = 'ConstrainedLanguage' : This enables Constrained Language Model, which blocks .NET, COM, and advanced scripting. It adds another layer of protection. Register the JEA endpoint Once the .pssc file is ready, the final step is to register it as a usable remoting session through the following command: Register-PSSessionConfiguration -Name ServiceDeskSession -Path 'C:\JEA\ServiceDeskSession.pssc' We can check with Get-PSSessionConfiguration if the session is ready. The following figure shows the result: Figure 14.15 : Checking if the session is ready The preceding figure shows that the JEA ServiceDesk endpoint is active and ready. Users in the DEMO\ServiceDesk group can connect to it with the following command: Enter-PSSession -ComputerName DC01 -ConfigurationName ServiceDeskSession Before entering with the right user, we will try to enter with a user who has domain admin rights. The following figure shows that we do not have access to the session configuration even if we are a domain administrator: Figure 14.16 : Connection error as the domain admin Now, we will use the user max.mustermann , who is a member of the ServiceDesk group. Figure 14.17 shows that we have successfully restarted the spooler service. We have also tried to restart the winrm service, but without success. That is the behavior we expected: Figure 14.17 : Connecting with user max.mustermann Constrained endpoints vs. JEA A constrained endpoint is like a custom-built lock, strong and effective, but every part must be created and configured by hand. It works well, but managing it can get complicated, especially when different users need different permissions. JEA, on the other hand, is like a security system with smart cards. Roles can be created once and then reused for different users or groups. Each person gets only the access they need, and nothing more. It is much easier to set up, manage, and scale across multiple systems. Let us say there are two people in the IT team: Ena needs to restart the spooler service. Katharina needs to restart both the spooler and W32Time. With a constrained endpoint, two separate session configurations must be created, one for Ena and one for Katharina, each with different allowed commands. This means two files, two endpoints, and more work to maintain. With JEA, one role can be created for each task, for example: A role called SpoolerRole that allows restarting the spooler. A role called TimeAndSpoolerRole that allows restarting both services. Then, the session configuration assigns these roles to the right people. If a third person joins and needs the same access as Ena, that person can simply be added to the same role, and there is no need to rewrite anything. In practice, roles are especially useful for teams such as the service desk or support administrators, because they can be applied in a flexible and controlled way. This makes JEA much easier to manage over time, especially in environments where access needs to be tightly controlled but flexible. That is why JEA is the modern and recommended way to delegate PowerShell access securely and cleanly. Conclusion In this chapter, the focus was on how to manage remote systems securely using PowerShell. It started with setting up PowerShell Remoting, then explained how to connect to remote machines and send commands, either one at a time or to many systems at once. From there, the chapter moved into more advanced and safer ways to control access using constrained endpoints and JEA. With JEA, it is possible to give users just the access they need, and nothing more. Whether it is a helpdesk team restarting a service or an automation task running under a service account, JEA makes sure that commands are limited, sessions are secure, and mistakes or misuse are blocked before they can cause harm. This kind of control is important in any environment where more than one person or system touches production servers. It also fits well into automation setups, where accounts must do their job without getting full admin rights. This chapter helped build a better understanding of how to use PowerShell remoting in a safer and more structured way. The next chapter will investigate how modern AI tools can be used to speed up scripting, help solve problems, and support PowerShell work in real time. From GitHub Copilot to intelligent code suggestions and even chat-based tools, the chapter will show how to bring the power of AI into the daily workflow of scripting and automation.

PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 15 AI-powered PowerShell Introduction PowerShell is already a powerful tool for automating tasks and managing systems. However, now, with the help of artificial intelligence ( AI ) tools like GitHub Copilot and others, scripting has become even faster and smarter. These tools can suggest code while typing, help fix errors, and even explain what a command does. It is like having a helpful assistant built-into the script editor. This chapter shows how AI tools can be used to make PowerShell work more easily. From writing scripts and generating templates to solving tricky problems, AI can assist at every step. Even if there is no idea how to start a script, these tools can give useful suggestions or examples to get started. It is not about replacing skills; it is about speeding up the process and removing the boring parts. It is also important to understand that AI is not perfect, and it can make mistakes, suggest the wrong command, or offer outdated examples. That is why every suggestion needs to be tested and checked. AI tools are helpers, not decision makers. Always stay in control, review the code, and never run anything without understanding what it does. Most of the chapter will focus on GitHub Copilot, but other AI-based helpers will also be mentioned. Everything is done using Visual Studio Code ( VS Code ), since it is the most popular editor for PowerShell. The goal is to build scripts faster, with fewer errors, and more time left to focus on the important parts of automation. Structure This chapter will cover the following topics: Working of GitHub Copilot Using Copilot with PowerShell Fixing and understanding errors with AI Tips to get better suggestions from AI Use cases where AI saves time in production Staying in control and avoiding mistakes Building a PowerShell tool with AI help Objectives This chapter will show how AI tools, especially GitHub Copilot, can support PowerShell scripting in practical and helpful ways. It will explain how Copilot works in the background, how it interprets comments and function names, and how it can be used step-by-step while writing scripts. The goal is to make scripting faster, easier, and more productive, while maintaining full control over the code. Before going deeper, it is important to understand a few AI limitations. Tools like Copilot or ChatGPT can sometimes generate incorrect results, known as hallucinations, where the tool invents commands or parameters that do not exist. They also have context limits, meaning the AI can forget earlier parts of a script or lose track of complex logic. Due to this, every suggestion must be reviewed carefully, and nothing should be trusted blindly. The chapter will also demonstrate how to use AI to build functions, create script templates, and assist with logic. It will explain how to guide Copilot with clear comments and how to deal with errors or unusual suggestions. A comparison between GitHub Copilot, ChatGPT, and other AI tools will highlight where each tool is most effective. Examples will show how AI can save time in daily tasks, especially in automation and production work. The focus will also be on safety. The chapter will explain what to allow, what to avoid, and how to recognize mistakes in AI-generated code. By the end, a complete script will be created with AI support, and best practices for safe, effective use will be clear. Working of GitHub Copilot GitHub Copilot is an AI-powered coding assistant that helps write scripts and code directly inside VS Code. It works in the background and watches what is being typed, then suggests the next lines of code. Copilot is trained on millions of public code examples and knows many programming and scripting languages, including PowerShell. It does not just suggest random code. Copilot looks at the current file, the function name, and even comments to guess what is needed next. The clearer the code or comment is, the better the suggestions. It can help write full functions, suggest parameter names, fix common mistakes, and sometimes even explain what a piece of code is doing. We already integrated GitHub Copilot into VS Code back in Chapter 2 , Enhancing Scripting with Visual Studio Code, so everything is ready to be used. Copilot is not perfect. It does not always give the correct answer, and sometimes the code may not even run. It is also important to know that Copilot uses OpenAI models in the background, and some companies may have restrictions or policies when these tools are used at work. However, it is a good starting point, especially when stuck or looking for a faster way to write standard tasks. The real power of Copilot comes when it is used together with knowledge and experience. It does not replace the script author; instead, it helps fill in the blanks, saves time, and speeds up scripting. Using Copilot with PowerShell Once GitHub Copilot is active in VS Code, using it with PowerShell is very simple. It starts giving suggestions while writing code, and no extra setup is needed. As soon as a script file is opened with the .ps1 extension, Copilot starts watching and learning from what is being typed. It is important to be careful when working on scripts that have production access, because Copilot can sometimes suggest code that is incorrect, unsafe, or not intended for critical environments. AI suggestions should always be reviewed, especially when they affect real systems, permissions, or automation tasks in production. Start writing a function or a comment We will begin by writing a clear function comment, like #Get all running services , as shown in the following figure: Figure 15.1 : Command generated by Copilot As soon as the preceding line is written, Copilot may suggest the full command. This suggestion appears in light gray text. If it looks good, press Tab to accept it. If not, just keep typing to ignore it. Letting Copilot complete the code Copilot keeps watching every line. If a parameter or variable is typed, it might offer the rest, as shown in Figure 15.2 , where it offers a full function with return values. Now you can decide to use it or not; you can even modify it based on your needs: Figure 15.2 : Copilot completing the code started by the user Even if the code that Copilot returns is not perfect, it often saves time by writing the base structure. Asking Copilot for a specific task Copilot works best when it is given clear instructions. This can be done by writing a short comment in plain English that describes the task, shown as follows: 1. # Create a new user and add to the ServiceDesk Group In the following table, you can see some examples of good and bad prompts: Bad prompt Good prompt Create a script Write a PowerShell function that creates a new AD user and adds them to the ServiceDesk group. Fix this Fix the error in this function. The variable $User is empty and should come from a parameter. Make it better Improve this script by adding error handling and basic logging. Write code to delete all users Write a safe test function that disables a single lab user. Do not delete anything. Table 15.1 : Copilot prompts Bad prompts are unclear, too short, or too open. They do not give Copilot enough information about what you want, so the suggestions can be wrong, unsafe, or completely unrelated. Good prompts are clear and specific. They tell Copilot what the script should do, what language is used, and what the limits are. This helps the AI understand the task and produce safer and more useful suggestions. Right after the comment/prompt is typed, Copilot will try to suggest the full PowerShell code for the task. For example, if the comment is # Create a new user and add to a group , Copilot might suggest using New-LocalUser and Add-LocalGroupMember . This works if the goal is to manage local users, but in this case, the goal was to create a user in Active Directory and not on the local machine. Copilot did not understand our comment right away because it was too general. In the following figure, you can see that the code given works, but it is not up to our expectations: Figure 15.3 : Copilot provides code for a specific task This shows how important it is to describe tasks in more detail. If the comment had been written like # Create a new Active Directory user and add to an AD group , Copilot would likely have used New-ADUser and Add-ADGroupMember instead. The more clearly the task is described, the better the suggestion will match the actual goal. Even for more complex tasks like creating a scheduled task or reading a CSV file, Copilot can often generate a working block of code from just one or two lines of comments as long as the description is clear. This saves a lot of time, especially when starting something new or when a quick draft is needed. However, the suggestions always depend on the quality of the prompt. Be specific, and Copilot will respond much better. Asking AI to generate functions, templates, and logic One of the most powerful things about using GitHub Copilot is that it can help write complete PowerShell functions, script templates, and even solve logic problems. We will start by typing the following comment: 1. # Script to list all users in an OU and export them to CSV The comment may trigger a full layout with Get-ADUser , filtering options, and export commands using Export-Csv . It is not always 100 percent correct, but it usually gives enough structure to move forward without needing to look everything up manually. The following figure shows the generated code: Figure 15.4 : Copilot provides a full layout Even for loops, if statements, or error handling, Copilot can suggest working logic. It understands typical script flows like foreach , try and catch , or checking for $null values. Instead of starting from scratch, it becomes possible to let Copilot give the first version, then focus on adjusting the parts that matter. The more descriptive the comment, the better the function or logic will be. Copilot is not magic, but it is smart enough to follow clear instructions and fill in the technical part. It is also important to know that Copilot may suggest outdated or deprecated commands, and it does not always follow correct casing or indentation. Due to this, a final review of the script is always necessary, especially when the code needs to be clean, modern, and safe to use. Fixing and understanding errors with AI While GitHub Copilot is very useful for writing code and suggesting common patterns, it often struggles when the script contains an error that is not simple or common. For things like fixing logic mistakes, syntax issues, or understanding why something fails, chat-based tools like ChatGPT or Copilot Chat usually give better help. Copilot works in the background. It tries to complete or fix code based on patterns it has seen before. However, it does not know the error that just happened, it does not read the error message, or understand the script's context deeply. It may fix a missing bracket or suggest a default example, but if the logic is wrong or unclear, Copilot often cannot help. On the other hand, Copilot Chat works more like a real conversation. It can take the full code, the exact error message, and give a clear explanation of what went wrong and how to fix it. It can even offer multiple ways to solve the issue, depending on what the script is trying to do. Now, let us compare Copilot and ChatGPT. We have the following function that has a syntax error: function Stop-Spooler { if (Get-Service -Name Spooler).Status -eq 'Running' { Stop-Service -Name Spooler } } We will ask the Copilot Chat to fix this and check what it suggests. We have the wrong function in our script and copied it into the chat. Also, we will give the information that the function throws an error. The following figure shows the result of the chat: Figure 15.5 : Copilot Chat analyzes the error We can see that the chat function has improved the script, but has not really fixed it. It also updated the script in the script pane in VS Code. The green background in the following figure is the updated script: Figure 15.6 : Copilot updates the script in VS Code While Copilot is great at writing standard code and filling in small pieces, finding errors is not one of its strong points. It does not read error messages and does not explain what went wrong. Even simple mistakes like missing brackets, wrong logic, or a small typo often get missed by Copilot. Now, let us try something different and ask ChatGPT instead. We will copy only the wrong function and paste it into the chat, without giving it extra information. The following figure shows its reply: Figure 15.7 : ChatGPT analyzes the error Even though ChatGPT did not say the function was broken, it still returned a fixed and improved version. It wrapped the command inside a try and catch block, added error handling with -ErrorAction Stop , and included helpful messages using Write-Host and Write-Warning . It even used -Force on the Stop-Service command to make sure the service can be stopped. The most important part is that ChatGPT also explains what was changed and why. It gives short bullet points that describe each improvement in simple terms. This makes it much easier to learn and understand what is going on. So even though it did not directly say, your if condition is wrong , it still fixed the logic and gave back a clean and working version of the function. That is something GitHub Copilot did not do. This is where chat-based AI really shines. It is a perfect example of why tools like ChatGPT are better for understanding and correcting code, while Copilot is better for suggesting new code in the editor. Both are useful, but for different jobs. Comparing Copilot with ChatGPT and other tools Now, that GitHub Copilot and ChatGPT have been tested in action, it becomes clear that they are useful for different things. Both tools use AI to work with code, but the way they help is not the same. Copilot works directly inside VS Code. It suggests code as it is being typed and tries to guess what comes next based on the current script. It is fast and helpful when writing functions, building templates, or repeating common patterns. It is always there in the background, so it feels like a second brain that finishes code quickly, but Copilot does not understand problems fully, nor does it give detailed answers. It cannot read error messages, and it does not really know what the script is supposed to do unless the comment is very clear. ChatGPT, on the other hand, works like a conversation. It is better for solving problems, explaining how things work, and fixing code that does not run. It takes the full script, the goal, and even the error message, then gives back an explanation or a better version. It is more helpful when something is broken or unclear, and the problem needs to be understood step-by-step. There are also other tools like Bing Chat , Google Gemini , but for PowerShell, Copilot, and ChatGPT are the ones that work best in practice. Both tools can speed up scripting, but only when used in the right way. Tips to get better suggestions from AI To get the most out of AI tools like GitHub Copilot and ChatGPT, it helps to know a few simple tricks. These tools work best when they are guided clearly, and small changes in how something is written can make a big difference in the suggestions that come back. Let us look at the best practices we can use: Use clear and detailed comments : The most useful way to guide Copilot is with a comment that explains what the script should do in detail. Write in steps, not everything at once : AI works better when things are built line-by-line. Start with a comment, then type a header, then let Copilot fill in the next block. If the code is written too fast, Copilot often stops suggesting, so writing slower can actually help. Correct bad suggestions by rewording : If Copilot keeps suggesting the wrong thing, do not fight it. Just reword the comment or try a more detailed version. For ChatGPT, it helps to include a sentence like, this is for PowerShell 7 , or this should work on domain-joined machines . Use examples instead of questions : When asking ChatGPT, it often helps to say, here is what I have. I want to change this part instead of something like how do I manage users? Keep trying small edits : AI tools improve with better input. If the first suggestion is wrong, try again with slightly different wording. One or two words can change everything. These small habits make working with AI much smoother. They reduce bad suggestions and help get results that are closer to the goal, especially when writing automation scripts in PowerShell. Note: Copilot can also be fine-tuned with organization wide settings. This allows companies to control how Copilot behaves, which rules it should follow, and which types of suggestions are allowed. It gives teams more control and makes Copilot safer and more consistent in a shared environment. Use cases where AI saves time in production Using AI tools like GitHub Copilot and ChatGPT in daily scripting tasks can save a lot of time, especially in busy environments where there is not always time to look up documentation or test many different solutions. However, no matter how helpful these tools are, the knowledge of people is always needed. AI can suggest code, but only a human expert can tell if it is correct and safe to use. So, every result must always be validated and reviewed before it is used in production. Let us look at some use cases: Generating script templates : When starting a new script, AI can help build the structure right away. Instead of setting up all the param blocks, try and catch, logging, and comments manually, Copilot can fill that in based on just a comment. However, it is up to the script author to check if all important parts are there, like error handling or parameter checks, and adjust anything that is missing or wrong. Repeating common patterns : In daily work, many scripts follow the same patterns, like checking if a user exists, testing a network connection, or exporting data to CSV. These patterns can be reused quickly with AI. Still, every suggestion should be looked at with care, because the logic or values might not fit the current system or the actual goal. Quick fixes in urgent situations : In real production environments, sometimes a quick fix is needed. A function breaks, a report fails, or a process crashes. In these cases, ChatGPT can help understand the issue, but no AI suggestion should ever be copied and pasted without testing it first; a wrong fix can make things worse. The final judgment must always come from the person who understands the system. Training new team members : AI can also help people who are new to PowerShell or scripting. They can use Copilot or ChatGPT to get started, test ideas, or understand code. However, it is important that they also learn how to think about security, performance, and best practices, things that AI tools do not always get right. That learning still comes from real experience and guidance. Writing documentation and comments : Even writing comments or simple documentation can be faster with Copilot or ChatGPT. They can suggest summaries or usage examples. Still, those examples should always be double-checked to make sure they are true and match what the script does. These tools can really save time, but they do not replace the need for good knowledge. The best results come when AI and real experience work together. Think of the AI as a fast helper, but the expert is still in charge. Staying in control and avoiding mistakes AI tools can be very helpful when writing or fixing scripts, but they can also lead to problems if they are trusted too much. That is why it is important to always stay in control. No matter how good a suggestion looks, it must always be reviewed and tested before using it, especially in production environments. One of the most common mistakes is running code without understanding what it does. AI might generate a working script, but it could use unsafe commands, skip error handling, or include logic that does not fit the real goal. Always read every line before running it. If something does not make sense, it should not be used. Another common mistake is trusting AI with sensitive tasks or sensitive data. For example, if a script deletes user accounts, changes permissions, or accesses important systems, these tasks must be handled carefully. Even if Copilot or ChatGPT gives a result that looks good, the code should always be double-checked. A wrong command in these cases can cause real damage. Be aware that any data entered into AI tools can be stored or logged. Always be careful and avoid sharing sensitive information. Also, and this is very important, never paste corporate or private data into a chat tool like ChatGPT. This includes usernames, passwords, internal IPs, customer information, or anything else that is private. These tools are not made for handling confidential content. Only use example data when asking for help, and keep real company information safe. It is also easy to forget that AI does not know the environment. It does not know what modules are installed, what group policies are active, or what systems are connected. So even if a script looks correct, it might not work without changes. The best way to stay in control is to treat every AI suggestion as a starting point, not the final version. Use it to get ideas, then make it fit the real system. Test it. Clean it up. Make sure it is safe and does exactly what is needed. Building a PowerShell tool with AI help Now, it is time to bring everything together by building a real PowerShell script with the help of AI. This will be a complete tool that solves a real task, shows how Copilot supports the process, and where ChatGPT helps when things get unclear. The tool will do the following: Check disk space on a remote server. Send a warning email if free space is below 10%. Log the result to a local file. This is a very typical task for system admins, and AI can help at each step, from generating the function to building the logic and handling edge cases. Follow the given steps: First, we will start with a clear comment. # Function to check disk space on remote computer and send email alert if free space is low Let Copilot suggest the first draft. In the following figure, we can see what Copilot has generated for us: Figure 15.8 : Copilot generates a script This is a good starting point, but not perfect. It is missing error handling, formatting, and log writing. Now we will ask ChatGPT to improve the code: Improve this PowerShell function. Add logging, a threshold parameter, and format the free space in GB. The newly generated function is shown in the following figure. This version now includes a parameter for the threshold, logging, and better formatting. Figure 15.9 : Improved function with a parameter for the threshold, logging, and better formatting Now, the script should be carefully reviewed and tested. Every line should be checked to make sure the logic fits the goal and nothing important is missing. For example, confirm that the email settings are correct and working, that the log file path exists or is created if missing, and that error handling is in place so the script does not break if something fails. It is also important to test the script on different servers, since not every system behaves the same. If any part looks unclear or does not work as expected, ChatGPT can still be used to help improve or fix those parts. Even with AI support, the final review and testing must always be done manually by an expert. Note: The Send-MailMessage cmdlet in our script is deprecated, and the AI did not warn us about it. This is a good reminder that AI suggestions must always be reviewed carefully, because outdated commands or unsafe practices can slip in without notice. Best practices for using AI in scripting Refer to the following table to understand the best practices: Guideline Explanation Review all generated code. AI can miss errors, use deprecated commands, or create unsafe logic. Always check the result. Give clear and specific prompts. Good prompts produce better suggestions. Vague prompts lead to bad or unsafe code. Avoid sharing sensitive data. Anything typed may be logged or stored. Never enter passwords, real tokens, or production details. Use AI as support, not as a replacement. AI helps with speed, but code review and peer approval are still required. Test everything in a safe environment. AI output can differ on different machines, models, or contexts, so testing is essential. Be careful with production scripts. AI might suggest risky changes or outdated commands. Double-check before deploying. Table 15.2 : Best practices Conclusion In this chapter, we explored how AI tools like GitHub Copilot and ChatGPT can make PowerShell scripting much faster, easier, and enjoyable. Instead of starting every script from zero, Copilot can suggest helpful code blocks, build templates, and write functions based on a comment or function name. It works in the background inside VS Code. ChatGPT, on the other hand, is better when something is unclear or not working. It can explain errors, improve logic, and help fix broken scripts step-by-step. We also learned that these tools are not perfect. They can make mistakes, suggest wrong commands, or offer outdated examples. That is why it is essential to check the results, test the code, and ensure it works in your environment. AI is a great helper, but it still needs human experience and knowledge to do things safely and correctly. We also covered the risks of using AI, especially when working with sensitive data or high-risk tasks. Never share private or company information in chat tools. Always stay in control of the script, and use AI only to support the process, not replace decision-making. With all of this in mind, AI can become a powerful part of the daily scripting workflow. It helps reduce repetitive work, gives fresh ideas, and speeds up writing. However, it always needs a final check from someone who understands the goal and the system. In the next chapter, we will look at advanced debugging and error handling in PowerShell. This is an important skill, especially for larger scripts and production environments. The next chapter will show how to find and fix bugs, how to handle terminating and non-terminating errors, and how to write clear and helpful error messages. This will make scripts more stable, easier to troubleshoot, and much more professional. PowerShell Automation and Scripting C HAPTER 16 Advanced Debugging and Error Handling Introduction In this chapter, we will explore error handling and debugging. When PowerShell scripts get bigger, it is not always easy to find out why something is not working. One small mistake can break the whole script, so you need to understand how to catch errors and check what is going on inside the script. We will start by looking at how PowerShell shows errors. Some errors stop the script completely, while others show a warning and keep going. You will learn how to catch these errors and how to work with the error details. You will also see how to write your own error messages that really say what went wrong. After that, we look at debugging, which means checking the script while it is running. You can stop the script at any point, look at the values of your variables, and go step-by-step to find the problem. Visual Studio Code ( VS Code ) has great tools for this. It makes finding problems much easier and faster. This chapter helps you write scripts that are more stable, easier to test, and easier to fix when something goes wrong. Structure This chapter covers the following topics: Ways PowerShell handles errors Common error handling practices Catching errors with try, catch, and finally Throwing errors using throw Writing errors using .NET exceptions Handling errors in loops $? and $LASTEXITCODE for native commands Debugging with PowerShell Objectives This chapter will help the readers to understand how to deal with errors in PowerShell and how to debug their scripts. They will first learn how PowerShell handles errors. There are different types of errors, and each one behaves in a different way. They will also learn how to work with $Error , ErrorVariable , and ErrorRecord . These tools help them understand what went wrong. Readers will also learn how to use try, catch, and finally to catch and handle errors in a better way. We will also show how to write your own error messages, so that the readers know exactly where the problem is. In the following sections, we move to debugging, which means running the script step-by-step to see what it is doing. Readers will learn how to use breakpoints, how to stop the script, and how to check the values of their variables while the script is paused. This helps them to find and fix problems much faster. After this chapter, the readers will be able to build scripts that are easier to understand when something goes wrong. Ways PowerShell handles errors Before we can catch or fix errors, we need to understand how PowerShell handles them. Not every error is the same; some are very serious and stop the script right away, while others are not so serious, and they show a warning, but the script keeps going. PowerShell calls these two types terminating errors and non-terminating errors. A terminating error means the script or command stops. Nothing runs after that unless you catch the error with a try and catch block. For example, if you use a command that needs a file and the file is missing, PowerShell might stop the script. A non-terminating error is softer. It means something went wrong, but PowerShell will continue to the next step. For example, if you try to get a list of users and one of them is not found, PowerShell will show an error, but it will still process the rest. It is crucial to know which type of error you are dealing with, because you handle them differently. Terminating errors can be caught with try and catch .
